HEADLINE: TECHNOLOGY; 
In Korea, Broadband IsPart of the Culture

BYLINE:  By DON KIRK 

DATELINE: SEOUL, South Korea, Oct. 28 

 BODY:
Kim Sung Bae recalls with a rueful grin how hard it was to persuade people to pay an extra 40,000 Korean won a month, about $30, for the new high-speed Internet connections he was trying to sell two years ago.
 "I said, 'The speed is ultra-fast, you can enjoy data-surfing and stock transactions and bill paying,' but people didn't believe me," said Mr. Kim, a salesman for Hanaro Telecom, founded four years ago, just before a round of economic crisis. 
 The timing, though, was right. By late 1999, the nation was in a boom of sorts, and high-technology companies were leading the way. 
"There were so many Internet venture companies in Seoul and other big cities," Mr. Kim said, "and the demand for fast service with high quality was growing. Now everybody uses it."
Today Korea is suffering new economic problems that have people asking whether another crisis looms, but one thing is certain. The broadband service Mr. Kim and hundreds of others worked so hard to introduce -- a way to receive images and information at several times the speed possible with ordinary phone lines -- is now so embedded here that nothing is going to change except, perhaps, electronic communication getting even faster.
While broadband is available in all advanced countries, it has caught on here twice as fast as in Canada, which has the second-highest broadband penetration.
By the end of the year, said Lee Sang Chul, president of Korea Telecom, the telephone company, partly government-owned, that provides 49 percent of Korea's broadband service, "the number of actual users nationwide is projected at 23 million," or half the nation's population.
"In some apartment complexes," he added, in a speech earlier this month, "the residential penetration rate of broadband hovers over 75 percent."
Just why has broadband caught on here so much faster than in Japan, the United States and Western Europe?
The answer lies in part in efforts by the Korean government and in part in competition between Korea Telecom, founded more than a century ago, the upstart Hanaro and several even younger, brasher competitors.
The real answer, though, lies with a people who like nothing better than to communicate. "It's a culture thing," said Lee Jae Hong, a director in the information and communication ministry. "People loved it. Subscribers increased dramatically. Our domestic Internet content has lots of moving pictures and graphics. Everything needs to go 'pali, pali' -- quickly, quickly."
Young people crowding rooms full of computers, housewives in apartment blocks, executives and office workers hustling to ride the crest of the information revolution -- all wanted the same thing: quick communication, quick games, quick contact.
There was "growing demand, responsive supply and appropriate government policy," said Lee Sang Chul. "These three elements created synergism to prompt explosive growth."
The demand is such that smoke-filled "PC rooms," where young customers spend hours at a stretch, have largely replaced the relatively leisurely cybercafes. To chat with friends, play games and do school work on line, food and drinks are a distraction.
"Koreans are fond of community," said Lee Taek Kyu, the 34-year-old owner of Orange PC, a third-floor room in a small building in central Seoul, packed with about 50 computers, almost all of them occupied by youthful customers paying 1,500 won, or $1.15, an hour. "In Seoul, there are between 15,000 and 20,000 rooms like this," he said. "They all have broadband service."
Games are undoubtedly the biggest attaction for the 20-somethings in his PC room, and Diablo and Starcraft, both from the United States, and a Korean football game rank as favorites. "They go out, they eat some, they come back, and they're here for five hours, maybe longer," Mr. Lee said as an assistant tried to clear the air with a disinfectant spray.
The broadband craze is, if anything, more pronounced in the apartment buildings that form the skylines of Seoul and other cities. Virtually every new building is now built with fiber-optic wiring to each apartment, and in the last year or two this wiring has been installed in most older buildings.
The advent of broadband, though, was not cheap. The government estimates the cost of developing the technology, building the infrastructure and marketing it at $30 billion between 2000 and 2005.
Korea Telecom and Hanaro, which rely largely on telephone wires, and Thrunet, which uses cable modems, are counting on huge subscriber bases. By the end of the year, there will be eight million subscribers, 49 percent on Korea Telecom's Megapass service, 26 percent on Hanaro's HanaFOS, 17 percent wired to Thrunet and the rest divided among half a dozen small providers.
Government influence is pervasive. "Korea Telecom rose quickly because the government had some vision," said Oh Sung Jin, deputy director of the communication ministry's broadband section. "We're pushing carriers to push the service with low fees."
The theory is that all providers will make money, but now all are suffering.
Korea Telecom stock has fallen to 40,000 won today from 180,000 won two years ago, while the government has found no buyers for its 40 percent stake. Stock in Hanaro, formed at the behest of the communications ministry to compete with Korea Telecom as a telephone provider, has fallen to 2,500 won from 20,000 won a year ago.
Hardest hit is Thrunet, capitalized in 1999 with $150 million from Korea's Trident Group, a computer maker, and supplemented by $100 million from Softbank of Japan and $40 million from Microsoft. Not listed in Korea, the stock hit $90 on the Nasdaq two years ago but is now worth $1.35 a share. 
Government researchers, though, remain committed to low prices. The important thing is return on investment, said Yoon Byeong Nam, vice president of the National Computerization Agency, responsible for what is formally called the Korea Information Superhighway Project. "At the beginning, they estimated, if they got 2 million subscribers they would break even. Now K.T. has 3.5 million and Hanaro has 1.8 million and Thrunet around 1 million. At first, K.T. managers complained the price was too cheap, but they don't any more."
Broadband's spread, however, gives some Koreans pause. "Parents worry that their kids are so addicted, through games, chatting, whatever," said Jang Song Hyon, a businessman. "Young people chat on computers for hours."
Mr. Jang's wife refuses to use the Internet, but admits the addiction is catching. He and his two daughters all have their own computers, linked to separate broadband lines.
"I pay at least half my bills through the Internet," Mr. Jang said. "Also, I make reservations and buy tickets that way."
His elder daughter, Jin Hee, says she spends 10 hours a day online, at home and the office, using five e-mail addresses. "I guess my home is fairly typical," she said. "Koreans like to talk to each other."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST 

 BODY:
Qualcomm to Unveil System To Improve Aviation Safety
Qualcomm, a leading wireless technology company, intends to announce plans today that it hopes will breathe life into Globalstar Telecommunications, the money-losing satellite company in which it holds a minority stake.
 In response to the terrorist attacks on Sept. 11, Qualcomm says it has developed an airborne communications system that can be used to improve existing aviation safety services. The technology functions on Globalstar's 48-satellite system, which allows two-way communications between aircraft and a ground-based network. Page C2. 

Juggling Act for Pakistani Paper
As editor of The Daily Jang, a Pakistani newspaper published in Britain, Zahoor Niazi is no stranger to managing competing interests. But his juggling act is increasingly common among the Muslim and Arab press in Britain as they struggle to balance readers' skepticism of American and British military actions with Prime Minister Tony Blair's message of war on terror -- not on Islam. C11.

AOL Gains Cable Rights in China
In signing a deal with the Chinese government to broadcast a cable channel in southern China, AOL Time Warner became the first foreign broadcaster to win the right to distribute programming on cable television systems in the country. Its key to success seems to be avoiding sex, violence and news. C11.

Microsoft Relents on Blocking Site
When Microsoft introduced Windows XP, it also upgraded its flagship Web site, MSN.com, and prompted an outcry by preventing users with certain browsers from viewing the site. In those cases, the users saw error messages recommending that they upgrade their browsers, preferably to Microsoft's Internet Explorer. At first, Microsoft held its ground, but it later backed down under relentless criticism. C2.

Koreans Snap Up High-Speed Access
While high-speed Internet service is available in all advanced countries, it is catching on particularly fast in South Korea. C3.

Nestle Seeks Haagen-Dazs Rights
Nestle plans to buy the United States rights to the Haagen-Dazs ice cream brand from General Mills to better compete with Unilever, the world's largest ice cream maker. Nestle would not comment on how much it planned to spend, but analysts have estimated the value of such a deal at $600 million to $650 million. C6.

A Startling Book Jacket Online
An e-mail message circulating on the Internet last week gave recipients a jolt when they clicked on the Web link in the text of the message, which took them to a book for sale on Amazon.com's British site. The book jacket bore Arabic script, the face of a Saudi prince in traditional attire and the image of a plane flying through the top of a skyscraper. In the current climate, it was hard not to think of the terrorist attacks. But the book, whose title translates to English as "My story with the skyscraper Alwaleed Bin Talal," is about a building under construction in Riyadh, Saudi Arabia. C3.

Interest Surges in E-Mail Marketing
As the anthrax scare disrupts mail-handling procedures, companies that provide e-mail marketing and online billing services said that they had seen a surge of interest from prospective clients and from current customers looking to make even more use of online options. Bob Tedeschi: E-Commerce Report. C7.

Page by Page History of the Web
Brewster Kahle, a computer scientist, has collected more than 10 billion Web pages. Last week, he introduced the Wayback Machine, a free service that makes them available to anyone with Web access. New Economy. C3.

HEADLINE: A NATION CHALLENGED: MILAN; 
Italian Tapes Portray Young Arabs Operating on the Edges of Islamic Terror

BYLINE:  By JOHN TAGLIABUE 

DATELINE: MILAN, Oct. 26 

 BODY:
Sometimes, they could be overheard negotiating the price of false passports and identification cards. Five hundred dollars a set, customers complained, was simply more than an unemployed Algerian could afford. 
 At other times, they offered travel advice. The best route to training camps for Islamic fighters in Afghanistan, they counseled, was through Iran rather than Pakistan, which was too full of intelligence agents. They also talked about the risks of cellphones. It was important to change phones often or, better yet, use messengers. 
 These conversations, by phone or in person among men of Arab background living in several Western European cities, were intercepted by Italian officials during four months early this year by tapping phones and bugging an apartment near Malpensa airport north of this Italian financial and business center. 
For all their talk of terror and tactics, though, the men on the tapes were neither associates of Osama bin Laden nor members of his Al Qaeda terror network. Investigators say they have no evidence that these men were involved with the Sept. 11 hijackers or were part of recently disclosed plots to attack American Embassies in Paris and Rome. 
Instead, the men were basically Al Qaeda aspirants, young Arabs who provided logistical terror services to other groups of Muslim radicals in North Africa, the Middle East and elsewhere.
The transcripts of the tapes, along with other Italian court documents, provide an inside look at groups operating at the edges of Islamic terrorism in Europe. In fact, their very distance from the core underscores what terrorism experts have described as one of Al Qaeda's central organizational principles, one that helps make it such a maddening foe -- its extreme decentralization. 
Investigators here say it is possible that in a kind of barter arrangement, Mr. bin Laden put his camps at their disposal, in exchange for occasional services in Europe like providing false documents. But there is no evidence of this. 
Unlike the Hamburg-based hijackers, who were of middle-class background and well educated, the groups here are composed mostly of uneducated individuals of working-class origins, mainly Algerians and Tunisians between the ages of 20 and 30, investigators say.
An exception is one of the leaders of the Milan group, a 43-year-old Tunisian named Essid Sami Ben Khemais. The police in Spain suspect that he may have met there last April with two men believed to have met Mohamed Atta, a mastermind of the Sept. 11 hijackings, when he traveled to Spain in July.
To officials in several countries, the attacks in America were confirmation that some groups of Arab radicals in Europe were gradually moving from their purely service role to active terror. Evidence came, officials said, with the arrest by German police in Frankfurt last December, after a tip from French investigators, of four Arab men closely allied to the Milan group who were suspected of planning to blow up a Christmas market in the French city of Strasbourg.
The man suspected of being the principal organizer of the plot, an Algerian named Mohamed Bensakhria, managed to evade the raid and escape to Spain. But he was arrested there in June and extradited to France. "The real alarm was Christmas last year," a senior European investigator said this week. "Sept. 11 was the confirmation that somewhere a pot was boiling."
It was last winter, too, that investigators in Italy began tapping the phones of Islamic radicals in northern Italy, harvesting information that led them to the apartment in Gallarate where the conversations were bugged.
The four months of wiretaps and bugged conversations revealed an intense telephone traffic between the Milan group and groups in Munich, Frankfurt, Brussels and elsewhere. 
They also gave evidence of frequent travels by members of the groups. Mr. Ben Khemais, for instance, traveled to Spain, where he met with other radicals. In August, the German police clandestinely searched the apartment in Munich of Lased Ben Heni, a 31-year-old Libyan who claimed to have trained at a Qaeda camp in Afghanistan. The police discovered used train tickets to Palermo in Sicily. The purpose of the trip remains obscure.
But Mr. Ben Heni, in a bugged conversation with Mr. Ben Khemais and four other Arab men, discussed plans to pick up large quantities of false documents from a hiding place near Gallarate. Whether the passports were for Arabs seeking passage to Afghanistan is unclear, but he talks about the route to reach the terrorist camps there. "You have to go to any embassy in France or London and ask for a visa for Mecca, but through Iran," he said. 
"There is total collaboration with the Iranians," he went on. "Pakistan used to be the most comfortable route, but in recent years there are too many intelligence services." 
The phone intercepts also disclose contacts with London, notably to Abu Doha, the pseudonym of a 36-year-old Algerian named Rachid Kefflous who was arrested there in February and awaits extradition to the United States on terrorism charges.
The tale of the tapes is filled with the logistics of procuring and selling fake passports, identity cards, driver licenses and other documents. In one conversation, the men discuss putting poison gas in cans of tomatoes. In others, they discuss chemicals for making bombs. 
After the Frankfurt arrests, the men are distraught. Mr. Ben Khemais, in a phone conversation with Tarek Maaroufi, a Tunisian-born Belgian living in Brussels, laments, "I'm informing you that half of the group in Germany was taken." To Mr. Maaroufi's expression of disbelief, Mr. Ben Khemais responds, "Yes, did you understand me? They've arrested the brothers in Germany and found the cache of weapons in Germany, in Frankfurt."
Investigators describe Mr. Ben Heni as the most soldierlike and aggressive, and he appears to suffer most from the passivity. To let off steam, he laces even the most practical conversations with diatribes against the West. In one conversation with Mr. Ben Khemais and another Tunisian, he suddenly begins talking about Mr. bin Laden. "Believe me, the sheik is not just watching," he says. "He is planning something because in his wishes he has a goal and he wants to achieve it, just as he has achieved all his desires."
Some of Mr. Ben Heni's words may be mere boasting, investigators caution. In the same conversation, late in the evening of March 9, he talks at length about millions of dollars Mr. bin Laden supposedly earned by selling to China undetonated cruise missiles, fired by the Clinton administration into Afghanistan after the attacks on two American Embassies in East Africa in 1998. Similar claims were raised in 1998 and the Chinese firmly denied them. But Mr. Ben Heni's most outsized boast is that groups like those shattered by this year's arrests demonstrate that the radicals have Europe in their grip.
"God loves us, because while it was once our wish to arrive in Europe and then return home with money," he said that same evening, "God loves us because now Europe is in our hands, because God showed us the way and we understood and are mujahedeen in his name.
"We have to be like serpents," he went on. "We have to strike, and then hide."
Apparently, they did not hide well enough. On April 3, after intercepted conversations made it clear that Mr. Ben Khemais and others were aware of the surveillance and were planning to flee, the Italian police swooped in, arresting three people in Gallarate and another in Milan. On Oct. 10, remnants of the groups were rounded up in other cities.
Mr. Ben Heni was arrested in Germany. But several others known to the police, including a Tunisian and an Egyptian, remain at large, possibly in France.

HEADLINE: U.S. Is Taking a Back Seat In Latest Talks on Climate 

BYLINE:  By ANDREW C. REVKIN

 BODY:
With the United States in attendance but sitting on the sidelines, more than 150 countries begin 12 days of talks in Morocco today aimed at completing the rule book for the Kyoto Protocol, a treaty requiring cuts in gases linked to global warming. 
 The goal of many of the countries at the meeting, in Marrakesh, is to achieve enough consensus on details that the treaty can be ratified and enacted next year, the 10th anniversary of the Rio Earth Summit, where a previous, but voluntary, climate treaty was forged. 
 After that nonbinding agreement failed to achieve reductions in the gas emissions, countries began the protracted talks leading to the binding commitments outlined in the Kyoto accord in 1997.
But proponents of enacting the treaty are hampered by two things: persistent disagreements over how to measure gas reductions and levy penalties when a target is missed, and the rejection of the treaty by President Bush last March. 
Several countries, most notably Japan, have said it would be difficult for them to ratify the agreement if the United States, the largest producer of heat-trapping greenhouse gases, did not join the treaty. 
Japan plays a pivotal role. Under the complex arithmetic of the treaty, it takes effect only when it is ratified by countries accounting for 55 percent of gas that industrial countries emitted in 1990. Without the United States, almost any combination of nations can reach that level only if Japan goes along.
Public opinion in Japan is strongly in favor of the Kyoto treaty, which was drafted on Japanese soil in 1997. But industries there have intensively lobbied against it in recent weeks, Japanese officials said, partly fearing that they would incur costs not shared by American industries if the pollution rules took effect. 
"Our government thinks that it is extremely important to have one international rule and that every country should act," said a senior Japanese environmental official. "Participation by the U.S.A. is very, very important," he said. 
Japanese officials have met three times with the Bush administration since mid-September to discuss climate change, but not ways to make the treaty acceptable to the United States, officials from both governments said. 
Mr. Bush and many senators have said that the Kyoto Protocol would harm the economy and unfairly require only the industrial nations to cut emissions, while fast-growing developing countries like China face no such constraints.
Administration officials said on Friday that they were attending the meeting only to prevent measures from being adopted that might saddle the United States with indirect costs or create harmful precedents.
They said that Mr. Bush's rejection of the treaty was final, but that the administration still planned to pursue other approaches to the problem. 
"Our position is that climate change is an important issue and one which President Bush has stated we're seriously committed to addressing," Paula J. Dobriansky, the under secretary of state for global affairs, said in an interview.
There are plenty of other obstacles to enactment of the treaty, with or without the United States, and dozens of parties are affected by the deal. Each country faces a different set of consequences should the treaty take effect.
Disagreements over details of the treaty's rules were whittled down significantly at the last bargaining session, held in Bonn in July. But last week, news reports from Europe made it clear that substantial differences still divided important players.
Germany, with a government influenced by a strong Green Party, has said no new credit should be granted to countries with vast forests for the role trees play in absorbing carbon dioxide. Russia, in contrast, has pressed for more such credits as a prerequisite for its support. 
On Friday, without citing Russia, the German environment minister, Jurgen Trittin, told Agence France-Presse that "it is out of the question to renegotiate, upwards or downwards."
Some countries were still holding out hope that the United States would eventually rejoin the treaty. 
But experts involved in the negotiations said that attacks on Sept. 11, by greatly boosting the popularity of Mr. Bush, lessened the likelihood that there would be a shift in the American position any time soon.

HEADLINE: A NATION CHALLENGED: THE OIL SUPPLY; 
Topping Off Strategic Reserve Is Proposed

BYLINE:  By NEELA BANERJEE 

 BODY:
As nervousness rises about how tensions in the Middle East could disrupt oil supplies, President Bush is considering the unprecedented step of filling the Strategic Petroleum Reserve to full capacity, according to Congressional and White House aides and oil industry experts.
 The aides, who spoke on the condition of anonymity, said that President Bush was reviewing a plan that would call for oil to be acquired in any of three ways: Congress could appropriate money to buy more oil for the reserve; the government could take royalty payments from oil companies that drill in the Gulf of Mexico in the form of oil rather than cash; or the White House could combine both approaches. 
 The Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries, led by Saudi Arabia, a United States ally, has so far held to a pledge made just after the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks to keep the flow of oil stable. Few people think a rupture in supplies is likely, but as the American-led bombing of Afghanistan drags on, some in Congress and the administration worry that anger in the Middle East over the air raids raises the chance of terrorist attacks upon the region's oil infrastructure or pressure on local leaders to curtail exports. 
"None of us know if markets will be disrupted or not," said Lawrence J. Goldstein, president of the Petroleum Industry Research Foundation. "But the possibility of a supply disruption is higher today than it was yesterday."
The United States imports less oil from the Middle East than it did 30 years ago, and it would not be immediately paralyzed by oil shortages if supplies from the region fell considerably. But the economy and consumers would be hit with sharply higher prices as supplies dwindled. Despite the country's reduced reliance on oil from the Persian Gulf, it consumes and imports far more oil over all than it did a decade ago, exacerbating the economy's vulnerability to a precipitous supply drop.
Created in 1975, the Strategic Petroleum Reserve was designed to smooth out, in the short run, the price spikes and possible shortages that could be caused by a disruption in the oil supply. A network of underground caverns in Louisiana and Texas, the reserve can hold up to 700 million barrels of oil. The most it ever contained was about 590 million in the early 1990's, which at the time would have been enough for 82 days if there were no imports. The reserve has about 545 million barrels now, but that would last just 53 days, according to the Department of Energy.
The reserve is expected to add 48 million barrels of oil by 2003, much of it as the payment for a deal last year under which oil companies received crude oil from the government to be refined into heating fuel.
Industry experts and Congressional officials said they thought the administration would most likely opt for the so-called royalty in-kind payments of oil. The government receives payments from companies that lease federal lands for oil and gas drilling and can take those payments in cash or in oil. 
Now President Bush has been presented with a plan that calls for the reserve to receive 100,000 to 125,000 barrels a day for at least the next two years, industry experts and Congressional officials said. That would yield about 70 million barrels. Added to the 48 million barrels already expected, that would nearly bring the reserve to full capacity, said Jan Stuart, head of research for global energy futures at ABN Amro in New York.
The in-kind payments would also help to stabilize oil prices, Mr. Stuart and others said. That in turn would bring some relief to oil companies and to oil-producing countries, which are losing billions of dollars in revenue as oil prices lag. "It helps in putting a floor to oil prices, and that's what people have been looking for after oil prices tanked a few weeks ago," Mr. Stuart said.
But the question is whether building the reserve's inventory through the royalty in-kind approach would provide enough oil fast enough to avert an emergency. Mr. Goldstein contends that appropriating money to buy oil on the markets would pump up the oil cushion faster than receiving a bit of oil daily from the royalty program. Prices now, at about $22 a barrel, are lower than they have been in years. Yet at a time when so many constituencies are vying for a shrinking pot of federal money, there are no champions to argue for tucking oil away for an indefinite period.
"We need security in the markets now, not two or three years from now," Mr. Goldstein said. "Oil prices are low now, so we would be buying an insurance policy at a very fair price."

HEADLINE: A NATION CHALLENGED: THE ALLIANCE; 
Rebel Alliance Is Frustrated By U.S. Raids

BYLINE:  By DAVID ROHDE 

DATELINE: BAGRAM, Afghanistan, Oct. 28 

 BODY:
A week ago tonight, soldiers and commanders of the rebel Northern Alliance gleefully watched American bombs begin to fall on Taliban positions just north of Kabul.
 Seven days later, American jets have bombed the wrong side of the front line twice, including an errant bomb that killed one person and wounded eight, among them four children, in a campaign that has left the alliance baffled. 
 Northern Alliance forces have actually lost ground to the Taliban, and refugees from Kabul warn darkly that the bombing campaign could swing public opinion firmly into the camp of the Taliban.
With the onset of Ramadan, the Islamic holy month, possibly curtailing military operations in only two and a half weeks, hopes of a quick alliance breakthrough are dimming. Blame for the false start appears to lie with both sides. 
On Saturday, American planes carried out their heaviest raids yet here, winning the praise of alliance commanders who are expected to wage a ground offensive.
But one of the final bombs of the day demolished a civilian house about two miles inside Northern Alliance territory, killing a 25-year-old mother of two and wounding eight other civilians. Officials in the local hospital also reported treating three civilians injured in bombing on the Taliban side.
Today, American jets carried out no major attacks in the immediate area, though they did target another part of Afghanistan, near Tajikistan, striking an area said by the Northern Alliance to contain several hundred Arab and other foreign soldiers. While that offensive is in support of the rebels, to members of the Northern Alliance here such bombing suggests the inconsistent nature of the American campaign. 
Alliance officials in that region praised the American strikes, saying that both sides had coordinated the plan. But in general, for alliance forces to break through Taliban lines or around the cities of Mazar-i-Sharif and Galoqan in northern Afghanistan, several days of unyielding strikes should be carried out in one place, alliance leaders back in the Bagram area say.
"Bomb it day and night for four days in a row," a senior alliance official said tonight. "Don't let them sleep. Then we will be able to break the lines."
But the alliance lost ground earlier this week around Mazar-i-Sharif, a city the alliance was thought to have had a good chance of taking only a few days ago.
It is becoming increasingly clear that the loose coalition of northern minority groups fighting the Taliban has its own problems, as it appears increasingly to be a largely ineffective political and military force. It is clearly reeling from the assassination last month of the charismatic military leader Ahmed Shah Massoud. The assassination, carried out by two men said to have ties to Osama bin Laden, appears to have nearly paralyzed the alliance.
The alliance has failed to choose the 60 delegates it will send to a long-delayed meeting on forming a post-Taliban government. Nearly a month ago, an alliance delegation in Rome agreed to send the 60 representatives to meet with 60 other people representing exiled dissident groups and the former king, Mohammad Zahir Shah. But neither side has selected its delegates. Alliance officials say they hope that the meeting will take place sometime before the end of November.
The plodding pace and logistical problems that cripple the alliance were also on display here today. Burhanudin Rabbani, political head of the alliance, gathered with various senior ministers today in the Panjshir Valley. But Gen. Mohammad Fahim, the alliance's new military commander, was unable to attend because a sandstorm kept his helicopter from leaving Tajikistan. Various senior generals including Abdul Rashid Dostum, the commander in the pocket of territory that the alliance controls south of Mazar-i-Sharif, also were unable to attend, in his case because it was too dangerous to leave.
With General Fahim absent, the meeting focused on political matters, a senior alliance official said. Weather permitting, General Fahim should arrive by Monday. 
If it meets, the leadership council could decide where alliance forces should attack. So far, alliance commanders near Kabul say they have received no orders yet to attack.
Refugees arriving from Kabul today, the sort of people who in the past have been fervent in their support of the United States and the Northern Alliance, questioned what both groups were doing. Shiraqa, an unemployed 30-year-old, said that popular support in Kabul was gradually shifting toward the Taliban. "They are fed up with the bombing," he said of the city's residents. "It's lasted too long."
He said that if the bombing continued with no clear result and mounting civilian casualties, frustration with the alliance and the United States would grow. Kabul residents, he said, are looking for decisive military action. "They are very interested in waiting for the Northern Alliance to come into Kabul," he said. "They are waiting, but no one comes."
The tensions here were evident in sermons given today at the funeral for the young woman killed by the American bomb. The woman, Koko Gul, died while sitting at home sewing with her family members. 
The first mullah to speak was an older man who said the Northern Alliance had itself to blame for the death because they asked for American assistance. The second mullah, a younger man, said all foreign countries should be forced out of Afghanistan. As they spoke, the young woman's husband, Mizra, who was at the front line at the time of the bombing, sat listening with tears rolling down his face. 
After the burial, the husband walked away with his hands clasped behind his back. His hands trembled as neighbors offered their condolences.

HEADLINE: China's Heir Apparent in Debut on the World Stage 

BYLINE:  By ERIK ECKHOLM

DATELINE: BEIJING, Oct. 28 

 BODY:
It might seem like the most commonplace of diplomatic missions: the vice president of China touring Moscow, London, Paris, Berlin and Madrid for handshakes with presidents, prime ministers and business titans.
 But the vice president is Hu Jintao, and to the cognoscenti of Chinese politics his journey is filled with meaning. As he makes the trip, which began in Russia this weekend and continues in Britain on Monday, his every word will be scrutinized, and not only in Washington and other foreign capitals, but also at home. 
 That is because Mr. Hu, 58, now appears virtually certain to take over as China's supreme leader in the next 18 months and, if he keeps his cards in order, he may well lead the world's most populous country for 10 years. He is expected to succeed Jiang Zemin, who is now 75 and slated for retirement as head of the Communist Party in the fall of 2002 and then as president that winter. 
Yet astonishingly enough, in an era when China is avidly pursuing global interests, the heir apparent has never before visited Russia or Western Europe, and is thought to have seen the United States only once, as part of a youth delegation in the early 1980's. In fact, no one can recall Mr. Hu ever meeting on his own with a senior American official.
"This trip is something of a coming-out party for Hu," said Joseph Fewsmith, an expert on Chinese politics at Boston University.
To foreign and domestic scholars, Mr. Hu is a political cipher. Even by the secretive standards of Beijing politics, his personal leanings are largely unrevealed on the great issues of the day, including the pace of political change at home and China's ultimate goals abroad.
His venture into big-power diplomacy is another calibrated step in Mr. Hu's cautious but apparently effective ascension, one that under the peculiarities of Chinese politics has required him to keep his head down.
Mr. Hu has been part of the ruling inner circle and the presumed successor since 1992, when Deng Xiaoping, then the paramount leader, labeled him the most promising leader of his generation and promoted him, at the tender age, for China, of 49, onto the all-powerful seven-member Standing Committee of the party Politburo. He has been China's vice president since 1998. 
This much, at least, seems clear: he is part of the governing consensus that favors China's economic opening, but under the continued rule of the Communist Party. Some Western diplomats say they are hopeful that as the spearhead of a new generation, not steeped in the revolution of the 1930's and 40's, he will be a force for faster change. But they also admit that they just do not know.
Party insiders describe Mr. Hu as a smooth and competent operator -- "not a lightweight" -- and he impressed both conservatives and reformers above him through a succession of jobs in the provinces and in Beijing.
He is said to have a photographic memory, and in meetings here with foreign officials, he is known for speaking clearly and without notes. But he hews to the script, and people come away with a feeling that he showed "no surface," as one diplomat put it last week.
The European tour is intended in large part to burnish Mr. Hu's credentials as a statesman for the home audience. The extensive news coverage here, featuring his friendly exchanges this weekend with President Vladimir V. Putin of Russia, has already begun.
"This will be the first opportunity to see him in action on the main international stage," said a Western diplomat. "And the Chinese officials preparing his trip have been very conscious of that. They need to make sure he appears on the right stages with the right backdrops."
The television spots will be the main message in what will mostly be glorified courtesy calls, diplomats said, though the war against terrorism has given him a current topic to discuss.
Mr. Hu's bland image may not simply reflect a cautious personality but also the special demands on him as the front-runner, building on Mr. Deng's blessing, but working hard, avoiding mistakes and not upstaging President Jiang, he managed to solidify his position among the party elite.
Compared with the convulsive personal politics of the Mao era, the coming legally scheduled transition "indicates a new degree of quasi-institutionalism in leadership politics," said Frederick C. Teiwes, an expert on China at the University of Sydney.
Still, the tone could not be more different from Western elections. Ultimate choices are made in secret by a small group of men, in a culture that punishes boasting or overt pursuit of power, and values consensus and stability. "Politically, Hu's profile contains many of the same qualities that made Jiang attractive in 1989," Mr. Teiwes said. "No involvement in the Tiananmen events, an image as a reformist and yet acceptability to both the conservative and reformist wings of the Communist Party."
Born in December 1942, Mr. Hu was a student of hydroelectric engineering at the prestigious Qinghua University in Beijing in the 1960's. 
During the Cultural Revolution he was sent to the remote province of Gansu, where he worked his way up the party ladder in engineering and construction bureaus. He also became involved with the Communist Youth League, and in 1984 he moved to Beijing as its national leader, developing wide contacts that serve him now.
In the 1980's, as the party cultivated younger leaders, Mr. Hu was one of the stars. At 42 he was made party secretary of the poor southern province of Guizhou, giving him hands-on government experience.
Then in 1988 he was given a thankless job: he was made party secretary of Tibet soon after anti-Chinese rioting had broken out. He helped preside over a crackdown, and that stint is seen by party colleagues as proving his toughness and loyalty, though he had health problems in Tibet's high altitude and often stayed outside the province.
More recently, Mr. Hu has had responsibility for major internal party affairs and for the Communist Party School, a core training ground and think tank. Under his guidance the school has become a more lively site of inquiry into political and economic alternatives, experts say, leading to speculation about Mr. Hu's interest in bolder change.
His public unveiling has been steady and deliberate. He gained his first diplomatic experience in the last few years, visiting Japan, South Korea and Africa. In 1999 he became vice chairman of the Central Military Commission, allowing him to start cultivating ties with the military.
Heads were turned in the spring of 1999 when he and not the president delivered a delicate television address to the angry students who threw rocks at the American Embassy here after the NATO bombing of the Chinese Embassy in Belgrade. 
In that appearance, the best glimpse many Chinese have had of Mr. Hu, he emphasized the need for everyone to get back to their studies and jobs, even while repeating China's anger over American policy.
While there has been much speculation that Zeng Qinghong, a party insider who is a protege of Mr. Jiang's, might rival Mr. Hu for the top spot, that now appears unlikely, Chinese and Western experts say. 
Among other signs, Mr. Jiang has not been able to install Mr. Zeng on the Standing Committee before next fall's party congress, when the new party head will be selected.
The other unresolved question, at least among Chinese and Western experts operating outside the "black box" of high-level politics here, is whether Mr. Jiang will also step down from his third major title: chairman of the military commission. Some Jiang supporters have floated the idea that he should stay on for continuity.
Some Chinese and Western experts say Mr. Jiang has the votes to keep that powerful post, which would definitely crimp Mr. Hu's powers. Other experts say that Mr. Jiang appears more likely to resign in full, either because he has lost ground this year, criticized for promoting a personality cult and ramming through his plan to open the party to capitalists or because, in yet another scenario, he is confident of his continued influence behind the scenes.
One of the seemingly strangest aspects of Mr. Hu's career is his avoidance of America and American officials. "For Hu to visit the United States would be unseemly -- as if he were making a power grab," Mr. Fewsmith said.
"Perhaps his trip to Europe should be seen as a sort of a substitute," Mr. Fewsmith said. "It does introduce him to the West and gives Hu a first-hand view of the West."

HEADLINE: MEDIA; 
A Juggling Act for a Pakistani Newspaper in Britain

BYLINE:  By SUZANNE KAPNER 

DATELINE: LONDON, Oct. 28 

 BODY:
As editor of The Daily Jang, a Pakistani newspaper published in Britain, Zahoor Niazi is no stranger to managing competing interests. But his juggling act is increasingly common among the Muslim and Arab press in Britain as they struggle to balance readers' skepticism of American and British military actions in Central Asia with Prime Minister Tony Blair's message of war on terror -- not on Islam.
 From The Daily Jang's offices on a secluded street in London, where the buzz of a half-dozen Pakistani reporters chatting in their native Urdu is periodically interrupted by the Islamic call to prayer, Mr. Niazi, 56, crossed his hands, studded with diamond and topaz rings, and discussed grievances shared by his Muslim readers. 
 Among them are the West's insistence on branding Osama bin Laden and his Al Qaeda network as Muslim fanatics, rather than simply as terrorists, and the willingness of the United States to bomb Afghanistan before producing conclusive proof of Mr. bin Laden's guilt. There is also a lingering distrust of Western powers, whose intervention, many readers think, may provide a temporary solution but lays the groundwork for fresh political squabbles.
"Many Muslims are angry about the same things as Osama bin Laden," said Mr. Niazi, who emigrated from Pakistan in 1981. "They just object to his methods."
That is not to say that Mr. Niazi allows The Daily Jang to publish inflammatory language. On the contrary, with Britain's independent media watchdog, the Press Complaints Commission, on the lookout for fanaticism, he said he is under more pressure than ever to tone down material that might incite violence.
In recent weeks, that has meant refusing to print about one-third of the 20 or so letters the newspaper receives each day. Some, he said, argued for a Muslim uprising to support Mr. bin Laden. It also meant toning down the language of some articles -- like one that reported on the call by Muslim extremists in Britain for a holy war against the Pakistani president, Pervez Musharraf -- so that readers "would not be incited to carry out the instructions," Mr. Niazi said. At the same time, he said, The Daily Jang must be true to readers who are distrustful of the West.
And so, to America's declaration of war on terrorism, The Daily Jang wrote in a Sept. 17, editorial: "The new enemy of the next cold war, till now faceless, nameless and stateless, has been finally identified and tagged and the line of division marked out. The Muslims, preferably Arab, stand damned and segregated."
An editorial on Sept. 24 questioned America's intentions in lifting economic sanctions: "It is doubtful that the United States will open the cornucopia of plenty for Pakistan as participation in the anti-terrorism coalition is mandatory not voluntary."
The voice of Asian newspapers and television stations has gained strength since the world spotlight last fell across this ethnic slice of Britain, in the gulf war. With the current crisis, Prime Minister Tony Blair has wasted little time in courting Asian and Arab journalists, inviting them to briefings, in the hopes they will carry his message back to their communities.
The number of Asian television channels has grown to more than a dozen, from a single station, Zee TV, five years ago, as new technologies make it easier and less costly to transmit news from remote regions. And mainstream media have begun courting the 1.9 million Indian, Pakistani and Bangladeshi population living in Britain, the largest ethnic minority, according to the National Statistics office.
The Daily Jang has changed since Mr. Niazi joined as a reporter in 1983, when the newspaper was written by hand each day, the work of eight calligraphers. Today, Mr. Niazi downloads files in Urdu from the paper's parent in Karachi, the Jang Group, a large South Asian publishing company.
The editor since 1992, Mr. Niazi has presided over changes in the newspaper that mirror shifts in the wider Pakistani community. The trickle of immigrants from Central Asia is slowing, as jobs in the northern textile mills dry up and new laws make it more difficult for people to seek refuge from political oppression. A growing number of the Daily Jang's readers are second- and third-generation Pakistani, who do not speak their native language. So Mr. Niazi has added more coverage of local British news, and has begun printing a quarter of the newspaper in English.
But even those who have lived most of their lives in England and consider themselves British bristle at the way the Western media are covering the Afghan crisis.
"The Western press are reporting from a very biased angle," said Khesar Ahmad, a 27-year-old management consultant who was born in Pakistan but moved to England when he was 3 years old.
And so, Mr. Niazi said, The Daily Jang is trying to offer an alternative view. He has been to news briefings at No. 10 Downing Street, the Prime Minister's residence, eight times since the conflict erupted, nearly triple the number he had attended in his 18 years at the newspaper. And while he said The Daily Jang would publish Mr. Blair's message "whether we agree with it or not," he also intends to air public opinion that remains distrustful of the West.

HEADLINE: In America; 
Shame In the House

BYLINE:  By BOB HERBERT 

 BODY:
"Ask not what your country can do for you . . ."
 It has been 40 years since John F. Kennedy, standing hatless and coatless in the bitter cold of a snow-covered capital, delivered the lines that turned out to be the most stirring and most famous of his presidency. 
 If you listened closely last week, you could hear an echo of that moment on the Senate floor. On Wednesday morning, in an address to his colleagues, Senator Edward M. Kennedy said: "Now we have seen, perhaps more clearly than ever before in our lives, how we are all in this together -- how if even one of us is hurting, all of us hurt. Our first thoughts on September 11 were about others, not ourselves." 
Senator Kennedy, now 69 years old, spoke movingly of the acts of extraordinary bravery and selflessness exhibited by Americans both at home and abroad in this sudden war against terrorism. And he called on the nation as a whole to adopt that spirit of selflessness as the new standard "by which we measure everything we do."
"The standard is clear," he said. "To seek what is right for our country, and not just for ourselves." He said it is essential that Americans not "strive for private advantage in a time of national need."
Not everyone is listening.
Senator Kennedy's speech was, specifically, a call for fairness and common decency as Congress moves ahead with its effort to help revive an economy that was faltering before Sept. 11, and has since been thrown into very serious trouble by terrorism and war.
But last week, as the House narrowly passed its version of an economic stimulus package, the dominant motive at work appeared once again to be greed. The Republicans who control the House thumbed their noses at the ordinary Americans who will absorb the brunt of the economic downturn and shamelessly gift-wrapped yet another bundle of tax cuts for the very well-to-do.
In Senator Kennedy's words, the House proposal, which contains more than $100 billion in tax cuts for corporations and individuals, "merely repackages old, partisan, unfair, permanent tax breaks -- which were rejected by Congress last spring -- under the new label of economic stimulus. The American people deserve better."
With Americans fighting and dying both at home and abroad, we are understandably in a season of patriotism. That patriotism should not be soiled by wartime profiteering.
The House package is a breathtaking example of cynicism and chutzpah. The bill's primary author, Representative Bill Thomas, a Republican from California, piously proclaimed that there is an urgent need to help businesses because they are the nation's employers. "They're the hardware store," he said, "the diner down the street, the gas station on the corner."
And then you look closely at the legislation and find that it overwhelmingly favors the giant corporations, with tax breaks approaching $1.4 billion for I.B.M., more than $800 million for General Motors and $670 million for General Electric.
It's a stimulus package in name only because the Americans who are the most strapped -- the consumers who would take any relief that they received and immediately pump it right back into the economy -- get the least. The package has very little to do with economic recovery. It's about using the shield of war and economic hard times as a cover for the perpetual task of funneling government largesse to the very rich.
Nearly $2 trillion in tax cuts were passed just a few months ago, but that was not enough. True greed knows no bounds.
The political analyst Kevin Phillips, in a commentary on National Public Radio, said: "Neither house of Congress has ever passed this kind of major tax bill in wartime, and no one in the House assumes that the Senate will accept it in whole. But the more extreme the House bill, the further that will drag the eventual compromise in that same inexcusable direction. The only real solution is a public outcry, tens of millions of pointing fingers and voices saying, 'Shame.' "
Forty years after the inauguration of President Kennedy, the most favored and least needy among us are proving themselves to be masterful at finding what their country can do for them.

HEADLINE: A NATION CHALLENGED: THE RECONSTRUCTION; 
A Nascent Effort, Still in Need of a Plan

BYLINE:  By CHARLES V. BAGLI 

 BODY:
Some of the city's most powerful real estate developers, business executives, hotel owners and civic advocates are forming and reforming alliances to plan for the reconstruction of Lower Manhattan in the wake of the World Trade Center disaster.
 Unsure of who will oversee the rebuilding effort, the business leaders, as well as union officials and architects, have at different times -- and sometimes simultaneously -- courted Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, Gov. George E. Pataki, Senators Charles Schumer and Hillary Rodham Clinton as well as the Bush administration. The politicians, in turn, have quietly huddled with their own preferred set of powerful people to talk over what will be the largest urban redevelopment project in American history. 
 But the issue of who will be in charge of rebuilding Lower Manhattan and the city's economy is no clearer today that it was in mid-September. 
"You don't have all the important stakeholders at the same table," said John H. Mollenkopf, director of the Center for Urban Research at City University. "They don't even know where the table is."
The issue is further complicated by the mayor's race and the longstanding friction between the Giuliani and Pataki administrations. The state would rather hold off establishing any kind of downtown commission until after the election on Nov. 6.
Unless something gels soon, civic leaders and downtown landlords say, other companies in Lower Manhattan may join American Express and Lehman Brothers in moving their operations to Midtown, New Jersey or Connecticut, an exodus that would be a devastating blow to the area. They say companies and residential tenants need to be lured back to the area with tax breaks, grants and a plan, with timetables and funding, that inspires confidence in the future of Lower Manhattan.
"You don't have to have 'the' plan," said Harvey Robins, a former aide to Mayors Edward I. Koch and David N. Dinkins. "But you do need a credible leadership, which can help you buy some time and help people feel that their interests will be reflected in this group and the subsequent plan for reconstruction."
A number of names have surfaced in conversations between politicians and business leaders, including Jerry I. Speyer, chairman of Tishman Speyer Properties; Robert E. Rubin, the former treasury secretary who recently became an economic adviser to Mark Green, the Democratic mayoral candidate; and Roland Betts and Thomas Bernstein, the investors who own Chelsea Piers and who are close to President Bush.
The public part of a public-private partnership remains unclear. The continuing friction between Mayor Giuliani and Governor Pataki has been a problem, real estate and business leaders say. The rivalry for credit and control peaked in late September when city officials tried to go around the Pataki administration and set up their own meeting with the White House to discuss federal aid. But according to city and state officials, White House officials invited state officials to the Sept. 24 gathering, where the White House officials chastised the city, saying that New York had to speak with one voice, and made it clear that federal money would flow through the state.
That quelled Mayor Giuliani's effort to set up a commission controlled by the city. Governor Pataki seemed to take a step forward on Oct. 9 when he unveiled a $54 billion proposal to rebuild New York that called for using federal aid to balance the state budget, pay for a wide array of transportation projects in Manhattan and elsewhere and to provide tax breaks for tenants downtown. But the proposal had a kitchen-sink quality to it that met immediate resistance in Washington.
"It landed like a lead balloon down there," said one person who advised the state.
So there are now a variety of lobbying efforts. The Giuliani administration is lobbying Congress for its own scaled-down version of the state proposal, while Senators Schumer and Clinton have been meeting with the Real Estate Board of New York and the New York City Partnership to develop a modest $5 billion financial plan.
At the same time, the real estate industry, the partnership and the city's construction unions are forming a lobbying alliance that includes Henry Kravis of Kohlberg Kravis, Hank Greenberg of the insurance giant A.I.G., Gene McGrath of Con Edison and Bill Harrison of Chase. According to several developers involved in the effort, the Real Estate Board is asking for help from Mr. Betts and Mr. Bernstein, who had been co-owners with President Bush of the Texas Rangers baseball team.
"It's an attempt to resurrect the same kind of business-labor initiative that helped us get through the fiscal crisis in the 1970's," said Kathryn Wylde of the partnership.
That effort, however, does not include the Regional Plan Association and other civic organizations that have formed their own coalitions. "Obviously, everybody thinks they're the best equipped to recommend what the governor and the mayor should do," said Steven Spinola, president of the Real Estate Board. "We believe we represent the people most affected, the owners and the tenants. When you add the Alliance for Downtown New York and labor, that's it."
According to one senior state official, the Pataki administration believes there is little demand for new office construction today, but there is a need for a Second Avenue subway and a transportation terminal near Fulton Street that would link subways, the PATH trains and a newly built Long Island Rail Road station.
"It is my view that one of the main components is going to be infrastructure or transportation projects, which are essential for a revitalized and healthy downtown," said Charles A. Gargano, chairman of the Empire State Development Corporation.
Sandy Weill, chairman of Citigroup, provided an indication of the problem during an Oct. 5 meeting of business leaders with Governor Pataki. Speaking by telephone, according to four people who attended the meeting, Mr. Weill suggested that the trade center be replaced by a baseball stadium, because there was no longer a need for so much office space.
Mr. Weill explained that the financial industry had too many people concentrated in Lower Manhattan.
Governor Pataki is considering creating a subsidiary of the Empire State Development Corporation as the authority that would oversee the downtown reconstruction projects, according to state officials and others involved in the negotiations. The authority would have a six-member board, with the state, the city and the Port Authority each getting two appointments and veto power. The idea has been floated to the Mark Green campaign, which appeared receptive, people close to the talks said.
The Port Authority of New York and New Jersey owns the 16-acre trade center site and runs the PATH rail system.
The Pataki administration is working closely with a small group of corporate executives headed by Mr. Speyer and Mr. Weill, as well as Robert Yaro, executive director of the Regional Plan Association. The association, a private planning organization, is putting together a "civic initiative" by 75 groups to develop some conceptual plans for the trade center site and a memorial to the people who died there.
Larry Silverstein, the developer who had taken control of the World Trade Center last August in a $3.2 billion deal with the Port Authority, and Brookfield Financial Properties, which controls World Financial Center, hired David Childs, an architect, and Alex Cooper, a planner, to develop plans for redesigning the office buildings and the surrounding area.
"We want to be a player in a process yet to be outlined of rebuilding Lower Manhattan," said John Zuccotti, chairman of Brookfield. "We're not going to do it, but we want to be an informed player."

HEADLINE: An Appraisal: A Crystal Beacon Atop a 20's Curiosity; 
Shimmering Tower on Eighth Avenue To Complete Hearst's Headquarters

BYLINE:  By HERBERT MUSCHAMP 

 BODY:
The issue is not how tall a skyscraper should be but whether it's worth the space it takes up on the ground. Norman Foster's design for the new Hearst Corporation headquarters is brilliant. The design is a work of architecture, not a piece of real estate. It helps clarify the distinction between the two. And it will reveal to disbelieving eyes that architecture is still a possibility in New York. The skyline has been waiting for this.
 Lord Foster's design is a parabuilding: a new addition that transforms the character of an existing structure. The host building in this case is Hearst's present home at 959 Eighth Avenue, between 56th and 57th Streets. The parabuilding is a faceted tower of steel and glass that rises 42 stories above the host. Herein lies a historical curiosity. The existing building, completed in 1928, was originally designed as the base of a taller structure. Now that goal will be handsomely fulfilled. Praise be to deferred intentions. 
 The building is a designated New York City landmark. Plans for the new design will be filed today with the city's Landmarks Preservation Commission. The design is also subject to an approval process that will postpone the start of construction until 2003. Hearst officials declined to estimate the cost. Lord Foster, who was awarded the Pritzker Prize for architecture in 1999, designed the Great Court of the British Museum and the reconstruction of the Reichstag in Berlin as the new home of the German Parliament.
Originally called the International Magazine Building, the present six-story headquarters was designed by Joseph Urban and George Post. It is a peculiar pastiche. As envisioned by Hearst, it was intended as a hinge between the Broadway theater district and a new cultural center, anchored by the Metropolitan Opera House, then planned for Columbus Circle. 
Urban, himself a hybrid of architect and stage designer, should have been an ideal choice to bridge the two areas. The result, however, was leaden. With its boxy shape, stumpy elevation and Art Deco ornament, and with concrete walls molded into fake stone blocks, this could be the Depression-era Medical Arts Building of a faded Midwestern downtown. The urn-tipped columns are ominous. The tips might sprout mouths and turn into steam whistles: feeding time for the Morlochs, deep underground.
Lord Foster's tower is a descendant of a building that has influenced the modern Manhattan skyline more than any other: Joseph Paxton's Crystal Palace. Coincidentally, Paxton's temporary structure was completed 150 years ago. Originally erected in Hyde Park in London for what would turn out to be the first world's fair, the Crystal Palace introduced the large-scale use of metal and glass prefabricated construction. The public loved it.
Architects and critics, however, refused to recognize the Crystal Palace as architecture at all. Paxton was not an architect, his method was based on greenhouse design, and critical opinion insisted on a distinction between engineering, like bridges and train sheds, and architecture, then based on the manipulation of period styles. In due course 20th-century architects sided with the Victorian public in recognizing the aesthetic properties of metal and glass construction. The modern office tower was born. Lord Foster's design recapitulates that birth. Mother here is Urban's host building. Baby is huge.
In elevation the design breaks down into four segments. The bottom two floors of the Urban building -- the base of the base -- will retain its existing program: main entrance and lobby on Eighth Avenue, shops along the street, subway connection in one corner. From here escalators will rise to a new main lobby at the third-floor level. The lobby, a column-free space, with ceilings that rise from 40 to 70 feet, has been conceived as a town square. It is enclosed on three sides by "buildings" formed from the upper floors of the existing building. These have been surgically reduced, retaining only the window-studded facades.
A solid glass clerestory surmounts the masonry facades. The tower is inserted into the base like a glass square peg in a solid square hole. Through the clerestory, pedestrians can see the bottom floor of the tower and the V-shaped columns that support it. The "town square" lobby inhabits the space beneath the bottom floor. The handling of this juncture is the key to the design.
The tower is supported by a perimeter "diagrad" system of steel triangular frames that form a structural tube. Stainless steel clads the exterior of the tube. Four office floors are supported by each frame. The triangular shape creates facets along the corners of the tower. In changing light, sparkle, shade and reflections will play across the entire shaft.
None of that phony-baloney skyscraper-crown stuff here, thank you. Instead, the building tapers at the top, in accord with the structural system, forming an organic pinnacle. Since the service space here does require coverage, the rooftop will capture sunlight. At night artificial lighting will intensify the beacon effect.
Now. Just what is it that makes architecture so different, so appealing? Integrity, we used to call it way back when. Meaning: in architecture, there should be a conceptual relationship between the parts and the whole. As in other Foster projects, the relationship here is relatively easy to grasp. It revolves around the correspondence between structure and form, the concept that 20th-century Modernists picked up from Paxton and turned into an urban tradition.
Many architects have felt restrained by that tradition. In the 1960's Robert Venturi's conception of the building as a "decorated shed" came as a liberation to one and all. If only more architects had known what to do with that freedom, this would be a wonderful town. And I do not mean to suggest that the modern idea of integrity was upheld either consistently or to noble purpose in the years when it ruled design.
Often it was violated to great effect. Eero Saarinen's T.W.A. terminal at Kennedy International Airport, for example, was denounced by purists because the smooth concrete surfaces of the roof belied the vast amount of steel it took to hold it up. Nonetheless the building's integrity was manifested in the relationship of its forms and spaces, its interior and exterior, and in the procession through its three-dimensional imagery. The possibilities for integrity are limited only by the mind's capacity to hold unity and complexity together.
That is the capacity that distinguishes architecture from real estate. If only more architects had it, my life would be a joy. On the other hand they might be unemployed. Real estate, of the genre that's likely to multiply in lower Manhattan, represents a substantial investment against integrity. It operates by dividing the whole into disconnected parts.
I hesitate to declare Lord Foster's design a recall to order. We're going to hear all too many recalls to order in the security state years ahead. But a recall to integrity of all stripes, that I can salute.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Above, Norman Foster's design for the new Hearst Corporation headquarters would top a base built in 1928 with a tower indebted to the Crystal Palace, inset, of 1851. (Foster & Partners); (Culver Photos)

HEADLINE: ROCK REVIEW; 
Mixing It Up For Causes Downtown

BYLINE:  By JON PARELES 

 BODY:
New York City has two distinct versions of downtown: the financial capital where the twin towers of the World Trade Center stood until Sept. 11, and the hotbed of free-thinking arts. The Beastie Boys, representing the second downtown, organized a benefit for the first downtown with two sold-out shows Sunday and last night at the Hammerstein Ballroom under the rubric New Yorkers Against Violence. Proceeds went to the New York Women's Foundation and the New York Association for New Americans, which helps recent immigrants, including undocumented victims of the Sept. 11 attacks.
 In the bohemian downtown spirit, Sunday's six-hour concert displayed eclecticism and urban connections: hip-hop, post-punk rock, new wave party songs, Japanese women rapping and something that has been rare at post-attack events: Islamic music. Rahat Fateh Ali Khan, from Pakistan, sang qawwali songs -- Sufi devotional music -- that worked up from straightforward melodies to swooping, raw-voiced improvisations, with him spitting out notes as fast as his drummer could rebound them. It was rocking, ecstatic music, far from the Taliban's ascetic Islam. 
 The night's political speech was by Benjamin R. Barber, author of "Jihad vs. McWorld," who said, "We cannot win the war against terrorism by force and by bombs alone." Ignoring chants of "U.S.A.!," he called for a world "where global capitalism doesn't mean opportunity for some and suffering for many and hopelessness for many more."
The musicians made more ambivalent comments. "I don't think anybody should be afraid to, like, speak out against violence right now," said Sean Lennon before he introduced his mother, Yoko Ono. She spoke about music helping people survive a state of siege and sang one of her wordless, shrieking, glottal-stopping abstract vocalizations. 
Little of the music was topical; the audience came for a good time. The Beastie Boys themselves romped through songs from their recent albums, which touched on various social concerns -- respect for women, tax money for health care, Buddhist ideals of nonviolence -- but mostly touted the Boys themselves and their superb disc jockey, Mixmaster Mike, who kept the tracks behind the rappers changing from verse to verse. The B-52's made their stand for hedonism, knocking out their asymmetrical, new-wave boogies about kisses, interplanetary excursions and imaginary dance crazes.
The Strokes, a New York band that has just released its debut album, "Is That It" (RCA), played a concisely overpowering set. The band sums up an entire school of clean-lined New York rock that turns minimal elements into rock universals, from the Velvet Underground through 1970's Iggy Pop (a Detroit expatriate) through the Feelies (who were actually from New Jersey).
Fast-strummed guitar chords make a glassy wall of harmony over brisk, unyielding drums; the band's singer and songwriter, Julian Casablancas, has the dry voice of someone nursing an emotional hangover, jaded but still exposed. The songs were about fractured relationships and identity crises -- "alone we stand, together we fall apart" -- but determined, insistent guitars and grand crescendos provided the certainty the characters never had. 
Saul Williams, a poet, declaimed and sang about the responsibility of artists to uplift the world's consciousness and built a song around the social critic Albert Murray's term, Omni-Americans. He was backed by a hard-rock band that jammed its way into a neo-psychedelic stomp and wail. 
Cibo Matto juxtaposed the gentle singing and rapping of Miho Hatori and Yuka Honda against earthy, danceable funk and rock from an eight-piece band. It performed its old, lighthearted songs about the sensuality of food alongside the Guns N' Roses urban taunt "Welcome to the Jungle" and "Sci-Fi Wasabi," which mentions New York City's "economic duality."
Events had given an unplanned jolt to the songs of Rival Schools, a new band including members from Quicksand and Civ, two of New York's stalwart post-punk bands. Rival Schools has streaks of Jane's Addiction dissonance and U2 reverberations in songs that churn and surge behind Walter Schreifels's high, frazzled voice. Singing about self-doubt and volatile romance, he also seemed to be blurting out some of New York's clashing responses to Sept. 11: "Lost it all again to start over/try to rout it then." While downtown has never been a unified state of mind, those sentiments wouldn't find many dissenters.

HEADLINE: More Dispiriting Reports on Japanese Economy 

BYLINE:  By KEN BELSON 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Oct. 29 

 BODY:
Japan's teetering economy got another dose of bad news today in the form of weak production figures, and the Bank of Japan released a half-year forecast that predicted no letup in the gloom. More unpleasant statistics are expected on Tuesday.
 Industrial output in Japan was a stark 12.7 percent lower in September 2001 than in September 2000, and fell 2.9 percent from August, the government said today. The month-to-month falloff was twice as steep as executives surveyed before the Sept. 11 attacks expected, suggesting that Japan's economy is cooling off as fast as the United States economy is. 
 As output, capital spending and exports all retreat, blue-chip companies like Fujitsu, and Mitsubishi Electric are cutting jobs, closing factories and slashing wages. Weaker companies are being dropped from life-support by the country's banks, which wrote off about 1.8 trillion yen ($15 billion) in nonperforming loans in the half-year just ended.
With short-term interest rates already near zero and the government reluctant to spend more to whet demand, the Bank of Japan said in its forecast that "it seems inevitable that Japan's economy will continue to experience severe adjustment."
The bank expects Japan's economy, the second largest after that of the United States, to contract as much as 1.2 percent in the year ending March 31, 2002, and slip another 0.5 percent the next year, with no reappearance of growth before September. The bank also expects consumer prices to fall 1 percent this year and another 1 percent next year, further eroding consumer spending and corporate profits.
"The bottom line is, these numbers are bleak," said Chris Walker, an economist at Credit Suisse First Boston in Tokyo. "A lot of people say bleak things, but this is the central bank, after all."
Worryingly, the Bank of Japan held the government responsible for most of Japan's woes, signaling that the bank is unwilling to take further steps of its own to rekindle growth. While making note of the economic fallout, the bank said that it was nevertheless "indispensable to make progress in structural reform," referring to accelerated write-offs of bad bank loans, now often criticized as overly harsh medicine at a time of such economic weakness.
In recent weeks, legislators from the ruling Liberal Democratic Party have shot back, calling on the central bank to set an inflation target and buy more government debt to stimulate growth. Some have even called for the laws governing the central bank to be rewritten.
While politicians argue, the private sector continues to retrench. Automakers reduced production 1.8 percent in September from a year earlier, according to industry figures, with Nissan Motor and Mitsubishi Motors making the largest cuts.
Chip makers, too, are feeling the pinch. NEC said last week that 9,000 of its employees would each take one day of unpaid leave a month through the end of the year. Toshiba, whose sales fell 11 percent in the first half of the fiscal year, announced its third wave of spending cuts since April.
The cutbacks foreshadow further trouble ahead. In a Kyodo News survey of 100 executives, all were pessimistic, and 94 said they thought the economy was "receding."
Households, an economic mainstay until now, are catching the gloom. Consumer spending, 60 percent of the economy, has not deteriorated as fast as other measures, mainly because prices are falling as fast as wages, leaving purchasing power about the same. But consumer confidence is at a three-year low. 
A new picture of Japanese consumers is expected on Tuesday, when employment and household-spending figures are released.
