HEADLINE: Student Designers Undaunted by the Prospects 

BYLINE:  By GUY TREBAY 

 BODY:
BEFORE it was clear to her that "you only have so much time on earth, so you had better spend it doing what you love," Diane Reeves sold computers for Dell. Ms. Reeves's clients were mostly corporate health care centers. She had a degree from the University of Texas, a husband, a young son, a house and a career that many might envy. So it came as a considerable surprise to those close to her when Ms. Reeves decided a year ago to quit her job, pick up stakes and move to New York to become, of all things, a fashion designer. 
 "I made huge sacrifices for this," Ms. Reeves said last week, as she pulled up a stool in Brigitte Conti's sewing class at Parsons School of Design, where Ms. Reeves, 35, was beginning the first term in a two-year program that grants Associate of Arts degrees in fashion. 
 "I dragged a husband and a child from Texas," Ms. Reeves said with a grin and an expression that was far from resigned. "And I'm looking for jobs now with salaries that are equal to my bonus at Dell."
Few professions, it would seem, registered the mass cultural chastening that followed the events of Sept. 11 as sharply as fashion. As early as last spring, economists were making it plain that the retailing outlook for the foreseeable future was grim. "It's tough now," said Kal Ruttenstein, the fashion director of Bloomingdale's. "There are not a lot of new companies opening up. Established companies are not hiring. It's a tough moment for kids to find work in fashion in America." 
zap 
Following the terrorist attacks, the same words and concepts that were once celebrated so lavishly turned up as pejorative terms: ostentation, frivolity, luxury, chic. Who needs those? 
"It seemed so frighteningly fateful that everything happened during Fashion Week," the designer Cynthia Rowley remarked last week. "It was like a call to attention that the glamour, the celebrity, the fame and the hype had gotten out of control. Our priorities were wrong." 
What were those priorities? Were they to sign on to a system that, as the designer John Bartlett recently said, "has gotten distorted to the point where all we've been seeing are the megadesigners and megamodels and everyone's ambition is to be mega?" Or is it equally possible that the expectations many people in fashion hold were already pointing toward simpler, more straightforward aims, fueled by traditional dreams?
Together with the Fashion Institute of Technology, also in Manhattan, Parsons is the pre-eminent incubator of design talent in the country. The alumni roster of the school, which was founded in 1896, includes many of those people in fashion who can be identified by a single name. Donna went to Parsons. So did Calvin, Tom, Isaac and Marc, to name just a few. 
It is easy enough, and understandable, to buy into cliches suggesting that most people who spend $20,000 a year in tuition at Parsons hope to forge big fashion careers, the kind that include one's name branded on the waistband of men's underpants, a heavily capitalized initial public offering, or at the very least a special on E! "More and more, it seems when I've done critiques" of students' work at fashion schools over the past few years, the designer Anna Sui said, "I've found that students were focusing only on money. You'd get the sense that they thought designers spent their days draping beautiful fabric on mannequins, sketching and then having lunch with their supermodel friends." 
But well before September, some fashion insiders say, the tenor of the business was already undergoing change. "What I started telling kids," Mr. Bartlett said, "is that the most creative thing is open a small storefront, where you can keep your costs low and it's much more hands-on." 
Writing to Ingrid Bergman, the filmmaker Jean Renoir once noted that the real basis for great achievement "is daily, humble work within the framework of a profession." Among the surprises a visitor to Parsons School of Design recently encountered is how many of its students came to fashion for reasons that would seem familiar to Mr. Renoir.
Before his arrival at Parsons from his native Thailand, Werawat Lungvedchakarn had already obtained a business degree in Bangkok and worked for a time as an accountant at one of his family's drugstores. 
"My brother is a physician," Mr. Lungvedchakarn explained. "My sister is a pharmacist. My family wanted me to go into the business, but it was like I was forcing myself. I tried, but it was not me. I came to do fashion because it satisfies my passion and my dreams."
Fiona Angelini, a student from Australia, quit a decade-long international career in the cosmetics industry, six of the years spent as a senior marketing executive at Revlon, to take up fashion studies at Parsons. 
"Eighteen months ago, I just said, 'This is not filling my soul,' " Ms. Angelini said. "I was a workaholic before, but I work harder at this than I ever have in my life." 
Jessica Mehta wangled a student visa and special loans to come to New York from India. Joanne Abellera left law school fully aware that "people look down on fashion, especially now, and feel it's frivolous, but I was very unfulfilled and this is the way to feel most creative for me."
At a social and psychic level, Thuy Diep, who is of Vietnamese origin, decided that fashion was less about sound bites and air time than deep psychic comfort. "People think of clothes as unimportant," Ms. Diep said, when of course the act of clothing oneself is "an important expression of art."
"Typically, the students here are the kind of kids who, in high school, if their parents said, 'What do you want to do?' and they said, 'Fashion,' they'd have said, 'You're crazy,' " explained Pamela Trough Klein, the director of Parsons' fashion program, which has 470 students enrolled. "We've had kids from Wharton. We had a guy who was a prosecutor in New Orleans. They come because they have to. They love design."
Lately, Mr. Ruttenstein said, while some in fashion found themselves questioning the purpose of fashion, its creative and commercial viability, others have held tight to optimism. 
"It's nice to think that things will change," Mr. Ruttenstein said, "that things will get better and people will be willing to gamble more and adventuresome talent will be hired again."
Ms. Angelini, for one, was overcome in September with a conviction that there were few things the world needed less than another person's concept for a pretty dress. "I was numb," Ms. Angelini said. "I couldn't do any of it. 
"But little by little, the desire came back, the ideas returned and they had a voice. In this business, whatever the economic climate, whatever the world events, the only thing you can hope for is that yours is a voice that people will want to hear."

HEADLINE: The Big City; 
Shed a Tear For Scalpers On Broadway

BYLINE:  By JOHN TIERNEY 

 BODY:
AT long last, we have a bit of economic good news in New York City. Broadway has discovered capitalism.
 The producers of "The Producers" are about to start offering 50 premium seats each performance for $480 apiece. That's a little less than the typical free-market price being charged by scalpers, but it's a big jump from the current top price at the box office, $100. 
 The show could take in an extra $120,000 per week, much of it from out-of-town visitors and corporations. These customers would ordinarily be patronizing ticket brokers who operate in New Jersey and Connecticut to avoid New York's law against ticket scalping. Once these theatergoers' money goes directly to Broadway instead of the suburbs, New York's economy and tax coffers will benefit.
There would be more benefits if the producers of other hit shows raised their prices, which would seem logical. But one should never expect logic from Broadway, as I've discovered in a lonely decadelong crusade for a free market in tickets. The same people forever whining about the death of the theater are the ones appalled at the notion of keeping it alive with money from paying customers.
Broadway producers and theater owners have long insisted that it would be a public-relations disaster to charge market prices for their hottest tickets, because the public would start assuming that theater is unaffordable. But selling a few high-priced tickets for sports events and concerts has not scared off the average fans -- it has just given them new appreciation for the many cheaper tickets that are still available. 
There are still $31 tickets to "The Producers," and there will always be good discount seats available on Broadway for shows past their prime. If you think every American has an inalienable right to buy orchestra tickets to "The Producers" for under $100, don't worry: eventually we will all have a chance to get them at the TKTS booth. 
Then why have theater owners and producers been so reluctant to sell a few high-priced tickets today? Perhaps because capitalist principles have been suppressed on Broadway by other forces: 
Shame. Pricing tickets according to market demand would provide customers with an instant ranking of shows' popularity. Theatergoers would see the best seats at "The Producers" fetching $480, followed perhaps by "The Lion King" and "Mamma Mia!" at $200 to $300, followed by everyone else all the way down to maybe $25.
It would be the theatrical equivalent of the sales ranking on Amazon.com, which provides an author with the mortifying knowledge that his masterpiece ranks precisely 286,593rd. No director wants to feel that shame, and no producer wants the audience to realize that they are at the 29th most popular show on Broadway. 
Bribery. When hot tickets are priced way below their market value, anyone with access to those tickets can profit. The most obvious beneficiaries are box-office employees who take bribes for choice seats, but a lot of other insiders can reap tax-free benefits by selling tickets to scalpers. 
Cronyism. The most vocal critics of a free market in tickets have tended to be politicians and journalists, who are used to special access to the best seats. They may pay the face value of the ticket, but they're essentially getting a gift, because the market value is much higher. To them, the system is working fine as is.
ANOTHER vocal critic is Isabelle Stevenson, the chairwoman of the American Theater Wing, which founded the Tony Awards, whose dismay at the $480 tickets was quoted in Saturday's paper. "Shame!" she said. "Shame on not caring about helping make theater more available." 
But charging market prices for some seats does makes theater more available -- to the kind of people who did not get tickets to opening night of "The Producers," as Ms. Stevenson did. Now that these outsiders are being given a shot at buying their way into "The Producers," other shows may decide to stop giving away their best seats to insiders. And the more tickets that are made available at market prices, the lower those prices will go. 
Suburban brokers have been getting $700 for some of the few good "Producers" tickets that are now available, but those prices may not last after hundreds of new tickets hit the market every week. In fact, some suburban brokers are already wondering how long the $480 price will hold. Broadway may just be learning capitalism, but the brokers know that the laws of supply and demand apply even to Mel Brooks.

HEADLINE: Unemployment Isn't as Easy as Falling Off a Horse 

BYLINE:  By ALAN FEUER 

 BODY:
It has been three months since Frankie Ferrara was set on fire. It has been just as long since he was flung from a window, punched in the face or run down by a speeding car.
 John Cenatiempo hasn't driven off a bridge since summer, and it's starting to show. He seems a little antsy and orders that extra Scotch at the bar these days. After all, it's been three months. 
 The only thing a stuntman wants more than a good steak dinner after an easy landing is a calendar filled with shooting schedules and well-paid jobs. The stuntman's credo is a simple one: When I fall, dear Lord, please don't let the cellphone break.
Recently, though, there have been fewer and fewer calls from Hollywood producers to the nervous corps of New York City's stuntmen. Because of last month's terrorist attacks, the moguls have shied away from car chases, pyrotechnics, skyjackings and blood, and the men who break their bones making the movie stars look good are starting to feel some pain.
"We deal with explosions and violence primarily, and that's all been pulled way back," said Mr. Ferrara, a stuntman with a side-of-beef torso and hands as thick as telephone books. "The last time I worked was in August, and it's been devastating ever since. Thank God we get residuals, or else I'd really be sucking wind."
Action movies are a stuntman's bread and butter -- not to mention his steak, his potatoes, his salad and his dessert -- and since the tragedy of Sept. 11, several thrillers have been scrubbed from production slates or put on indefinite hold. The studios, for instance, have not released Arnold Schwarzenegger's "Collateral Damage," about a fireman whose family is killed in a terrorist strike. The Tim Allen comedy "Big Trouble," which involves a bomb on an airplane, has also been shelved for the foreseeable future.
Though some feature films and television shows like "Law and Order" have slowly resumed production in New York, the men who specialize in on-screen mayhem have taken a tremendous hit. Recently, a group of them gathered for dinner at a steakhouse in Gramercy Park to drown their sorrows in sauteed spinach and peppered beef. The conversation ranged from firefighters to fireballs to figuring out what to do now that Hollywood is temporarily disinclined to blow things up.
"It's been totally dead," said Danny Aiello Jr., who has worked on movies like "Diehard III" and "Cop Land." "It's been driving me crazy. As a stuntman and a stunt coordinator, I've had absolutely nothing to do."
Mr. Aiello, in fact, has been spending time playing basketball and golf and working for his new charity, World Trade Aid, which gives scholarships to the sons and daughters of those who died in the trade center attack. Mr. Ferrara has been loving his children and working on his home in southern Brooklyn. Pete Bucossi, another stuntman, got a part-time job as the stunt coordinator on the soap opera "All My Children" a few weeks ago. The most alarming stunt he has arranged so far is showing one actor how to grab another actor's tie.
The images from the trade center catastrophe -- the orange blossoms of flame, the smoking piles of rubble -- are frighteningly familiar to the professional daredevils who spend their days sweating it out among celluloid disaster. Nevertheless, they said, the pyrotechnics of the attack looked far too stagy. They have seen things go boom on screen before, and it didn't look like that. "It actually looked fake," Mr. Aiello said. 
These men live with fear like others live with a nagging head cold. Their wives tell them to be careful every morning before they head off to work to bruise their bodies. Now, the admonitions have a darker undertone, they say.
And the stuntmen are in awe of the firefighters who rushed into the buildings without a second thought to get the victims out. 
"People say we're crazy because we jump from burning buildings, but at least we know where the fire's been set ahead of time," Mr. Aiello said. "These guys go in blind and show no fear. We're not crazy. They're the crazy ones."

HEADLINE: Argentina May Restructure Its Debt, Risking Default 

BYLINE:  By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER 

 BODY:
Argentine bond and stock prices fell sharply yesterday after the country's economy minister indicated that he wanted to voluntarily restructure Argentina's billions of dollars in public-sector debt, including that owned by foreigners.
 Such a move acknowledged how much more serious the country's financial crisis is. The economic slowdown around the world after the terrorist attack on America is just deepening a recession in Argentina that already is more than three years old. And there are fewer and fewer spending cuts the country can make so that it can meet its interest and principal payments. 
 But some analysts argue that the effort to restructure the debt, although necessary to reduce the country's debt burden, could lead to a default. Some major credit agencies have said that even a voluntary restructuring of Argentina's debt would lead them to give the debt a default rating. But it is not clear if a voluntary restructuring would qualify as a legal default. Some analysts argue that it would not.
The news sent Argentine bonds prices down as much as 14 percent while yields on one benchmark bond soared to 45 percent. The yield on an index of Argentine bonds jumped to almost 20 percent points over comparable United States Treasury securities. The local stock market index, the Merval, plunged 8.76 percent.
Delays in new economic policy announcements from the government also made investors nervous yesterday, as did the resignation of Argentina's undersecretary of finance. Some analysts interpreted his resignation as a sign of disagreement within the government over how to resolve the country's debt problems.
Domingo Cavallo, the economy minister, acknowledged the new effort to manage the debt in an interview with the local newspaper La Nacion. Mr. Cavallo said that he wanted an "integral solution" to the country's debt problem "as soon as possible," according to a translation by BNP Paribas. But he emphasized that he wanted such restructuring to be voluntary, adding that he did not like the word restructure because it seemed to imply coercion.
The newspaper also said that Mr. Cavallo had been working with Jacob Frenkel, a former top official of the International Monetary Fund and a former governor of the Bank of Israel. Mr. Frenkel is now the president of Merrill Lynch International.
A Merrill spokesman said nothing had been formalized with Argentina but confirmed there had been discussions about hiring Mr. Frenkel and Merrill.
Argentina has about $132 billion in public-sector debt, of which about $70 billion is in global bonds. Mr. Cavallo indicated that he wanted to swap this old, high-interest-rate debt for new securities that have a lower interest rate. He did not say how he would entice investors into such a swap, but indicated that Argentina would be better able to guarantee that the principle and interest would be paid.
Several analysts said that such a big swap, or restructuring, of debt would be hard to accomplish voluntarily. If it provided any major relief for Argentina from its burden of interest and principle payments it might turn out to be a very unattractive swap for investors. "If you do an exercise that massive on favorable terms, the chances that you will be successful with a voluntary restructuring is not very high," said Jose M. Barrionuevo, director of emerging markets strategy and economics at BNP Paribas.
Mr. Cavallo had been working on a swap of old debt for new, lower-interest debt with local banks and pension funds and apparently still wants to include this debt in any swap.
But some top credit rating agencies said that they would view such a swap as a default, even if voluntary.
In a statement on Oct. 16, Standard & Poor's said it regarded the completions of a debt exchange "as tantamount to a default -- if the total value of the securities or cash offered is materially less than the originally contracted amount (even though it may be greater than the current depressed market value of the debt)."
S.& P. now rates Argentine sovereign debt a CCC+, which reflects a high probability default. D is the default rating.
On the other hand, many long-time analysts in the emerging market bond business view voluntary swaps of debt as a way to avoid default. "If an exchange is done in a voluntary manner that would not constitute a default," said Desmond Lachman, director of emerging markets economic research at Salomon smith Barney.
In June, Argentina swapped about $30 billion of debt that delayed current interest payments in exchange for high interest in the future and it was not a default.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Traders reacted as stock prices plunged yesterday on the Buenos Aires Stock Exchange after Argentina's economy minister indicated that he wanted to voluntarily restructure the nation's public-sector debt. (Reuters)

HEADLINE: Detroit Mayoral Rivals Wage Close Battle of Generations 

BYLINE:  By DANNY HAKIM 

DATELINE: DETROIT, Oct. 29 

 BODY:
While the events of Sept. 11 may have led to a spirit of bipartisanship in Washington, this city's mayoral candidates, both Democrats, are locked in a bitter generational battle that is one of the closest races here in decades.
 Kwame M. Kilpatrick, 31, the minority leader of the State House and the son of a congresswoman, is vying to be the city's youngest mayor since 1933. He is an affable former star Florida A&M tackle prone to getting in trouble for speaking his mind. 
 His opponent, Gil Hill, 69, the City Council president, is a spirited, folksy former homicide detective best known for a brush with Hollywood; in the "Beverly Hills Cop" movies, he played the scenery-chewing Detroit boss of Eddie Murphy. 
Mr. Kilpatrick won the Sept. 11 nonpartisan primary by about 16 percentage points, outpolling 20 other candidates. Polls since have shown that his lead over Mr. Hill in the Nov. 6 election is in the single digits.
That politics as usual continues here is not a surprise. For Detroit, the biggest issue has not changed: the economy was in bad shape before Sept. 11. Now, it is worse.
"This is a hard city to scare," said Rodney Stewart, a 59-year-old electrician. "It's a working man's town. What scares people most around here is the economy."
David Littman, chief economist of Comerica Bank, warned. "If U.S. unemployment rates move to 5 percent, which I expect, Detroit's will be 11 plus." 
The Big Three automakers all reported net losses in the third quarter, aggravated by bleak September sales, by parts shortages due to the shutdown of air traffic and border slowdowns, and by a price war that has driven up sales but threatened profits. It has not helped that Northwest Airlines, which has a hub here, has laid off 10,000 workers. 
"When the American economy sneezes," Mr. Hill said, "Detroit gets pneumonia." 
Dennis W. Archer, Detroit's departing mayor, said the city's budget would have to be cut by 5 percent, or about $60 million, to avoid a deficit.
Mr. Archer's eight years in office came after two decades under Mayor Coleman A. Young and his formidable political machine, a time when whites, and then middle-class blacks, left the city for the suburbs. The city's population fell from more than two million in the 1950's to less than one million in the 2000 census. 
Mr. Archer, who plans to run for the presidency of the American Bar Association, repaired relations between the suburbs and Detroit and brought big-ticket items downtown like G.M.'s headquarters and Comerica Park, the stadium of the Detroit Tigers.
But there is still much to do. Crime and drugs remain a problem. Abandoned buildings can be seen in many neighborhoods, and street lights work only intermittently.
"I live in Palmer Woods, one of the nicer neighborhoods, and our street lights don't work," said Mario Morrow, an independent political consultant and analyst. "My mother takes the bus and checks the schedules, but the buses don't go by the schedules." 
"Some of the streets are in shambles," Mr. Morrow added. "It'll tear your car up going down the street."
Mr. Kilpatrick wants to consolidate the city's 43 agencies without cutting the work force and expand small-business development. And, while recent debate in the city has focused on casino development, he believes it is more significant that Compuware, a software company, has decided to move its headquarters to Detroit.
Mr. Hill's focus has been more meat and potatoes. Fixing basic services to stem middle-class flight is more important than any digital revolution, he has said.
Mr. Kilpatrick grew up on the campaign trail. His mother is Representative Carolyn Cheeks Kilpatrick, and his father, Bernard Kilpatrick, is a former county commissioner. 
But despite his political roots, his candor makes consultants wince. He angered lawmakers from other parts of the state when he referred to Michigan as "the Mississippi of the North" over the state government's treatment of Detroit. He angered gay and lesbian groups when he said on a local television show that he did not want his sons "even seeing that type of lifestyle." 
A recent advertisement by Hill supporters had Mr. Kilpatrick, who has been criticized for accepting money from a group that runs homeless shelters, quip: "We tried to get a check from Mother Teresa. She wasn't writing checks." 
Mr. Kilpatrick struck back with commercials criticizing Mr. Hill's City Council record and his tenure as the city's top homicide inspector, recalling a quotation from a magazine in which Mr. Hill said, "I was a mediocre inspector, at best."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Gil Hill, 69, left, and Kwame M. Kilpatrick, 31, are in a bitter struggle to succeed Mayor Dennis W. Archer in one of the closest mayoral races Detroit has seen in decades. A major issue is the city's ailing economy. (Photographs by Jim West for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS AND BONDS; 
Major Gauges Drop Sharply as Investors Take Profits

BYLINE:  By SHERRI DAY 

 BODY:
The stock market fell sharply and broadly yesterday as investors took a look around and decided the time was right to sell.
 The Dow Jones industrial average fell 275.67 points, to 9,269.50, a loss of 2.9 percent. The Nasdaq composite index lost 3.9 percent of its value, closing down 69.44 points at 1,699.52, while the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index dropped 2.4 percent to end the day at 1,078.30, lower by 26.31 points. 
 Investors seized the chance to take profits won in the powerful stock rally of recent weeks. Before trading commenced yesterday, the S.& P. had risen 14 percent since Sept. 21. The Nasdaq had jumped 24 percent in that period. 
But investors had other reasons to sell stocks yesterday, including a growing fear that the war in Afghanistan could be long and might delay an economic rebound in the United States. A 1.3 percent decline in the value of the dollar against the euro also hurt stocks, and Congressional dickering over a stimulus package that was supposed to help the hobbled economy seemed to disturb investors as well. 
"People just seem to be lost because they know the economy is bad, but they don't know how bad," said Paul McManus, a portfolio manager at the John Hancock Core Equity Fund. 
The market's biggest decliner was the Boeing Company, which lost what may be the largest military contract in history on Friday. Its shares plummeted 10.4 percent. But technology stocks, which had been roaring in recent weeks, also fell yesterday. Declines in semiconductor, computer and software issues weighed heavily on the Nasdaq composite.
"There is very little in the way of economic news and very little in the way of earnings updates or conference calls to try to provide some excitement here," said Christopher J. Wolfe, equity strategist for J. P. Morgan Chase's private bank. 
With virtually no economic data released yesterday, investors relied on sentiment, which has been flagging. The result was a broad-based sell-off, in which few sectors were immune. "The news broadcasts were very negative over the weekend," said Charles R. Wolf, an analyst at Needham & Company. "The government doesn't know how to handle the anthrax scare, we're not doing well in Afghanistan, are we going to go into Iraq? There wasn't one piece of good news." 
Nor is there likely to be any soon. Investors are bracing for a series of economic reports this week that may be dismal, including consumer confidence data and unemployment figures for October. Tomorrow, the government will report on the third-quarter gross domestic product. 
"The economic data is very important," said Owen Fitzpatrick, the head of the U.S. Equity Group at Bankers Trust Private Banking. "That's what the market will start to trade on now that the earnings season is almost over." 
Mr. McManus said that selling picked up as the session progressed. "What happens on these days is once people see the market starting to roll over, then everybody just steps out of the way," he said. "If a bet doesn't work out then they take it off."
As one of the 30 Dow industrials, Boeing exerted a significant drag on the market yesterday. For more than six years, the company had been competing against Lockheed Martin for the contract to build the next generation of stealth fighter jets, a $200 billion Defense Department program. Lockheed won the contract Friday after the stock market closed. Still, Lockheed's shares fell 92 cents yesterday, to $49 each.
General Motors fell $2.64, or 5.8 percent, to $42.76, after the company said that it would sell its satellite television unit to EchoStar Communications for nearly $26 billion. EchoStar and the News Corporation had been in a bidding war for Hughes Electronics, General Motors' satellite division, maker of DirecTV. EchoStar shares fell 4.6 percent, to $24.08. Gemstar TV-Guide International, the satellite arm of News Corporation, which withdrew its bid on Saturday, plummeted 13 percent, to $20.14, on the deal's announcement. 
Semiconductor stocks were particularly weak as companies reported sluggish sales and price cuts. The Philadelphia Stock Exchange's semiconductor stock index dropped 33.53 points, or 6.97 percent, to 447.21. 
Among other technology issues, Microsoft dropped 4 percent, I.B.M. lost 2.3 percent, and Dell Computer shed 5.5 percent.
Telecommunications stocks also weakened, with the exception of WorldCom, which rose 3 percent, to $13.77. After an encouraging earnings report from the company, analysts at Lehman Brothers raised the company's rating from buy to strong buy.
Analysts said that although yesterday's pullback was normal given the market's recent gains, trading could be erratic until investors could identify some evidence of an economic recovery. --------------------

Treasury Prices Rise
By Reuters
 Treasury bond prices rose yesterday as pessimism spread about the depth of the nation's economic slump before a flurry of economic reports.
The 10-year Treasury note rose 10/32, to a price of 104 3/32. The note's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, fell to 4.48 percent from 4.52 percent on Friday.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond rose 4/32, to 101 23/32. The bond's yield fell to 5.26 percent from 5.27 percent on Friday.

HEADLINE: Catering for Rescue Workers Angers Restaurant Owners 

BYLINE:  By SARAH KERSHAW 

 BODY:
Since Sept. 11, a Long Island catering company, working under contract with the Salvation Army, has been providing an average of 7,500 meals a day to rescue workers in New York City, delivering waffles, stuffed shells and pork chops from its kitchens in Suffolk County. 
 The arrangement has angered struggling restaurant owners in Lower Manhattan, including one restaurateur and caterer a few blocks from ground zero, who said Salvation Army officials told him that the agency would seriously consider dividing the food business among the all-but-empty downtown restaurants. 
 Instead, the Salvation Army stuck with the suburban caterer, Whitsons, which is delivering 7,500 meals a day to four sites around the city. Salvation Army officials would not say how much they were paying the company, which they said they had used for more than 20 years to provide Thanksgiving dinners and other meals to the needy. 
Maj. George Polarek, who is overseeing the relief effort for the Salvation Army, said that several restaurant owners had approached him, seeking to cater meals, and that dividing up the business would be logistically overwhelming. He also said the agency needed a caterer that could provide a large volume of meals in a hurry; Whitsons had the experience with large catering jobs and a good track record with the Salvation Army.
"They've been doing so much to help the rescue workers that we decided to stay with them," Major Polarek said. "In the midst of a crisis, we didn't put this out to bid. There's no reason to put this out to bid. When you need to feed 100,000 people, you go with the people you know."
Officials at Whitsons, in Huntington Station, also refused to disclose how much the Salvation Army was paying for the food, but Holly Von Seggern, the caterer's director of communications, said the company had donated more than $50,000 worth of labor and was not charging the Salvation Army for delivery, inspection and other services related to providing the meals. 
David Gruber, the owner of Brewbakers, a restaurant at Pearl and John Streets, said that early this month, when he realized that catering meals at ground zero might offset some of the economic losses he and other business owners had suffered since the Sept. 11 terrorist attack, he approached Salvation Army officials to propose that they contract with local businesses to provide the meals. 
"I told them we were dying down here," said Mr. Gruber, whose restaurant was closed for two weeks after the attack and has had few customers since it reopened.
"You know how many downtown restaurants could have gotten themselves on their feet with some of this business?" he said. "I'm two minutes away and they've got someone coming by bridges and tunnels."
E. Charles Hunt, executive vice president of the Greater New York Chapters of the New York State Restaurant Association, was also critical of the Salvation Army, saying, "They certainly should have given some preference to the people in the Lower Manhattan area."
The American Red Cross, which is providing an average of about 16,000 catered meals daily at ground zero and another 27,000 at the Family Assistance Center at Pier 94, is using two Manhattan caterers for most of those meals. A spokeswoman for the agency said the caterers, Bouley Consulting, a division of Bouley International, and Great Performances, were selected because they were the lowest bidders.

HEADLINE: Sept. 11 Exacts an Economic Toll From the Hospitals of New York 

BYLINE:  By KATHERINE E. FINKELSTEIN 

 BODY:
Minutes after the second plane hit the World Trade Center, hospitals in Manhattan activated their disaster plans: They canceled elective surgery, discharged stable patients and closed walk-in clinics, emptying their beds to await the thousands of expected casualties.
 But those patients never arrived. And in the following weeks, the fallout from Sept. 11 -- including transportation difficulties and telephone failures -- kept regular patients away, and some hospitals stood one-quarter empty. Once anthrax hit, panicked New Yorkers in search of Cipro flooded emergency rooms, costing time and resources but not needing to be admitted and therefore generating little income. 
 And now, the city's high-tech hospitals, which have functioned largely as free family centers, bereavement sites and giant cafeterias, face new capital costs -- from building decontamination showers to designing ventilation systems that can withstand bioterrorism. 
In six traumatic weeks, hospitals in New York City have lost $367 million, most of it from a dramatic decrease in patient admissions, according to the city's Health and Hospitals Corporation and the Greater New York Hospital Association, a trade organization. The hospitals also made many sudden emergency purchases, from $50,000 in bed linen at one hospital, to $49,000 in blood collection at another, according to grant applications submitted to the Federal Emergency Management Agency.
At a time when New York City is seeking billions in federal aid to recover from the attacks, these numbers may sound small, said Patricia Wang, a senior vice president at the hospital association. But the city's vast nonprofit medical centers have little margin and huge costs, just like the airline industry, she explained. "The difference is, we weren't one hundred percent grounded."
Indeed, at every step of the way, the hospitals were up and running, and hemorrhaging cash. So now, as some vendors threaten to withhold credit and hospitals struggle to meet payroll, they are launching a vast effort, not only to recover lost revenue, but to avert Medicaid cuts that loomed even before the trade center attacks.
Beyond these immediate struggles, however, the larger issue in New York City and the nation will be how to restructure a health care system that must function as a vital part of national security in the case of further terrorist attacks, said James R. Tallon, president of the United Hospital Fund, a philanthropy group. Questions of restructuring will have the greatest impact in the city, where many of the nation's teaching hospitals are concentrated. 
"How do we go from a highly competitive marketplace to one in which the government works with the health care community to make decisions about new investments?" Mr. Tallon said. 
All hospitals are required by federal and state regulations to have well-articulated disaster plans, the most dramatic requiring them to empty their hospitals of all nonurgent patients to prepare for casualties.
And so on the morning of Sept. 11, the hospitals were effectively "ordered to lose money," said Kenneth E. Raske, president of the Greater New York Hospital Association. And as out-of-town patients decline to travel to New York City, and elective surgeries are postponed, hospitals continue to lose money. 
By 7:30 a.m. the day of the attack, all 33 operating rooms at New York University Hospital at 30th Street and First Avenue in Manhattan were humming with a vast array of elective procedures that on a normal day bring in $1.7 million in revenue. 
But when Dr. Steven Hofstetter, chief of surgical services, got the call that the city was expecting 40,000 casualties, systemwide, he immediately swept through the operating rooms and canceled elective procedures, including those for dozens of patients already waiting. 
Dr. Hofstetter even ordered that two patients who had already been anesthetized and were about to undergo long, complex procedures be awakened and transferred to recovery rooms, so as not to tie up the services of surgeons and nurses for hours. 
Dr. Lawrence Grossman, a cardiothoracic surgeon there, was a third of the way through a complex chest reconstruction when he decided to halt the operation, close up the patient and send him to the recovery room, to reschedule his surgery for another day. 
And by 1 p.m., Dr. Hofstetter ordered that all the next day's surgeries canceled as well. By Thursday morning, according to a grant application, N.Y.U. Medical Center had lost $3.6 million in expected revenue, not including the cost of a vast data center two blocks from the World Trade Center that was destroyed, disabling electronic patient records, lab work and billing systems. 
The city's vast public hospital system, which estimates it has lost $27.4 million since the disaster, also quickly transferred 300 patients from four of its trauma hospitals to nursing homes and mental-health facilities around the city, said Dr. Luis R. Marcos, president of the city's Health and Hospitals Corporation. 
As similar scenes played out across the city, the financial losses, from staffing command centers and emergency morgues to recouping crushed ambulances, had just begun. St. Vincent's Manhattan Hospital on West 12th Street, the closest comprehensive trauma center to the disaster site, instantly closed all six of its walk-in clinics, opened six ancillary emergency areas, and purchased a two-week supply of equipment, including 300 oxygen tanks and 18,000 pieces of burn linen. 
While St. Vincent Catholic Medical Centers' network of seven hospitals treated 17 percent of the 5,943 patients from the disaster, for weeks the inpatient admissions at its Manhattan center remained down by almost half. Mark Ackerman, chief corporate services officer, said that the St. Vincent system had lost $11 million in the weeks immediately after the attack and expected to lose $27 million over six months, from capital expenses and depressed revenue. "It is devastating," he said. 
Hospitals also lost supplies by the ton. 
On Sept. 11, with few patients emerging from the rubble, Dr. Ron Krinick, chief of orthopedic surgery at N.Y.U. Downtown Hospital, just blocks from the disaster site, grabbed his staff and the most expensive equipment -- numerous $8,000 battery-powered drills for on-site amputations and $12,000 clamps for setting mangled bones and set up a triage site at 1 Liberty Plaza. 
But then firefighters warned of a building collapse and everybody ran, abandoning the equipment. "We lost every drill in the hospital and most of our casting equipment," Dr. Krinick recalled, adding, "We replenished it immediately -- by going into debt." 
At N.Y.U. Medical Center farther uptown, teams of doctors grabbed needles, bandages, gloves and intravenous tubing from the shelves, as they set off for impromptu triage sites near the trade center. "No one was saying, 'Who are we going to charge this to?' " said Dr. Hofstetter.
Most was unused but too covered with ash and dirt to be salvaged, a $1 million loss in off-the-shelf medical supplies alone, he estimated.
Meanwhile, on the Upper East Side, Mount Sinai Hospital, bearing a Jewish name that could invite a terrorist attack, increased its security budget by 30 percent. 
And it kept its dialysis machines open around the clock, as federal regulations dictate that in the event of disaster, hospitals closest to patients' homes must assume responsibility for their kidney treatment. The overtime cost for this service alone was $70,000, said Joan Lebow, a hospital spokeswoman. 
And seven of New York Presbyterian Hospital's ambulances were crushed beneath the rubble, for a net loss of $667,000, after insurance reimbursement, according to the hospital's grant applications. 
The costs just grew from there. 
Most of the city's hospitals estimate that, in the weeks that followed, their admissions fell by one-fourth. Some doctors speculate that patients may have stayed away on the mistaken assumption that the hospitals were swamped. "It was totally dead for two weeks," said Dr. Michael Mojica, a pediatric emergency room doctor at Bellevue Hospital Center. "No one came to the hospital. We were not doing anything." 
At St. Vincent's Manhattan, where admissions were down by half, things got so slow that emergency-room doctors resorted to treating rescue dogs for smoke inhalation. 
But N.Y.U. Downtown Hospital was the hardest hit financially. With Lower Manhattan closed to traffic, the hospital's referral base around the trade center destroyed, and the phones and electricity out, it had almost no patients. Since the disaster, it has lost $3.8 million in revenue, more than half of its annual revenue. "We need cash and we need it now," said the hospital's president, Dr. Leonard A. Aubrey. 
And then on Friday Oct. 12, as news broke that an NBC employee had tested positive for anthrax, the hospitals woke up to a new financial challenge: how to handle a deluge of patients that, at best, would needlessly burn time and money, and at worst, contaminate the hospital if, for example, they brought anthrax spores in on their clothing. 
And despite the prohibitive costs, hospitals have begun to prepare. They must train their doctors to recognize the symptoms of anthrax and other potential biological weapons, create separate triage systems for patients complaining of sore throat and fever, and build outsized decontamination showers in the event of a large-scale bioterrorism attack. 
Bellevue Hospital Center at 27th Street and First Avenue, though already in debt, expects to build a $500,000 decontamination shower, said Dr. Lewis Goldfrank, director of emergency medicine. 
And at St. Luke's-Roosevelt Hospital Center on 59th Street, part of a health network that estimates $15 million in losses since the attack, officials are considering a new ventilation system to separate the emergency room from the rest of the hospital. This change will inevitably become a vast new capital expenditure. As Dr. Dan Weiner, director of emergency medicine, explained, "There's just no way to pass this cost on to patients."

HEADLINE: Drop in Price Pushes OPEC To Consider Cut in Output 

BYLINE:  By NEELA BANERJEE 

 BODY:
After talks over the weekend and yesterday, officials from the Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries indicated that the group would probably authorize a substantial cut in daily output at its next meeting on Nov. 14 to bolster low prices.
 In the wake of the Sept. 11 attacks and the decline of the global economy, the group, which supplies about 40 percent of the world's oil, has failed to keep prices in the range of $22 to $28 a barrel that it has long favored. The slump in oil prices has cost the 10 voting OPEC members billions of dollars in revenue. 
 On Saturday, the Saudi oil minister, Ali al-Naimi, said, "All necessary measures will be taken with all major oil producers to manage the oil price and return it to the target band of $22 to $28 a barrel."
After discussions at the group's headquarters in Vienna yesterday, Mr. Naimi echoed those thoughts, saying OPEC "will decide the level it takes to maintain the price band." 
Mr. Naimi and other ministers declined to specify how deep the output cuts would be. But most industry analysts, who said the overtures were credible, estimated that the decrease would be 700,000 to a million barrels day. The group has pulled back production three times this year by a total of 3.5 million barrels, or about 13 percent. 
Prices rose slightly on the news of the possible reduction. Crude oil for December delivery rose 12 cents yesterday, to $22.15 a barrel, on the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Oil traders were lukewarm about the production decrease in large part because OPEC has had limited success in reining in rampant overproduction among its members.
The group authorized a reduction of a million barrels a day effective Sept. 1, but members continued to produce about 1.3 million barrels a day more than their official quotas to cash in on the relatively high prices. But data for oil production so far in October show that OPEC nations are overproducing by 500,000 to 700,000 barrels a day, a signal, industry experts said, of tightening compliance.
OPEC has also been trying to persuade major oil producers outside the group to reduce production or at least hold to current levels -- so far in vain. Russia and Mexico, two major producers with the additional capacity to pump more oil, said they had no plans to trim output.
All sorts of unknowns loom that could thwart OPEC's plans. Oil demand has fallen substantially as the global economy has slumped. But some analysts said the group risks cutting too deeply just as heating oil demand picks up. The course of the American retaliation against Osama bin Laden could also jar the world economy again, and if it expands to include Iraq, oil production in the Persian Gulf could be thrown into disarray. 
More probably, Iraq could shake up the delicate balance that OPEC is trying to strike between supply and demand by withdrawing its exports from the market. In early December, the United Nations oil-for-food program is up for regular review, and Iraq has often reduced its exports of about 2.1 million barrels day to press for greater leeway. Saudi Arabia has traditionally filled the breach and made up for lost Iraqi oil. Whatever cut OPEC may make might not go into effect until Jan. 1, said Roger Diwan of the Petroleum Finance Company in Washington, so that the group could gauge just how to respond to Iraq's maneuvers.

HEADLINE: TV Stations Scrambling for Antennas, Ratings and Revenue 

BYLINE:  By BILL CARTER 

 BODY:
Six weeks after the collapse of the World Trade Center, hundreds of thousands of homes in the New York area remain unable to receive more than two television stations, and the local broadcasting industry, already reeling from economic losses of tens of millions of dollars, finds itself increasingly desperate to find a long-term solution that will restore its ability to reach every possible viewer.
 Because the main antennas atop the north tower came down, all the local stations, except Channels 2 and 41, which had backup antennas on the Empire State Building, have lost a significant part of their audiences, ranging between 10 percent and 35 percent. 
 WNET, Channel 13, which was most vulnerable to the damage to its broadcast signal because it has the smallest percentage of viewers who subscribe to cable television, has suffered the largest loss, about a third of its audience. The station has completely stopped on-the-air fund-raising. 
"If nothing changes and we continue to lose one-third of our audience, we would be taken out of the business," the station's president, Bill Baker, said.
According to the BIA Financial Network, a firm that follows advertising spending on local television stations, advertising revenue for the New York market will fall by about 16 percent for this year, twice the drop the firm was predicting before Sept. 11.
All the stations have found space on a tower in Alpine, N.J., or the Empire State Building, enabling viewers in some areas to receive some of the stations. But those sites offer poor solutions. The tower in Alpine is far too small to broadcast clear pictures throughout the metropolitan region, and the Empire State Building has too little space and electrical power to accommodate the demands of all the stations.
The long-term situation may be rectified only by the construction of an enormous stand-alone broadcast tower, perhaps as tall as 2,000 feet, preferably somewhere near the twin towers site. 
That solution is fraught with complications, including the cost, estimated at between $30 million and $40 million, to be divided among the stations. In addition, the location will be critical, since the new antennas could interfere with television signals in cities like Philadelphia and Boston, and will have to avoid established flight patterns for local airports.
The 10 local stations have formed a coalition to address the problems, and Mr. Baker, of Channel 13, was appointed its chairman. He described the dilemma the stations face as "a catastrophe that is not easily solvable."
He noted that about 20 percent of the 7.3 million homes in the New York metropolitan television market are not hooked up for cable or satellite service, a figure that becomes much larger if the total number of sets is included. Many homes have only some of their sets wired for cable and depend on the broadcast signal for other sets.
The collapse of the twin towers cost the stations more than their antennas. All the stations had also installed technical equipment for the coming switch to digital television signals. All of that was wiped out as well.
Mr. Baker, whose channel had started its digital service two months before the attacks, said that the price for the lost equipment alone surpassed $20 million. He said he hoped insurance would cover the loss.
Tom Kane, the president of WABC, Channel 7, said that his station's ratings had dropped more than 20 percent, and that advertising revenue was off by roughly the same amount. "We've tried to figure the economic value of our lost business for insurance, but we don't know how quickly viewers will return, so the damage will continue," he said.
Channel 7, which is owned by the Walt Disney Company, improved its reach this weekend with the help of a sister radio station, WPLJ. Mr. Kane said Channel 7 took over the space on the main antenna on the Empire State Building formerly used by WPLJ radio, which moved to a smaller antenna there. That allowed Channel 7 to reach a much wider area than it had reached from Alpine. The station instantly became much easier to view in Brooklyn, Queens and the Bronx, where few homes had been able to see it.
The main reason for the improvement is height. Broadcasters need a clear path to viewers' homes. The tower at the Alpine site is only 900 feet high. The Empire State Building site is about 1,300 feet high. (The trade center, with its antenna, was about 1,700 feet.)
Station executives said the Empire State Building management has been moving aggressively to try to offer the broadcasters what they need. "I think they have a shot at becoming the primary permanent location," Mr. Kane said. "But they know they have to make their case quickly, before an alternative site is selected."
No other tall New York building is a likely site, several engineers said, because none were configured for such use. The G.E. Building, which houses NBC, for example, might be a likely candidate, but it would need a rooftop antenna about 1,000 feet tall. The engineers said one so large was likely to collapse the building. 
The ideal permanent site, the station executives agreed, would be a stand-alone tower, ideally about 2,000 feet tall. That would be a structure considerably higher than the World Trade Center.
The best location for such a tower, many television executives agree, is Governors Island, which has both the land that would be required, and the proximity to the old site. But because of the hurdles involved in gaining acceptance for that site, which include environmental concerns and potential political conflicts, alternative sites are still being sought.
One other site widely mentioned is Fresh Kills, the former waste dump on Staten Island. That location would create far fewer complaints from either housing or environmental opponents, several station executives said.
But Mr. Baker raised the question of whether Fresh Kills, which is 14 miles south of the city center, would be approved by the Federal Communications Commission, which he said would insist on the signal not interfering with stations in Philadelphia, or the Federal Aviation Administration, which would have to ensure safety for flights from Newark International Airport.
At least one other senior station executive suggested that Fresh Kills should be taken more seriously than Governors Island because it would be easier to win approval from both federal agencies than all the constituencies that would raise objections to using Governors Island for the benefit of television stations.

HEADLINE: Many of the Newly Laid Off Head for Retraining 

BYLINE:  By JACQUES STEINBERG 

DATELINE: PITTSBURGH, Oct. 29 

 BODY:
One was a flight attendant who wanted to become a private investigator. Another, cradling his 2-year-old son in his arms, had been a baggage handler but wanted to learn how to repair heaters and air-conditioners. Yet another had known only one job since turning 20 -- he had been an airplane mechanic for 11 years -- but said he was hoping he could apply those same skills to fixing computers.
 There were two dozen in all. Each had been laid off by US Airways in the last five weeks, and each came here to an annex of the Community College of Allegheny County this morning to register for a job training program that will cram two years' worth of college-level courses into nine months. 
 The college designed the program to accommodate the workers, nearly 2,000 in all, who have lost their jobs at the US Airways hub here and who must now acquire skills in those few fields where "help wanted" signs remain prominently displayed.
Across the country, tens of thousands of workers who have lost airline and other jobs in recent months -- most of them lacking college degrees -- have begun similar retraining, intent on reinventing themselves as dietitians, welders, teachers' aides, nurses or even massage therapists. Some of those who are college graduates have begun taking the postgraduate courses required to become licensed teachers.
That such jobs exist suggests that some sectors of the economy, particularly those related to the care of an aging population, may well sop up many of these workers. But those switching careers will most likely have to adjust to lower salaries and new definitions of themselves.
"It's going to take time for me to be able to say I don't fix airplanes anymore," said Dave Dumas, 31, the would-be computer technician, who took the first steps today toward attaining his associate's degree. "I was just thinking how strange it's going to be to do homework and sit in a classroom. It's been a long time."
Though more than 100,000 people have been pared from the airlines' payrolls since the hijackings of Sept. 11, they are among only the most recent victims of an economy that has been slumping badly for a year. And many of the earlier victims have responded in much the same way. Thirty people who lost jobs in Silicon Valley earlier this year, for instance, have since enrolled at Trinity College in San Jose, Calif., where they are pursuing a less stressful line of work: they are studying to become massage therapists.
"I decided I no longer wanted to work with people who were frustrated over the operation of a machine," said one of the new massage students, Jill Brazda, 42, laid off by an Internet software company in June. "It makes me feel good that people will have a better day or won't get so upset on the freeway because they came in to get a massage from me."
Ms. Brazda worked in Silicon Valley for 10 years and earned nearly $80,000 last year. But with massage therapists in Northern California routinely charging $75 or more an hour -- and with 25 massages a week considered a reasonable workload -- Ms. Brazda estimated that her new income could well approach $100,000 annually, after she receives her license next year.
While teaching in an urban public school is far less lucrative, interest in such jobs has grown noticeably since the economy began flagging, said Barbara Radner, a professor of education at DePaul University in Chicago. Professor Radner leads a one-year, low-cost training program for college graduates seeking to enter teaching after working in other jobs. Those who complete the program must teach for at least five years in the Chicago schools.
Though applications are not due until January, Professor Radner said she had already received serious inquiries from a number of people who had lost their jobs since the events of Sept. 11. They include an account manager at an advertising agency, a real estate agent and an executive at United Airlines.
Those who are suddenly unemployed and do not have a college degree have often been surprised at the flexibility of community colleges. The program here at Allegheny -- which mirrors efforts by community colleges serving airline hubs in Arizona and North Carolina -- will begin on Nov. 19, two months into the fall semester and almost two months before the start of the spring semester.
Roy Flores, Allegheny's president, said he knew that the former US Airways employees did not have the luxury of waiting to begin their new careers. He said the college had worked with local employers to make sure the courses offered matched jobs known to be available.
Allegheny is charging about $4,000 for the nine-month associate's degree program, about what it would cost to take the same courses over two years. Most of those who enrolled today said that the tuition would be a burden but that they saw it as an investment, one they would finance using unemployment insurance, severance pay and, in some cases, retraining assistance from the federal government.
Many of the former airline employees were red-eyed. Marsyl String, 47, laid off after working as a customer service manager at US Airways nearly half her life, said she was having difficulty imagining doing anything else. She said she had finally decided she would learn how to become a designer of Web pages. 
Ms. String said she was especially grateful for the accelerated time line at Allegheny because her husband was also in jeopardy of losing his customer service job at US Airways.
"I'm his backup," she said. "He told me to hurry up and go to school and get a job. It's always been a risk to have our eggs in one basket."
Many who came to school here today said that working behind the scenes at an airport had fulfilled a childhood dream, and that they had never intended to leave until they retired. "It was exciting thinking that you were loading bags onto a plane that was off to Europe," said James Strain, 43, who recently lost his job as a baggage handler at Pittsburgh International Airport.
"You'd look up at the plane and see the kids in the window smiling at you," he said. "You'd wave and make their day, and they'd make yours."

HEADLINE: Investment Banks Pay Some Workers Not to Show Up 

BYLINE:  By SUZANNE KAPNER 

 BODY:
When Jamie Odell, a recent graduate of Princeton University, received the e-mail note from J. P. Morgan Chase, his prospective employer, he was relieved.
 "I'd heard so many horror stories from peers who had been laid off," said Mr. Odell, 24, who was supposed to start training as a financial analyst last month. 
 Instead of sending a pink slip, the firm was offering to pay Mr. Odell $20,000 upfront, roughly a fifth of his annual salary, if he would defer joining the firm for a year. As an added incentive, the company said it would pay $5,000 extra if he worked for a nonprofit organization. Otherwise he would be free to spend his time any way he liked, as long as he did not work for a competitor. 
Mr. Odell now teaches math and social studies to fifth and sixth graders at Our Lady Queen of Angels in Manhattan.
About a tenth of J. P. Morgan Chase's 600 college recruits in the United States have taken up the deferral offer. 
Voluntary deferrals, or flexible leave, as these programs are often called, have become a way for consulting firms and other businesses for which people are the main expense to reduce overhead in a time of economic uncertainty. But for the cutthroat business of investment banking, where job cuts often require employees to clear out their desks and leave the premises immediately, these programs are a novelty.
A handful of investment banks are experimenting with such programs for the first time this year. Merrill Lynch is offering employees who have been with the firm for at least two years the option of taking sabbaticals of six months to a year. Those who participate get a fifth of their salaries plus full benefits. Salomon Smith Barney, a unit of Citigroup, has given its recent college recruits the option of deferring their starting dates for a year on a similar basis.
Some banks have been forced to rescind offers outright. Credit Suisse First Boston, which said last month that it would cut 2,000 jobs, has had to tell some newly recruited college graduates, who had been set to join the firm this fall, that their positions no longer existed, a person close to the company said.
Deferral programs are intended to help companies react to a business cycle that has sped up in recent years. That pace can make it difficult when a firm like J. P. Morgan begins recruiting potential candidates as early as October, nine months before they will begin their training. Longer lead times became particularly problematic in the current downturn, when business declined more swiftly than during previous slumps, noted Martin Atkin, head of the junior resource management group at J. P. Morgan Chase. 
Such programs are not without their risks, said John Bowmer, chief executive of Adecco, a Swiss company that is one of the world's largest recruiters of temporary and permanent employees. "What happens if the economy doesn't come back next year?" he asked.
Although a 12-month sabbatical may seem like a dream come true for many, it can be a strain on the pocketbooks of recent graduates, especially those saddled with student loans.
In any case, many investment banks will still be forced to resort to layoffs for more senior staff members as they curtail expenses in what is proving to be a globally synchronized recession. And deferral programs mean these companies will have fewer spaces for 2002 college graduates. But the programs do help these banks save face.
"The cost isn't just dollars," Mr. Bowmer said. "It's the cost of a company's reputation."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Jamie Odell teaches math because his new employer, J. P. Morgan Chase, has deferred his starting date and given him a reduced salary. (Don Hogan Charles/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Rome Journal; 
Provocateur Is Back to 'Spit on' Detractors of U.S.

BYLINE:  By MELINDA HENNEBERGER 

DATELINE: ROME, Oct. 29 

 BODY:
Oriana Fallaci, the war correspondent who practically invented it's-all-about-me journalism some 30 years ago, had in effect become the Italian J. D. Salinger, refusing to give interviews and publishing not a word for the last decade.
 But now, at age 71 and sick with cancer, she has suddenly returned to her role as professional provocateur. In an expletive-rich indictment of Muslim immigrants and Italian ambivalence toward the United States that filled four full pages in the country's leading newspaper recently, she sent Italy's intelligentsia on a search of its soul. 
 Her essay, "The Rage and the Pride," began by announcing that reports that some Italians had celebrated the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks on America had so enraged her that she had decided to break "the self-imposed silence I have kept for 10 years to avoid mixing with cicadas." 
"They say: 'Good. Serves the Americans right.' ' she wrote. "And I am very, very, very cross. Cross with a cold, lucid, rational rage, that commands me to answer them and first of all to spit on them."
That was just a warm-up for the scorn she heaped on fellow Italians.
"My country, my Italy, is not the Italy of today," she wrote, "the pleasure-loving, vulgar Italy of people who think only about retiring before they are 50, the evil, stupid and cowardly Italy of the little hyenas who would send their daughter to a brothel in Beirut just to shake hands with a Hollywood star, but when the kamikaze of bin Laden reduce thousands of New Yorkers to mush, laugh and say it serves America right."
She was tougher still, though, on Arab immigrants, saying their arrival in Italy amounts to a colonization, "a secret invasion." Going well beyond Prime Minister Silvio Berlusconi's recent comments about the superiority of Western civilization, she wrote: "We might as well admit it. Our churches and cathedrals are more beautiful than their mosques."
Her tone and anti-immigrant theme were all but universally rejected, but the criticism of her native Italy as a country of divided loyalties, deeply conflicted about the American-led military action, is getting a serious hearing.
The discussion took on new life this week after Corriere della Sera, the Milan newspaper that published her original piece, ran an article with the headline, "One Italian Out of Four 'Justifies' bin Laden."
The article said one-quarter of all Italians could not only understand the reasons behind the Sept. 11 attacks but could also "justify" them -- although the actual polling data showed that 27 percent found the attacks completely wrong, 22 percent thought them mostly wrong and only 6 percent considered them mostly right.
Signs of mixed feelings abound here. Some graffiti say, "Siamo tutti americani," ("We are all Americans"), but other spray-painted messages declare, "Siamo tutti palestinesi." ("We are all Palestinians.")
In Italy, as in many other countries, condemnation of the terrorist attacks has been mixed at times with a sense that what happened to America amounted to some form of retribution for the country's dominance of the world's economy, culture, politics and military power.
As so often in the past, it appears -- at least to some -- that she has hit a nerve in a country where Mr. Berlusconi, a conservative, has already used strong anti-immigrant language that was welcomed by some Italians even as it has angered others.
Ms. Fallaci grew up poor in Florence and fought in the anti-Fascist resistance as a teenager. She always took sides in writing about wars from Vietnam to the Persian Gulf, and she became the best-known political interviewer of her generation.
She famously called Yasir Arafat "a man born to irritate," and described Henry A. Kissinger, whom she interviewed in 1972, this way: "Do you know that obsessive, hammering sound of rain falling on a roof? His voice is like that."
"Each interview," she once said, "is a portrait of myself, a strange mixture of my ideas, my temperament, my patience, all of these driving the questions."
In the latest article, she was not even vaguely sisterly toward Muslim women: "If in some countries women are so stupid as to accept wearing the chador, so idiotic as to marry a cretin who wants four wives, that's their problem. But to treat them with indulgence or tolerance or hope is suicide."
This report, from Manhattan, where she now spends most of her time, was as angry as anything she had ever written.
Yet most of the prominent writers and thinkers who answered her in print disagreed but did so carefully and with a respect that was almost as astonishing as her original article. 
The novelist Dacia Maraini wrote that "if you turn this into the first move of a holy war, you are helping him," meaning Osama bin Laden, adding, "It is a trap, Oriana, in which you seem to have fallen, spurred on by the impetuousness and the courage -- a little quixotic in this case -- that characterize you."
Giovanni Sartori, professor of humanities at Columbia University and professor emeritus at the University of Florence, concluded that while he did not completely agree with her, "Oriana Fallaci must be right, since her accusers are thoroughly wrong."
The leader of the leftist opposition, Francesco Rutelli, a former mayor of Rome, said: "I subscribe to a small part of her intervention. She's not a political leader, so she can write what she wants. And all her life is a life of literature, passion and personal commitment, so what she writes is important."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Oriana Fallaci in 1966. Her new diatribe against immigrants -- and Italians -- in Italy is getting a surprisingly respectful hearing. (Ugo Mulas); Oriana Fallaci, in chador, with Ayatollah Ruhollah Khomeini of Iran, second from left, in 1979. Her personal journalism always hit a nerve, as did a recent article that she published in Italy after 10 years of public silence.

HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs; 
Drilling for Tolerance

BYLINE:  By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN 

 BODY:
In April 1988 Saudi Arabia asked the U.S. to withdraw its newly appointed ambassador, Hume Horan, after only six months. News reports said King Fahd just didn't like the U.S. envoy. What the Saudis didn't like about him, though, was that he was the best Arabic speaker in the State Department, and had used his language skills to engage all kinds of Saudis, including the kingdom's conservative religious leaders who were critical of the ruling family. The Saudis didn't want someone so adroit at penetrating their society, so -- of course -- we withdrew Mr. Horan.
 Ever since then we've been sending non-Arabic-speaking ambassadors to Riyadh -- mostly presidential cronies who knew exactly how to penetrate the White House but didn't have a clue how to penetrate Saudi Arabia. Yes sir, we got the message: As long as the Saudis kept the oil flowing, what they taught in their schools and mosques was not our business. And what we didn't know wouldn't hurt us. 
 Well, on Sept. 11 we learned just how wrong that view was. What we didn't know hurt us very badly. On Sept. 11 we learned all the things about Saudi Arabia that we didn't know: that Saudi Arabia was the primary funder of the Taliban, that 15 of the hijackers were disgruntled young Saudis and that Saudi Arabia was allowing fund-raising for Osama bin Laden -- as long as he didn't use the money to attack the Saudi regime. 
And most of all, we've learned about Saudi schools. As this newspaper recently reported from Riyadh, the 10th-grade textbook for one of the five required religion classes taught in all Saudi public schools states: "It is compulsory for the Muslims to be loyal to each other and to consider the infidels their enemy." This hostile view of non-Muslims, which is particularly pronounced in the strict Saudi Wahhabi brand of Islam, is reinforced through Saudi sermons, TV shows and the Internet.
There is something wrong with this picture: Since Sept. 11, the president of the United States has given several speeches about how Islam is a tolerant religion, with no core hostility to the West. But the leader of Saudi Arabia, the keeper of the Muslim holy places, hasn't given one.
The truth is, there are at least two sides to Saudi Arabia, but we've pretended that there's only one. There is the wealthy Saudi ruling family and upper middle classes, who send their kids to America to be educated and live Western-style lives abroad and behind the veil at home. And there is an Islamist element incubating religious hostility toward America and the West, particularly among disaffected, unemployed Saudi youth. 
It is said that truth is the first victim of war. Not this war. In the war of Sept. 11, we've been the first victims of our own inability to tell the truth -- to ourselves and to others. It's time now to tell the truth. And the truth is that with the weapons of mass destruction that are now easily available, how governments shape the consciousness, mentality and imagination of their young people is no longer a private matter.
We now have two choices: First, we can decide that the Saudi ruling family really is tolerant, strong and wants to be part of the solution, and thus we can urge its members to educate their children differently and ensure that fund-raising in their society doesn't go to people who want to destroy ours. If so, I don't expect the Saudis to teach their kids to love America or embrace non-Muslim religions. 
But if countries want good relations with us, then they have to know that whatever religious vision they teach in their public schools we expect them to teach the "peaceful" realization of that vision. All U.S. ambassadors need to make that part of their brief. Because if tolerance is not made universal, then coexistence is impossible. But such simple tolerance of other faiths is precisely what Saudi Arabia has not been teaching.
If the Saudis cannot or will not do that, then we must conclude that the Saudi ruling family is not really on our side, and we should move quickly to lessen our dependence upon it. I was for radical energy conservation, getting rid of gas-guzzlers and reducing oil imports before Sept. 11 -- but I feel even more strongly about it now.
"Either we get rid of our minivans or Saudi Arabia gets rid of its textbooks," says Michael Mandelbaum, the Johns Hopkins foreign policy specialist. "But one thing we know for sure -- it's dangerous to go on assuming that the two can coexist." 

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH
