HEADLINE: Challenges Abound for New Chief at Beleaguered United Airlines 

BYLINE:  By LAURENCE ZUCKERMAN 

 BODY:
Labor leaders and investors gave John W. Creighton Jr., United Airlines' new chief executive, a firm clap on the back yesterday, but the honeymoon may not last long at United, the world's largest and most troubled employee-owned company. 
 Mr. Creighton, who met with United's senior officers yesterday and mingled with employees at O'Hare International Airport in Chicago, will presumably have to move quickly to stanch losses estimated at $20 million a day. He must also refashion a management team that has lost credibility with employees and manage the expectations of the labor unions that helped topple his predecessor. 
 The perils were immediately apparent. Although Frederick C. Dubinsky, chairman of the Air Line Pilots Association unit at United and a director of the parent UAL Corporation, strongly endorsed Mr. Creighton in an interview yesterday as "the right person for the job," he was cool to the idea of wage concessions. Many analysts think that such concessions are critical to turning United around.
"I don't think any employee at United Airlines intends to pay for the right to come to work," Captain Dubinsky said. "We are not indentured servants." 
Mr. Creighton, former chief executive of Weyerhaeuser and a UAL director since 1998, was named UAL's chairman and chief executive in a special session on Sunday after the board pressed James E. Goodwin to resign. 
Mr. Goodwin, since being named chief executive in 1999, had alienated the company's unions to the point where the board concluded he was not able to lead the company through its current financial crisis, the worst in its history. 
After the terrorist attacks last month, the airline industry has experienced its largest decline in passenger traffic. United, the second-largest carrier, eliminated 20,000 jobs and reduced its schedule by 26 percent. Still, it is hemorrhaging cash. The airline was already in trouble before Sept. 11. United lost $605 million in the first half of the year as the nation's economic slowdown led to a decline in business travel at a time costs were rising to pay for pilots' wage increases. Analysts expect UAL to lose about $600 million for the third quarter when it reports its earnings on Thursday. 
The economic effects of the terrorist attacks and the revolt against Mr. Goodwin sent UAL's stock price to a 15-year low on Friday, closing at $13.93. Yesterday, it ended trading at $14.15, up 22 cents. 
Mr. Creighton, who is 69, is credited with having cut costs and refocused Weyerhaeuser on its core forest products business in his time as chief executive there from 1991 to 1998. In 1994, he introduced a program that resulted in a $600 million increase in operating profit over three years. 
A Wall Street analyst praised him for being calm and methodical. But the analyst, who insisted on anonymity, also said that Mr. Creighton was cautious and wondered if his style would serve him well at United. 
"What was needed at Weyerhaeuser was not what is needed at United," the analyst said. "He could take three years. He didn't have any immediate issues that needed to be dealt with in three months." 
Mr. Creighton's strongest backer is the International Association of Machinists and Aerospace Workers. The union represents 40,000 United employees, owns about 20 percent of the company and, along with the pilots, has a seat on the UAL board. 
The machinists also represent about 7,000 Weyerhaeuser workers. R. Thomas Buffenbarger, the union's president, and Mr. Creighton serve together on the board of the Boy Scouts of America. It was Mr. Buffenbarger who recommended Mr. Creighton for his spot as a UAL director. 
"We give him high marks for credibility and integrity," Mr. Buffenbarger said yesterday. "He is a shrewd businessman. He has a faith in his employees. He will work with us. But he expects a full-fledged commitment from our side as well. We are not afraid of that, and that is what makes the relationship work."
But Denny Scott, an economist with the Western Council of Industrial Workers, which represented 3,000 Weyerhaeuser workers when Mr. Creighton was there, criticized him for demanding concessions and for fighting union-organizing campaigns. 
"The advice I would give workers at United is to be very guarded and to watch very closely," he said. 
United's unions are ready. The Association of Flight Attendants, which represents 26,000 United employees, wants to know what Mr. Creighton is going to do about Avolar, UAL's new business-jet subsidiary. The union says that Avolar's plans not to use its members as flight attendants violates its contract.
"His response will show how he differs from previous management in terms of working with employees as opposed to against us," a union spokesman, Jeff Zack, said.
The machinists union, which is fending off a representation challenge at United from a rival union, is sticking to a demand for an industry-leading wage increase like that received by the airline's pilots last year. Most analysts consider this unrealistic today.
All the major unions are looking for Mr. Creighton to shake up United's executive ranks. "I am scratching names off my Christmas card list," Captain Dubinsky said. He said United's entire labor relations team, which he called incompetent, should be fired.
Mr. Buffenbarger was also direct, calling for the dismissal of Andrew P. Studdert, United's chief operating officer. "Andy Studdert is probably the person most responsible for Jim Goodwin's inability to run this airline," he said. "He has no credibility with this union. Zero. Zilch. None. He has got to go." 
Yet investors and many United managers are looking for Mr. Creighton to rein in the airline's unions, which many blame for its problems. Some have said that Mr. Creighton can use the threat of a filing for bankruptcy protection, which would wipe out the unions' equity stake in the airline, as a stick. 
But Captain Dubinsky, who will be replaced at the union and on the UAL board by a new pilots' leader at the end of the year, said United's public shareholders had much more to lose than the pilots, who hold 25 percent of UAL stock.
"If we were to lose the equity at this point, given the value, who cares?" he said. "You still have to feed your family." 
He maintained that pilot pay is more important than the stock, which is tied up in retirement accounts and therefore cannot be traded. "Stock is long term," he said. "The grocery bill is short term." 
To many at the airline and on Wall Street, that summarizes the failure of United's employee ownership. Can Mr. Creighton turn that around? 
"Jim Goodwin never respected employee ownership, never understood it, never committed to it," said Christopher Mackin, president of Ownership Associates, a consultant in Cambridge, Mass., who has worked for United on the issue of employee ownership. "The question here is going to be how Creighton picks up the neglected opportunity of ownership that has laid fallow there since the early, honeymoon days."

HEADLINE: A NATION CHALLENGED: THE COMMUTERS; 
When the Attack Fallout Moves the Office to the Suburbs

BYLINE:  By RANDY KENNEDY 

 BODY:
Kristian Areche grew up in Washington Heights. He loves his neighborhood. But it does not make much sense for him to live there anymore.
 Before Sept. 11, it did. To get to work, he took the No. 1 train to the World Financial Center. The trip was about 10 miles. It took about half an hour and cost less than $20 a week. 
 Now, this is the way to work: a used Honda Civic. A 60-mile drive to his new office in South Brunswick, N.J. An hour and a half on the road, with luck. And $1,650 a year for insurance. He calls his car the beast, and he hates it. But what he and his wife hate more is the thought of moving. 
"We don't have anything against New Jersey," he said. "We just don't want to live there. We're New York City people."
In an overcrowded metropolitan region -- where people have often chosen their homes because of where their jobs are or their jobs because of where their homes are -- Kristian Areche and his wife are among those people now facing a set of tough choices about both life and work.
Mr. Areche, a page designer for The Wall Street Journal, is one of the workers displaced from the World Trade Center area who will not be coming back to Manhattan.
There may soon be many more like him. Former Manhattan employees of Citigroup and Deloitte & Touche might wind up in Connecticut. Corporate campuses from Jersey City to Princeton to Parsippany, N.J., might take workers from Merrill Lynch and American Express.
Officials have estimated that 100,000 jobs were displaced by the attacks. Many companies have not made final decisions on which employees will remain in satellite offices, and there are no statistics yet on how many have moved to the suburbs so far. But people involved in the financial services industry predict there could be tens of thousands who arranged their lives around commuting to Lower Manhattan who will never return there. Because many financial companies already had cheaper office space in the suburbs, it became easier for them after the twin towers attack to transfer some of their operations to those offices.
Many companies that have moved some of their employees from Manhattan would not let their workers discuss the relocation publicly, partly because the companies were afraid of being seen as abandoning Manhattan.
But many of the displaced workers now think of themselves as urban exiles, piecing together tough new trips from bus, ferry and train schedules. Some are doing the reverse commute, from city to suburb. Others are trying to make their way from suburb to suburb, along transit lines not intended to move people efficiently that way.
They are confronting questions they never imagined.
Some are wrenching: Do we pull up stakes and move to be closer to the new office? Do we pull our kids out of the school we worked so hard to get them into? Or do we try to rearrange our lives around a two-hour-plus trip to work?
Other questions seem trivial, in light of what has happened. But as people who made their careers in Manhattan try to write new formulas for enjoying their lives again, those questions are important, too. Such as: Do I really want to work in the suburbs?
Mr. Areche, 28, and his wife, Harriba, a bartender in NoHo, used to go out in the city on Fridays, the only work night she got off early. Now he spends Friday nights on the road.
He used to buy a paper on the way to the subway and read it on the train. But he can't read the paper and steer at the same time. He used to get a sandwich at his favorite deli near the World Financial Center. Now when he wants a bite, he usually drives to a strip-mall restaurant like T.G.I. Friday on Route 1.
"To go anywhere around here you have to drive," he said last week, in his car. "It's too far to walk. Where are you going to walk? Into the woods? To see a deer?"
Mr. Areche, one of about 250 workers at The Journal whose jobs have been transferred permanently to South Brunswick, takes pains to stress that his problems are paltry compared with those of people who lost loved ones or jobs as a result of the attacks.
Dow Jones, The Journal's corporate parent, has provided hotel rooms and other commuting assistance to soften the blow as he and other workers decide how they will deal with the transfers.
But many have already had enough of a glimpse of their new commuting trip and new office to know what it will mean for their lives.
One Dow Jones employee now working in South Brunswick had bought a house in Westchester just before the attack. Another lives on the Upper East Side and her new trip usually clocks in at more than two hours, door to door.
Paul Bernard, who runs an executive coaching and career management firm in Manhattan, said he had had many clients since the terror attacks who found themselves grappling with the rearranged geography of their lives. They were asking him whether they could find another job, back in the city or closer to home. Or they were thinking about moving closer to the new office.
But Mr. Bernard cautions them to be careful, because they may uproot themselves only to find that their company shrinks, they lose their job and they have moved for nothing.
The other option, finding a new job, and in the right location, is not so easy anymore.
"For a long time around here, with the economy so good, people have worked on the assumption that they can have everything," he said. "And that is not the case anymore. For many people, if they want their job, they may have to face the commuting problems that people face in London and in Los Angeles, where a two-hour drive is not uncommon."
Mr. Bernard described one Manhattan couple, who did not want to be identified, in which the husband is now working in New Jersey, with a long trip, and does not know if his financial-services job will return him to Manhattan. The wife is a successful lawyer. They just had their second child. Their lives -- already built on "thin margins of comfort" in being able to handle everything -- have become a scramble.
"They're both questioning how long they're going to be able to handle this," he said. He added that they had considered moving to Chicago, where they thought it would be easier to find a downtown job and afford a downtown lifestyle.
Glenda Williams, an associate senior director at the Consortium for Worker Education, a nonprofit organization that is helping train and place lower-income workers affected by the attacks, said she had also been hearing from workers with severe new commuting problems.
"They call in on our hot line," Ms. Williams said, "and they say, 'My company has moved to Jersey, way out there somewhere, and I don't have a car. What am I going to do? Or, 'They've moved to Long Island, and I told them I can't go out there and so they just laid me off.' It's a very hard time for a lot of people right now."
Mr. Areche thinks he can make it all work.
But he lasted only three days on New Jersey Transit trains before giving up in exasperation.
"It just took too long," he said. "You have to arrange your whole day around the train schedule." He rented a car. Then he finally "broke the piggy bank" and bought the first car he had owned since 1989.
His inexperience as a suburban commuter showed. The first car he bought, on eBay, turned out to be a lemon, and he had to tow it back to the seller using his rental car. His next purchase was the 1989 powder-blue Civic, with rusted-through fenders and 168,795 miles. It cost $600, plus $200 for a tuneup. The windows whistle when it gets past 50 miles per hour.
He considers himself lucky because his shift begins at 3 p.m. and ends at 10, so he misses the rush-hour traffic both ways. But his luck usually runs out when he gets back to the city. Last Wednesday, he spent 45 minutes looking for a parking place near his apartment.
"Everybody else is already home and parked by the time I'm looking for a spot," he said. "It wears you out."
He has considered renting an apartment near South Brunswick, where he could bunk some nights of the week. But his wife, who moved to the city six years ago from France, has said she might as well go back to France if she is never going to see her husband. Mr. Areche adds that if they have children, he does not know how they will be able to deal with his trip to work.
"We're just not sure what we're going to do," he said. "I love my job, I really do. And I love where I live. It's where I know everybody. We're really going to try to make it work."
Others are not quite so sure.
Ben Craft, who works on the copy desk at The Wall Street Journal, has an even more difficult trip, from Port Chester, N.Y., to South Brunswick. Most nights he stays in a New Jersey hotel because it is just too difficult to go back and forth: The trip, about 80 miles, using Metro-North, New Jersey Transit and a Dow Jones shuttle from the train station, usually takes more than three hours.
"I have a job, I didn't lose anybody, I'm not hurt, so I can't really complain," said Mr. Craft, 27, sitting at his new desk the other day. "But this is not the kind of experience I want to be having at this point in my life."
"It's just not the same getting out of a shuttle bus on a corporate campus in New Jersey as it was stepping out of the subway and walking past Wall Street," he added. "You felt like you were right in the middle of things. Now, you have this feeling that you've been, well, back-officed."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Kristian Areche bought a used car to get to his new office in South Brunswick, N.J., where he has worked since Sept. 11. "We don't have anything against New Jersey," he said. "We just don't want to live there." (Ozier Muhammad/The New York Times) Map of New Jersey outlining the old and new trips: Mr. Areche's work trip has gone from half an hour to an hour and a half.

HEADLINE: Campaign Finance 

 BODY:
To the Editor:
 I am astonished that while American special forces are hunting Osama bin Laden in Afghanistan and Congressional and Postal Service employees line up to be tested for anthrax, you continue to obsess about "campaign donors" (editorial, Oct. 25). It has become difficult to take seriously an editorial page that argues that in the wake of the attacks on our country, missile defense is less relevant while simultaneously arguing that campaign finance reform is "more important than ever" (editorial, Oct. 16). 
 With confidence in government at all-time highs, campaign finance reform is clearly a solution in search of a problem. Americans ranked it near the bottom of their list of priorities before Sept. 11; now it has appropriately dropped off the list completely as the president, Congress and the country are focusing on terrorism and the economy.

MITCH MCCONNELL
U.S. Senator from Kentucky
Washington, Oct. 26, 2001

HEADLINE: The Senate's Quaking Moderates 

 BODY:
As the Senate prepares to consider an economic stimulus package, the atmosphere is much different from earlier this year, when it passed President Bush's unaffordable and unfair tax cuts. Back then many moderate Republicans and Democrats voted for what they saw as a popular program to share the surplus with the taxpayers, despite their own quiet misgivings. Now there is a sense of crisis, demands for sacrifice, a realization that the federal surplus is gone and a determination to pass something that will actually boost the economy. Unfortunately, the stimulus package being put together by the Republican Senate leadership fails in every way to address the new circumstances. 
 The full package is to be unveiled today by Charles Grassley of Iowa, the ranking Republican on the Senate Finance Committee. The advance indications are that its centerpiece is an acceleration of all the top-bracket tax cuts that were to have been phased in by existing law. This is an almost shocking proposal, by some estimates transferring more than half the tax cut to the wealthiest 1 percent of taxpayers. Not a single American is unaware that the United States faces a long and potentially costly new era of spending on security needs at home and abroad. Cutting taxes for the wealthy in the next fiscal year does nothing to stimulate the economy now and undercuts the whole idea of equity in the tax system. 
 The business tax breaks being readied by the Finance Committee Republicans are in some respects not as bad as the ones passed in the House, whose stimulus package was so weighted down with corporate giveaways that the Republican leadership could barely shove it through. But the Grassley proposals would still not take effect in time to stimulate the economy.The business tax cuts would also have an adverse impact on state budgets. Many states base their own tax receipts on federal corporate tax rates. Dozens of states are facing deficits and cutbacks next year, and they cannot afford a Congressional tax cut that forces states to raise taxes or cut spending.
The Republican proposals are attempts to push the stimulus plans in the wrong direction. Moderates in both parties need to put together a package that does more than reward the campaign donor class. Many moderate Democrats are now aware that, in going with Mr. Bush's program earlier this year, they were hoodwinked because the package that finally passed was filled with gimmicks and tricks disguising its truly regressive identity. They, and the country, cannot afford to be fooled again. 

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: A NATION CHALLENGED: THE POLL; 
Survey Shows Doubts Stirring On Terror War

BYLINE:  By RICHARD L. BERKE and JANET ELDER 

 BODY:
Americans for the first time are raising doubts about whether the nation can accomplish its objectives in fighting terrorism at home and abroad, including capturing or killing Osama bin Laden, saving the international alliance from unraveling and protecting people from future attacks, the latest New York Times/CBS News poll shows.
 Despite threats about anthrax unfolding virtually every day and little discernible progress in the air campaign against the Taliban, Americans are still offering President Bush their overwhelming approval. 
 Mr. Bush's job approval rating, which soared after Sept. 11, stands firm at 87 percent. And Congress has an approval rating of 67 percent, the highest since the Times/CBS News Poll began asking about it in the 1970's. 
Even so, after six weeks in which people were not inclined to critique aspects of the government's response, there are stirrings of discontent that extend both to how the nation is responding to domestic terrorism and to how it is handling the war. 
The public is questioning whether the government is doing enough to forestall what it increasingly expects to be another terrorist attack in this country within months. Fifty-three percent say another attack is very likely, up from 46 percent two weeks ago and 36 percent two weeks before that. Most people say they expect the attack to be in the form of bioterrorism. These responses came before Attorney General John Ashcroft announced yesterday that new terrorist attacks were expected as soon as this week.
Yet more than half the public says the government in Washington has not done enough to prepare for a biological attack, and nor have state and local governments.
Nearly half of Americans say the government is withholding information they need to know about the recent anthrax cases. More than a quarter say public health officials are wrong in advising people not to ask their own doctors for Cipro, an antibiotic used in treating anthrax.
While security has been tightened at airports, leading to the now-common sight of long lines, Americans are still jumpy about flying. They want the federal government to take complete control of hiring and supervising all airport security personnel.
The nationwide telephone poll of 1,024 adults was conducted Thursday through Sunday. It has a margin of sampling error of plus or minus three percentage points.
Tom Cale, a poll respondent who sells cars in Fairmont, W.Va., is among those who support the nation's leaders but have nagging concerns. 
"It's not that we don't have competent people in positions of authority," Mr. Cale, 50, said in a follow-up interview. "They just haven't written the book yet about the potential dangers that are out there."
Bracing for more terror, Mr. Cale said: "The next attack would be what you least suspect. It's going to be something that few people would see that would affect the most people, like sabotaging gas supply lines, or taking out two or three main power stations."
Joan Kautz, 49, a clerk for a rental car agency in Linden, N.J., said: "With the added security at the airport, bioterrorism is the only way to get in here. That's why they've used the mail, and even now the government is not protecting our postal workers."
In one of the most striking shifts, only 18 percent of Americans said they had a great deal of confidence that the government could protect them from terrorism; a month ago, 35 percent had such confidence. A majority, 58 percent, said they had a fair amount of confidence. The rest had little or no confidence.
Similarly, people feel that the government can protect them from anthrax and smallpox, but the degree of confidence is another matter. Only 15 percent have a great deal of confidence that the government can protect them from anthrax; 19 percent say the same about smallpox.
Beyond the efforts at home, Americans are not entirely satisfied with the military action in Afghanistan and seem less hopeful than they were before the bombing began. Although most respondents said the war was going well for the United States, the largest proportion -- 58 percent -- said it was going only somewhat well. Twenty-five percent said it was going very well, and 13 percent said the war was going badly.
Only 28 percent are very confident that the United States will capture or kill Mr. bin Laden, who is believed to be the mastermind behind the Sept. 11 attacks. In a CBS News poll two weeks earlier, 38 percent said they were very confident.
"Osama bin Laden is like a ghost," said Eleanor Roth, 67, a retired receptionist from Dayton, Ohio. "I would rather him be captured than killed to avoid him being labeled a martyr. Maybe they will find him, but then again, many of these terrorists have been on the loose for years. And with all of our technology, it doesn't seem to make a difference."
James Oleszcsuk, 57, a longshoreman from Baltimore, was even less optimistic. 
"It won't be easy getting bin Laden," Mr. Oleszcsuk said. "First it was weeks, then months, now years to get this guy. It makes me wonder what kind of intelligence reports the government is getting. You hear so many things, you don't know what to believe. The political implications of capturing him would be problematic with many of the Middle Eastern countries. I don't think it's going to happen."
In another sign of mounting uneasiness about the war, only 29 percent said they were very confident in the ability of the United States government to maintain the international alliance of countries that support the military campaign; two weeks ago, 46 percent were very confident.
The public is prepared for a long and bloody conflict in Afghanistan; a majority of Americans say they are willing to accept the deaths of several thousand American troops there. Eight out of 10 respondents said they thought the conflict would extend beyond Afghanistan into neighboring countries and other parts of the world.
"I wouldn't be surprised if this war took three to five years," said Judy Adams, 48, a homemaker from Jonesville, La. "We have fought for our country for over 200 years to keep our land and our families safe. If we don't stand behind our president and pull together as a nation, we're not going to see our land stand."
Americans continue to view Israel favorably while they back the establishment of a Palestinian homeland in the occupied territories of the West Bank and Gaza Strip. About half the public views Saudi Arabia as a friend of the United States but not an ally.
There were signs of anxiety in the poll, perhaps because it was conducted at a time when developments have made people feel more vulnerable. In recent days, the Bush administration and leaders on Capitol Hill appeared to lack a coordinated message in responding to the anthrax threat.
Most Americans say they have been closely following the news about anthrax sent through the mail, and many have begun handling their own mail more cautiously.
The poll turned up mixed messages about the extent to which people are panicky. While they are concerned about whether the government can protect them, Americans are calm at home. Only a quarter say they are worried about terrorism in their own communities. While 20 percent of Americans say they are more on edge now than they were before the attacks on the World Trade Center and the Pentagon, few say they are having trouble sleeping or are experiencing a loss of income.
For all their misgivings about the government's capacity to protect them, the public remains steadfast behind its leaders. Most people say that Mr. Bush has clearly explained the goals of both the military action in Afghanistan and the war against terrorism in general.
Nearly 8 in 10 respondents approve of the way Mr. Bush is handling the war on terrorism; more than 6 in 10 approve his handling of the economy. Six in 10 Americans now say the country is moving in the right direction. Last June, well before the attacks, only 4 in 10 said the same thing.
The public's support for its leaders and government extends far beyond the White House. Over almost the last three decades, the job approval rating for Congress has never approached 67 percent, where it stands today.
The Watergate scandals first eroded the public's trust in government. For more than 27 years, people said they were more distrustful of government than trusting.
But in the aftermath of Sept. 11, people hold a different view. Now, more than half of Americans said they trusted the government to do what was right just about always or most of the time. In 1998, when the Times/CBS News Poll last asked the question, only 26 percent said they trusted the government.
Still, the poll found that the public was not prepared for a more activist government. Despite the high profile of many government agencies since Sept. 11, people still favored a smaller government with fewer services over a bigger government with more services, 52 percent to 43 percent.
Julie Hartfield, 22, a nursing assistant in Rochester, N.Y., said she did not know what to expect now.
"I feel like America was a little too sure of itself, thinking that no one could touch us," Ms. Hartfield said. "After the first anthrax outbreak, they should have made sure security was tight. Now there are outbreaks all over the place, and you wonder, 'What's next?' "

How the Poll Was Conducted
The latest New York Times/CBS News poll is based on telephone interviews conducted Thursday through Sunday with 1,024 adults throughout the United States.
The sample of telephone exchanges called was randomly selected by a computer from a complete list of more than 42,000 active residential exchanges across the country. Within each exchange, random digits were added to form a complete telephone number, thus permitting access to both listed and unlisted numbers. Within each household, one adult was designated by a random procedure to be the respondent for the survey.
The results have been weighted to take account of household size and number of telephone lines into the residence and to adjust for variations in the sample relating to geographic region, sex, race, age, marital status and education.
In theory, in 19 cases out of 20 the results based on such samples will differ by no more than three percentage points in either direction from what would have been obtained by seeking out all American adults.
For smaller subgroups the margin of sampling error is larger.
In addition to sampling error, the practical difficulties of conducting any survey of public opinion may introduce other sources of error into the poll. Variations in the wording and order of questions, for example, may lead to somewhat different results.

HEADLINE: TV SPORTS; 
Series vs. Jordan? Hair's the Winner

BYLINE:  By Richard Sandomir 

 BODY:
It is baseball's rotten luck to have tonight's Game 3 of the World Series compete against Michael Jordan's return in the baggy shorts of the Washington Wizards against the Knicks. Curiosity about Jordan may be enough for his game to seriously dent the viewership of Fox's broadcast of the pitching matchup of Roger Clemens, with his hamstrung hamstring, against Brian Anderson.
 For those conflicted over whether to watch Game 3 on Fox or Jordan on MSG (locally) and TBS (nationally, but blacked out in the New York market), here is some guidance to watching one, the other or both: 
 OWNERSHIP Look at it as a choice between the kindly old Wizards owner, Abe Pollin, a Yoda-like character who once told Jordan to buzz off and the blustery, turtlenecked George Steinbrenner, a "Seinfeld" character who once told Ken Clay to buzz off. Advantage: Jordan. 
THE TOUGHER SPORT Jordan was better at buying a nice bus for his minor league team than he was at playing baseball, evidence that baseball is tougher than basketball. If Jordan could only bat against Randy Johnson, this verdict would be reversed. Advantage: World Series.
LENGTH OF GAME The Wizards-Knicks game will tip off soon after 7:30 p.m., the Arizona-Yankees game at nearly 8:30, enough time to see nearly the entire first half before the first pitch. Jordan's return will end by 9:45, which should be the bottom of the second at Yankee Stadium. Advantage: Jordan.
THE HAIR FACTOR Knicks Coach Jeff Van Gundy has the oddest hair of all sports coaches and managers (winning in a romp over New Mexico State's basketball coach, Lou Henson), and he seems not to care; Yankees Manager Joe Torre can cover his stray strands along his hairline with a cap. Advantage: World Series.
SHAVED HEAD FACTOR Jordan versus Arizona's Matt Williams versus the Yankees' Don Zimmer. The look favors Jordan, in a way that makes you believe that Yul Brynner copied him. Williams looks cue ball pasty. Zim looks cute, but good luck/honeydew cute. Advantage: Jordan.
MEDIOCRITY VS. THE BEST Jordan, 38, is the best scorer on a bad team, which is like being Red Klotz on the Washington Generals. The Knicks are a grinding, enervating team. But the Yankees and Diamondbacks are league champions and won more than 90 games each. Be serious -- Derek Jeteror Jahidi White? Advantage: World Series.
THE ANNOUNCERS Locally, it is MSG's Marv Albert and Walt Frazier against Fox's Joe Buck and Tim McCarver. Buck is part of a two-person announcing dynasty, with his father, Jack. The Alberts have four voices, including Steve, who hosts the "Battle Dome" syndicated extreme sports warrior series. And while McCarver likes wordplay, he has never described anything as "hooping or scooping," although Curt Schilling's split-fingered fastball produced great rhyming temptation. Advantage: Even.
PREGAME BUZZ TBS/TNT's Charles Barkley over Fox's Kevin Kennedy. TBS and TNT's "Inside the N.B.A." (the TBS program tonight at 7 is not blacked out locally) is longer than Fox's pregame program and a lot more fun. Yesterday, Barkley said the preseason Jordan was as good as he was going to get. "Don't you think he has another notch to turn it up?" asked his partner, Kenny Smith. "No way," Barkley said. Expect a mirthful debate over whether the number of pounds Barkley will lose will match Jordan's scoring average. Advantage: Jordan.
ECONOMIC VALUE Jordan's career value to the economy was pegged at $10 billion by Fortune magazine several years ago, and now that he is back, he is expected to pull the economy out of the recession, find Osama bin Laden and make purple popular for reasons that exclude dinosaurs named Barney. The Yankees' new YES network is valued at nearly $900 million. Advantage: Jordan.
THE BUILDINGS Tough choice between Yankee Stadium, 78, and Madison Square Garden, 33. You can wax about the Krispy Kreme shop at Penn Station, but nothing at the Garden compares with the not-exactly-a-cemetery attraction that is Monument Park. At the Garden, you walk on its Walk of Fame plaques on the floor outside the lobby. Clemens communes with Babe Ruth's plaque; we can trample on Willis Reed's. Advantage: World Series.

Airwaves
Bob Wolff has created a sobering, must-watch three-part series for MSG's "SportsDesk" on the dangers of aluminum bats and the economic incentives for amateur baseball to use them. The first installment ran last night; the next two run tonight and Friday night at 10:30.

HEADLINE: TECHNOLOGY; 
A Pivotal Choice for Cingular Wireless

BYLINE:  By SIMON ROMERO 

 BODY:
Cingular Wireless, the nation's second-largest mobile phone carrier, intends to announce broad changes in its technology today, people close to the company said yesterday. The decision will benefit some of Europe's largest communications equipment companies at the expense of competitors in the United States and Canada.
 Cingular, which has 21.3 million customers, has decided to overlay a cellular communications technology called global system for mobile communications, or GSM, across its nationwide network, the people close to the company said. The decision is part of a shift to a technology that is widespread in Europe and Asia but that has only recently gained ground in the United States. 
 Cingular, which is second in the number of customers to Verizon Wireless, will award about $3 billion of equipment contracts to three European companies as part of the announcement. The companies -- Nokia of Finland, Ericsson of Sweden and Siemens of Germany -- will produce handsets and network gear for Cingular, a venture based in Atlanta that is 60 percent-owned by SBC Communications and the rest by BellSouth.
The move by Cingular is seen as a blow to companies like Lucent Technologies, of Murray Hill, N.J., and Nortel Networks of Canada, which compete with the European concerns for large equipment contracts. A Lucent spokeswoman said yesterday that a decision by Cingular to opt for GSM technology would not endanger the company's current contracts. Lucent had stopped developing equipment that could have been used in a Cingular GSM system.
Cingular's choice of GSM technology is also a blow to Qualcomm, which pioneered a competing technology called code division multiple access, or C.D.M.A.
Cingular opted for the European technology for several reasons. Because GSM is more common than other wireless standards, the company expects to take advantage of economies of scale and make cheaper purchases of handsets and network equipment.
Also, about 30 percent of Cingular's territory is already served by GSM technology, so the company will have to spend less to install a single technological standard across its network. The technology Cingular will be replacing is called time division multiple access, or T.D.M.A. 
In addition, GSM may make it easier for Cingular to share its network with those of other companies, or to eventually merge with a company in the United States or Europe. And much new wireless technology is based on the GSM system.
Cingular provided a glimpse into potential network-sharing agreements with a deal it made with VoiceStream Wireless, a unit of Deutsche Telekom, earlier this month. Under the deal, Cingular and VoiceStream agreed to share systems in New York, California and Nevada. 
The planned announcement in New York today by Cingular's chief executive, Stephen Carter, is also important for wireless customers in New York City and its suburbs, a region where the company did not have a notable presence until the VoiceStream deal. Cingular customers from other parts of the country will have an easier time using their service in New York because of the VoiceStream deal and the shift in technology. 
Cingular, which will immediately begin overlaying GSM technology on its network, will also announce plans today to adopt a new technology for its next-generation networks, which are expected to increase the Internet services available on mobile phones. This technology is known as EDGE, for enhanced data rate for global evolution.
Cingular follows AT&T Wireless Services and VoiceStream in opting for GSM technology. Among the largest wireless carriers in the United States, Verizon, Sprint PCS and Nextel Communications still operate with technologies that are more common in the United States than in other countries.
Another reason Cingular chose GSM over competing technologies may have to do with negotiations in Washington to transfer ownership of spectrum licenses from NextWave Telecom, a bankrupt wireless company, to Cingular and other large carriers. 
The companies are making progress toward reaching a settlement in which it would be easier for Cingular to overlay GSM technology across its network. If Cingular thought that it could not obtain additional airwaves from a settlement with NextWave, the company might have surprised the industry by opting for C.D.M.A. technology, analysts said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Stephen Carter, Cingular's chief, may also touch on Internet access from next-generation networks. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Honda Profit Rose Sharply in Last Quarter 

BYLINE:  By KEN BELSON 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Oct. 30 

 BODY:
Profits were up sharply at Honda Motor in the quarter ended Sept. 30 because of strong sales, cost reductions and a favorable trend in exchange rates, the company said today.
 Net income rose 45.2 percent from the comparable period in 2000, to 84.3 billion yen ($691 million), on a 16.1 percent rise in sales. 
In North America, where Honda sells more than half its cars, sales rose 7.3 percent, but the positive effect on profits was amplified by a 14 percent fall in the yen's value against the dollar over the first half of Honda's fiscal year, which began on April 1. 
 The company said that although the economy is "severe" and "unpredictable" in its main markets, the United States and Japan, it expects profits of 330 billion yen ($2.7 billion) for the fiscal year, 4.8 percent higher than it had first estimated. 
Analysts said Honda's heavy dependence on the American market makes it vulnerable as the United States economy slows down. Honda makes some of the cars it sells there at American plants, but it supplies others from Japan, and Honda's automobile exports from Japan fell 14.5 percent in the six months ended Sept. 30, the Japan Automobile Manufacturers Association said. 
Honda and other Japanese carmakers generally do better in tough times than their American rivals because many consumers view Japanese cars as cheaper to run and requiring less fuel and fewer repairs.
"The resilience of the Japanese automakers during the last 20 years should serve them well in the next 20 months," said Stephen Usher, an analyst at J. P. Morgan Securities in Tokyo.

HEADLINE: German Art Arrives In Its Bijou Museum; 
Lauder's Pet Project Nears Completion

BYLINE:  By CAROL VOGEL 

 BODY:
As workers scrambled one recent morning to put finishing touches on the Neue Galerie before its official opening on Nov. 16, Ronald S. Lauder arrived at the Louis XIII-style mansion, on 86th Street and Fifth Avenue, and headed straight to the director's office on the top floor. There, in a sunny space filled with furniture and objects by early 20th-century architects and designers like Otto Wagner and Josef Hoffmann, he zeroed in on a large wooden crate leaning against a wall. 
 "You can't open that yet," said Renee Price, the director of the new museum, moving to head him off, as if trying to stop a child bent on ripping open a Christmas present before the magic day. 
 Visibly disappointed, Mr. Lauder moved on, explaining that the crate contained the original table from Mies van der Rohe's Barcelona Pavilion. "To have a museum of German art with that table is quite incredible," he said. 
Mr. Lauder's new museum was born of the same tradition that gave rise to two of Manhattan's small gems, the Frick Collection and the Morgan Library. Like the industrialists Henry Clay Frick and J. P. Morgan, Mr. Lauder is blessed with deep pockets, a passion for collecting and a sure eye.
The Neue Galerie, devoted entirely to German and Austrian fine and decorative arts, is the result of an idea he hatched 10 years ago with another passionate collector, Serge Sabarsky, a dealer and collector of German and Austrian Expressionist art, who died in 1996.
Decisions about everything from the 600-page inaugural catalog to the typeface used for the wall labels have been overseen by Mr. Lauder, the chairman of Estee Lauder International, as well as of the Museum of Modern Art, and a former United States ambassador to Austria.
His interest in German and Austrian art dates to 1957 when as a 13-year-old he used money given to him for his bar mitzvah to buy an Egon Schiele drawing. "Later, when I began seriously collecting I couldn't afford Matisse or Picasso," Mr. Lauder said, "and I quickly realized that the works of Klimt, Schiele, Kandinsky and Klee were in the same league but were a lot cheaper."
Today his private collections span everything from rare Greek and Roman antiquities to contemporary art by masters like Jasper Johns and Robert Rauschenberg -- but the Neue Galerie is the culmination of his first art-world love.
Mr. Lauder, who spent $20 million to buy and renovate the Neue Galerie building, not including the millions he has put into art for the museum -- last May at Sotheby's he paid $22.5 million for a rare self-portrait by Max Beckmann -- said unapologetically, "It's a unique luxury."
The first exhibition, "New Worlds: German and Austrian Art, 1890-1940," will feature works from the museum's permanent collection, and loans from the Sabarsky collection and the Lauder family. It is to run through Feb. 18. 
At a time when many museums are struggling with slashed budgets and sagging attendance, the Neue Galerie is a tiny oasis sheltered from today's shaky economy and the impact of the World Trade Center attacks.
The name is a nod to its Central European heritage, inspired by Vienna's famous Neue Galerie, which was founded in 1923 and became one of the most important places to see the work of artists like Klimt, Schiele and others associated with the Secession, the turn-of-the-last-century revolt against academic art.
As the capital of a powerful European empire, 1900 Vienna was a place whose great outpouring of creativity and intellectual ferment contained the seeds of some of the 20th century's most important artistic and intellectual movements. 
Mr. Lauder first met Sabarsky in 1967 when he was just 23, when his brother, Leonard, took him to Sabarsky's Riverside Drive apartment to show him a drawing. "I still remember walking into the entrance and seeing 12 drawings and watercolors by Egon Schiele," Mr. Lauder recalled. "I spent the whole time transfixed. Seeing my reaction, Serge realized he'd met a person as crazy as himself."
The friendship, which lasted 30 years, was bonded by their ardor for the work of artists like Beckmann, Klimt and Kokoschka. "Starting this museum was our dream," he said. 
Two years before he died Mr. Sabarsky bought the six-story brick and limestone mansion on 86th Street designed by Carrere & Hastings, the architects who built the New York Public Library. The Sabarsky Foundation paid $9 million for the building, specifically to turn it into a museum, and later sold it to Mr. Lauder. 
While he provided the money to buy and renovate the building and establish a permanent collection for the museum, he does not plan to support it entirely out of his own pocket. The institution, like its neighbors the Guggenheim, the Metropolitan and the Whitney, is a nonprofit tax-exempt institution with an educational mandate and a board primarily made up of museum professionals from here and abroad. And like its larger neighbors, the Neue Galerie plans to apply for city and state financing to help pay for operations once it is up and running. 
As chairman of the Museum of Modern Art, Mr. Lauder has been one of the institution's biggest benefactors, responsible for some of its most significant acquisitions. When news of the Neue Galerie began circulating around the art world, some people wondered how it would affect his involvement with the Modern.
"Before I did anything I sat down with people from the Modern," he said. "The Modern's collection of German Expressionist art is superb. The two collections will work well together." His argument is that if the Modern had the Neue Galerie's collection it would have room to show only one or two pieces, even with its forthcoming expansion. "The collection would get lost there," he said, adding that the two museums plan to collaborate on many projects.
"We're a small, specialized institution," he said. "We're not trying to be the Modern."
To renovate the Neue Galerie building, Mr. Lauder hired Annabelle Selldorf, a German-born architect known for her sensitive approach to art-related projects. In addition to 4,300 square feet of exhibition space on the second and third floors, the building houses a bookstore, a design shop that will sell reproductions of objects by masters like Adolf Loos and Josef Hoffmann, and a Viennese cafe named for Mr. Sabarsky. 
Cafe Sabarsky was inspired by the great cafes where Viennese society, its artists and intellectuals met to exchange ideas on art, music, social theory and psychoanalysis. The cafe retains the mansion's original wood paneling and features a Hoffmann chandelier and reproduction bentwood furniture typical of the era. It will be run by Kurt Gutenbrunner, the owner and chef of the nouvelle Austrian restaurant Wallse in the West Village.
Revenue from the shops, restaurant and $10 admission charge is expected to offset some of the museum's operating costs, including salaries of its 18-person staff.
Both the cafe and bookstore will be open six days a week. The museum will be open only four. When the Neue Galerie opens it will have capacity to accommodate only about 350 visitors at a time, officials said. They project 150,000 to 200,000 visitors by the end of the first year.
So far the museum's collections consist of over 800 works from Sabarsky's Foundation, 500 from Mr. Lauder and his family and about 100 works purchased by the museum with funds provided by Mr. Lauder.
Among the three sources, the museum will have access to more than 100 paintings and works on paper by both Klimt and Schiele, making it the largest concentration of works by these artists outside Vienna. 
There are parallels between the fears and anxieties being felt today in the wake of the Sept. 11 attacks and those expressed by the artists on view at the Neue Galerie. "Some like Otto Dix, Max Beckmann Kokoschka and Kirchner went through World War I and never quite recovered," Ms. Price said. "The aftermath, the anxiety, is so prevalent in their work. Also, being labeled a degenerate artist was something many artists never overcame."
Speaking of the collection, Mr. Lauder said, "Most of these things have never been seen in public before." In addition to showing its permanent collection, which will rotate every six months, the museum plans to do two loan exhibitions a year that will travel to other institutions. A show of early Kokoschka portraits that opens in March 2002 will travel to the Hamburger Kunsthalle in Hamburg, Germany. 
Paintings, furniture and objects are shown together in context. In one room, for example, there are six paintings by Klimt, including two portraits of women, one of the Baroness Elisabeth Bachofen-Echt and the other an anonymous portrait called "The Dancer," one of the artist's last paintings. They hang alongside a painted beechwood table designed in 1904 by Koloman Moser, the Viennese Modernist painter and furniture designer. The work was executed by the Wiener Werkstatte, that group of artists and designers who produced everything from paintings to teaspoons in Vienna around 1900. In the same room are three clocks by Austrian architects -- Loos, Hans Prutscher and Joseph Urban. 
The second and third floors have been turned into exhibition spaces. The second floor is devoted to Austrian art, in particular the work of Klimt, Schiele and Kokoschka, along with objects by Loos and another Viennese architect, Otto Wagner. The third floor will feature early 20th-century German art by masters like Beckmann, Klee, Nolde and Dix along with decorative arts from Bauhaus architects and designers like Marcel Breuer and Mies van der Rohe. 
Mr. Lauder hopes the institution will become something of a hangout. On Sunday mornings he plans to hold small chamber music concerts similar to ones he heard while he was living in Vienna. "People can have a great breakfast at the cafe and then go upstairs and listen to chamber music," he said. 
Mr. Lauder says he believes there is a whole generation of young collectors with a terrific appetite for German and Austrian art. "They're discovering it for the same reason I did," he said. "There are still great pieces to be discovered, and there is still much to learn."

HEADLINE: Caught in Terror's Undertow 

BYLINE:  By ALAN COWELL 

DATELINE: DERBY, England, Oct. 27 

 BODY:
The day after the terror attacks in New York and Washington, Mick Lomax, a labor union official here, started waiting for what he knew -- and dreaded -- would be coming. 
 As the initial shock and disbelief subsided, he began to envision the chain of events to come: airline passengers would stop flying, airlines would cut back on schedules, jets would be mothballed, orders for new ones would be postponed or canceled. 
 And the end of the line would be here, Mr. Lomax calculated: thousands of miles from the Eastern Seaboard of the United States, jobs would be lost at the Rolls-Royce P.L.C. plant in this central English city, where more than one-third of the engines powering the world's civilian airliners are produced. 
Civic survival has been a struggle here, as the big companies that once ran the city -- the railways and Rolls-Royce -- have steadily pared back their presence. In the 30 years since Rolls-Royce, then near collapse, was bailed out by the British government, its work force has dwindled by more than half, to 13,000. The drop reflects increased automation, outsourcing and other modernizations. 
Mr. Lomax was kept waiting only six weeks before the other shoe -- a big new round of layoffs -- dropped on Derby. The news highlighted the perils of depending on manufacturing as the British economy is increasingly driven by services. It also sparked a debate about whether the events of Sept. 11 have been turned into a kind of all-purpose corporate camouflage for bad news.
On Oct. 18, Rolls-Royce P.L.C. -- long ago disconnected from the luxury car company that shares its brand name and is now owned by Volkswagen -- said that it expected a 30 percent drop in sales of commercial aircraft engines next year. That would cut by 50 percent its profits from civilian aerospace, the line of business that accounts for half the company's sales. 
The other half is faring better. The military aerospace unit, based in Bristol, expects to garner about $1 billion in extra business from the winning bid of Lockheed Martin to build the Joint Strike Fighter for the Pentagon. There are also marine and energy-equipment businesses.
But the woes in civilian aerospace, the company said, mean that an additional 11 percent of its global labor force will be let go. That means a loss of 5,000 jobs worldwide, 1,000 of them in the United States and almost 2,000 here in Derby, where Rolls-Royce's fortunes and the city's have become almost interchangeable.
"A majority of people depend on Royce's," said Iris Levy, 61, referring to the company the way local residents do. A cafe worker whose son-in-law and former husband work at the plant, Mrs. Levy said her son-in-law "has got the family and the mortgage, and it'll affect him badly" if he is one of those laid off. 
The company looms so large in Derby, said Anne-Marie Miller, a 30-year-old municipal surveyor, that she could count 50 or 60 friends and relatives who work there.
Derby once had a reputation as a major center for engineering and design, particularly for the state-run national railway system. But the railways were broken up and privatized in the mid-1990's, dissolving Derby's central role. Combined with the downsizing at Rolls-Royce, Ms. Miller said, "we are losing that historical value as an industrial base."
In many ways, that is the story of Britain as a whole, which was once an industrial powerhouse but now derives only about 18.5 percent of its economic output from manufacturing. The region around Derby had been bucking the trend, retaining some engineering jobs in the railway breakup and attracting more with the construction of a major Toyota auto assembly plant. The service industries created their jobs elsewhere while Derby kept its bias toward engineering and precision manufacturing -- and because of that, the city's unemployment rate, at just over 5 percent, is now one to two percentage points higher than the national average.
Rolls-Royce remains the biggest employer in Derby, a city of 258,000, with one in every eight workers on its payroll before the latest layoff announcement. "Rolls-Royce is Derby, and Derby is Rolls-Royce," said Mr. Lomax, the labor union official. "The two are synonymous."
Mark Slaviero of the Chamber of Commerce here puts Derby's reliance on manufacturing at 28 percent of all economic output, more than half again the national average. He said the latest Rolls-Royce cutbacks had been a shock. "It's put a depressing mood over the city," Mr. Slaviero said. "There's been restructuring over the past couple of years, but I don't think people expected this."
Perhaps they should have, Mr. Lomax said. The global economy was slowing before Sept. 11, he noted, and many industries, including airlines, were feeling the pinch. Now, he said, opportunists are taking macabre advantage of the cover provided by the terrorism attacks.
"We felt there had been a lot of companies around the world using it as an excuse for what they had been wanting to do for a long time," Mr. Lomax said.
Others are less cynical, at least about Rolls-Royce, which says the layoffs are motivated solely by the business slowdown. "I don't think that anyone thought the company had any other choice," said Robert Jones, the leader of the Derby City Council, which is dominated by the Labor Party. "You can't make airplane engines if people aren't buying airplanes."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Many Derby residents, like Iris Levy, have relatives who work at the Rolls-Royce plant. She is worried about the effects of lost jobs.; Mick Lomax, a union official in Derby, England, said recent layoffs at the Rolls-Royce factory would hurt the town. "Rolls-Royce is Derby, and Derby is Rolls-Royce," he said. "The two are synonymous." (Photographs by Mike Sheil/Black Star for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Confidence In Economy Is at Its Lowest Since 1994 

BYLINE:  By Reuters 

 BODY:
Consumer confidence plummeted in October to its lowest level in more than seven years, as a growing number of Americans lost their jobs and prospects for a quick economic recovery dimmed, a private business research group said yesterday.
 The group, the Conference Board of New York, said its index plunged for a fourth consecutive month in October, to 85.5, the lowest level since February 1994 compared with 97.0 in September. 
 While some other measures of confidence have pointed to stabilization since the Sept. 11 attacks, economists generally agree that sentiment is likely to deteriorate and will threaten consumer spending, which represents about two-thirds of overall economic activity. 
"Widespread layoffs and rising unemployment do not signal a rebound in confidence any time soon," Lynn Franco, director of the Conference Board's Consumer Research Center, said. "With the holiday season quickly approaching, there is little positive stimuli on the horizon." 
The index's drop in October was far worse than Wall Street expectations of a decline to 95.6 and touched off a sell-off in stocks and the dollar.
The present situation index, which measures consumers' views of the economy now, fell sharply, to 107.6, its lowest since January 1996, from 125.4 in September. The expectations index, which measures consumers' outlooks for the next six months, plunged to 70.8 in October from a revised 78.1 in September.
While the overall confidence index is far above recent troughs of below 50 in the recession of the early 1990's, Ms. Franco warned it was approaching the 80.0 "recession line." She also said that the sharp fall in the present situation index "is not comforting."
In a sign the jobless rate, now at 4.9 percent, is likely to rise, 20.7 percent of consumers surveyed said jobs were "hard to get," compared with 18.8 percent in September.
The Conference Board said consumers who rated current business conditions as "bad" rose to 20.6 percent in October from 18.3 percent in September. Americans who said conditions were "good" fell to 18.9 percent from 22.3 percent, while the percentage of consumers expecting their incomes to increase fell to 17.7 percent in October from 21.1 percent in September.
Fewer consumers said they planned to buy a home within six months, and they also scaled back plans to buy major appliances.
But more Americans said they intended to buy a car, in keeping with findings last week from the University of Michigan that showed many were taking advantage of zero-interest financing for new cars offered by the automakers.
The Conference Board's survey is based on a representative sample of 5,000 households across the United States that usually receives a response rate of about 70 percent.

HEADLINE: Smart Shopping by the Pentagon 

 BODY:
The Pentagon's decision to buy a new generation of stealthy fighters from Lockheed Martin can be a good deal for the military and taxpayers alike, even though total costs could run to $200 billion over the next few decades. Economies of scale and design are concepts not usually associated with mammoth Pentagon purchases. The joint strike fighter program, intended to produce more than 3,000 planes at the relatively modest price of $38 million each, may turn out to be a happy exception. 
 The Pentagon must make sure that the losing bidder, the Boeing Company, remains a viable producer of military aircraft. It must also hedge itscommitment to the new fighter, which will also be known as the F-35, by developing less vulnerable unpiloted drones to drop bombs on enemy positions. 
 The concept of a joint strike fighter, usable by the Air Force, Navy and Marines, allowing longer production lines and lower costs, dates back to the Clinton administration and has been supported by Pentagon officials from both parties. The final design incorporates much of the advanced stealth technology developed for the Air Force's F-22 fighter at less than half that program's cost of $83 million a plane. In addition to its multiple roles at home, the F-35 will also be used by Britain's navy and air force, and perhaps by other NATO countries as well. That expanded market will further economize on costs. 
Competition between Lockheed and Boeing helped hold down costs on the F-35. That can happen in the future only if Boeing survives as a military aircraft manufacturer. At least for the short term, that seems assured. Boeing is a big and diversified company, despite the current slump in its commercial airliner orders, and has plenty of other Pentagon business including substantial shares of the F-18 and F-22 fighter programs. 
Had Lockheed lost the F-35, it might have been forced out of the defense business. That would have been costly. Years of consolidation in the defense industry, encouraged by the Pentagon, have left only two major military aircraft makers, Lockheed and Boeing. Further shrinkage would end design and cost competition, which could bloat future defense budgets and damage military effectiveness.

HEADLINE: Indian Chief Miffs Canada, Not Sparing Indians 

BYLINE:  By BARBARA CROSSETTE 

DATELINE: OTTAWA, Oct. 20 

 BODY:
One year into his term as Canada's paramount Indian chief, Matthew Coon Come is catching trouble everywhere.
 Fellow Indians are unnerved by his born-again Christianity. Separatists in his native Quebec Province have been stung by his demand for the right to secede from them if they persist in trying to leave Canada. This summer at a United Nations conference on racism and discrimination in South Africa, he outraged many Canadians as he denounced the country's human rights record for all the world to see. 
 "I was only quoting U.N. reports and Canadian Human Rights Commission reports," he said, sitting at his boardroom table in his conservative business suit, his feathered headdress hanging from a coat tree by his desk.
Mr. Coon Come, 45, a Cree Indian born as a dog sled bearing his mother raced to a tent on his father's trap line, was elected to a three-year term as leader of the Assembly of First Nations, Canada's indigenous parliament, by the chiefs of 633 Indian tribes, or "bands" as they are known here. The chiefs knew what they were getting. 
Since the age of 16, when he read in a newspaper about a threat to the region of his birth, he has been a tenacious agitator for Indian rights, leading a long and successful campaign to stop a $13 billion Quebec hydroelectric project that eventually acquired international dimensions. 
"I saw in a paper that reservoirs were going to be created in Quebec," he said. "One of those reservoirs was my community. I picked up that map and said, 'Our home is going to be under water.' When you have walked on the land, and you know what damage a beaver can do, you just magnify that and you realize the flooding that's going to take place." 
Because New York State was the intended buyer of the electricity, Mr. Coon Come asked Eskimo and Cree boat builders to make him a symbolic craft -- part kayak, part ode, a Cree canoe -- and he sailed it down the inland waterways to Albany. 
Earlier, while still a student, he had organized a demonstration in Ottawa against the project. His girlfriend and now his wife, Mary Ann Matoush, also an Indian, got clubbed to the ground by the Royal Canadian Mounted Police. As he dragged her to safety, he recalls asking himself, "Where do we fit in this picture?"
That fitting in has never been easy, as even his name testifies. Coon means "snow" in his language, and Come was attached arbitrarily and inexplicably by a non-Indian census taker. 
A boy growing up with slingshots and a bow and arrow for toys, Mr. Coon Come was sent away to a boarding school at 6. Such schools have since been closed, after years in which they contributed to the loss of Indian culture, and the sexual abuse of children. 
He later graduated from Trent University in Ontario and had begun to study law at McGill in Montreal, when he was asked to go back to his James Bay Cree community as a deputy chief. Eventually, he became chief of that group and then of all the Cree, Canada's largest tribe.
Mr. Coon Come said that his priority as national chief of Canada's indigenous people was to get the government to honor 200 years of treaty commitments, giving Indians more land and autonomy. 
He opposes increased government oversight, now being discussed by political leaders, and would do away with the Department of Indian Affairs. He wants to work with the government as equals.
"The colonialistic attitude: we know what's best for you -- gone are those days that belong in the garbage can where slavery is," he said.
"I believe we can be participants in the economy, but we do need capital to put the infrastructure in place -- communications, connectivity, electricity, roads, marinas, airstrips, water and sewage lines," he said. 
Geographic isolation of Indian and Inuit, or Eskimo, people, most of whom now live all across the country's far north -- and call the rest of Canada "down south" -- has made economic development challenging. 
But that same isolation has allowed Canada's 660,000 Indians to keep languages and cultures intact better than Indians in the United States have done, Mr. Coon Come said. 
Indians also take better care of their environment, he said. Integrated into economic decision-making, they would insure not "sustainable development" on their lands, but "sustainable creation," he said. 
But he has also been quick to acknowledge the problems in Indian communities -- "bad apples" mismanaging government funds, suicide, diabetes, alcoholism and jails full of indigenous people. 
Not everyone in the communities he represents have been happy about it. 
"I made a reference to fetal alcohol syndrome," he said. "There are alarming statistics. My comment was that some people drink and smoke too much. I was attacked for that -- right across Canada. They called for my resignation."
"I didn't say you couldn't smoke or you couldn't drink," he said. "To me that's a choice. At the end of the day, it's a choice whether you take that drink or not. It's not the devil that makes you do it."
Such strong conviction stems in part from his beliefs as an evangelical Christian, a conversion that has led to problems both within the communities he represents as well as his own family, traditional practitioners of shamanism. "You have to understand, I was the only son," he said. "It wasn't easy."
His open profession of faith was an issue when he ran for national chief last year. "They said, 'You will have no respect for our traditions and culture and beliefs,' " he said. He told them he was a hunter whose spirit had never left the land and who respected all beliefs. The issue faded. 
Indeed, both Mr. Coon Come and his wife -- they have five children -- were both taught by their parents how to live in the wilderness. Until moving to Ottawa last year, they were comfortable in moosehide coats and snowshoes.
"I can take off these clothes you see now, and I'd be able to survive out on the land," he said. "My father taught me all the skills that I need."
The lessons go on. His father ventured down to Ottawa last week to see his son's new city home. Awake early, the father went for a walk on the golf course behind the house. When he returned, he wanted to know why his son was not out hunting. "He said, 'There are a lot of geese here,' " Mr. Coon Come said. " 'You could feed your family.' "
"My father is a hunter," Mr. Coon Come said. "Never went to school. Supported himself by living off the land. To him, if there's game out there, how come you're not out hunting?"

GRAPHIC: Photo: Matthew Coon Come, Canada's paramount Indian chief, at the Parliament in Ottawa. He says his priority is to get the government to honor 200 years of treaty commitments, giving Indians more land and autonomy. (Robert J. Galbraith for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS; 
Stocks Tumble, Spurred by Dive in Consumer Confidence

BYLINE:  By SHERRI DAY 

 BODY:
Stocks again fell sharply yesterday as stunned investors responded to a report that consumer confidence in October fell to its lowest level in seven years.
 The Dow Jones industrial average fell 147.52 points, or 1.6 percent, to close at 9,121.98. The technology-laden Nasdaq composite index dropped 32.11 points, or 1.9 percent, to 1,667.41. The broader Standard & Poor's 500-stock index lost 18.51 points, or 1.7 percent, to 1,059.79. 
 Investors continued to book profits made in the market's recent upswing. Stocks declined at the onset of trading yesterday, but quickened their descent after the consumer confidence numbers were released. The report showed a closely watched index of confidence plunged 11.5 points in October, far more than the 1.4-point slide Wall Street expected. 
The index measures the sentiment of consumers, who for a long time underpinned the deteriorating American economy. The survey, of 5,000 households in regions across the country, could be the first in a series of dismal economic reports coming this week.
"This is a very worrisome report, and what's worse is that by Friday morning it could be old news," said Oscar Gonzalez, an economist at John Hancock Financial Services. "If, as expected, the October employment report shows a sharp rise in unemployment and a sharp decline in job growth, it could be a crushing blow to confidence. Having a job is probably the single biggest factor in consumers having confidence."
Investor sentiment was also clouded by worries about the stability of Argentina's economy, new cases of anthrax, the American military airstrikes in Afghanistan and the possibility of additional terrorist attacks.
"Last week, there was a bit of ebullience building that we were maybe cruising to an easier-than-expected victory in Afghanistan, and that there had been no more terrorist attacks in the U.S. and this anthrax thing -- though a tragedy -- seemed to be fairly limited," said Steve Massocca, president and head of trading at Pacific Growth Equities. "It seemed like the economy was starting to perk up and things were starting to turn around.
"Then, I think it became clear that the victory wasn't going to be so easy in Afghanistan; economic indicators started to come out and they were pointing to further depreciation. All of that sort of weighed on the market."
On Monday, the stock market gave back a good part of its gains from recent rallies, with the major indexes falling 2.4 percent or more. 
Though yesterday's decline was broad, crossing into all sectors, technology issues led the way down. There was little news to cause a sell-off in the sector, but because technology stocks are normally volatile they often lead the market when big moves take place, analysts said.
Among technology issues, Internet stocks were particularly hard hit. Juniper Networks fell $1.01, to $22.99; CheckPoint Software lost 87 cents, to $28.67; Amazon dropped 63 cents, to $6.42, and CNet declined 37 cents, to $4.45.
TheStreet.com's Internet stock index dropped 6.37 points, or 4.19 percent, to 145.51.
For a second day, semiconductor stocks continued to be weak. Intel dropped 64 cents, to $23.54; Flextronics dropped 72 cents, to $18.19; Applied Materials slid $1.26, to $33.25; Xilinx lost $1.63, to $28.38; and Maxim Integrated Products fell $2.13, to $43.16. 
The Philadelphia Stock Exchange's semiconductor stock index dropped 17.53 points, or 3.92 percent, to 429.68. --------------------

Treasury Prices Advance
By Bloomberg News
Treasury bond prices rose yesterday after a report showed consumer confidence plunging on the threat of rising job losses and of more terrorist attacks.
"Everywhere people look, there's bad news: The economy is shrinking, the budget is deteriorating and there might be other terrorist attacks," said John Poole, who manages $10 billion at Mellon Private Asset Management in Boston. "In such an environment, they will stick with Treasuries."
The 10-year Treasury note rose 19/32, to a price of 104 22/32. The note's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, fell to 4.41 percent from 4.48 percent on Monday.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond rose 28/32, to 102 19/32. The bond's yield fell to 5.20 percent from 5.26 percent on Monday.

HEADLINE: City Is Still Standing, but the Tours Aren't Walking Much 

BYLINE:  By DAVID W. CHEN 

 BODY:
Since the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks, Big Onion Walking Tours has not had a school group show up for a previously scheduled tour of a part of New York. No Central Park? No Chinatown? Not even the multiethnic eating tour? No thanks, the groups have said; we're all afraid.
 But yesterday, Seth Kamil, Big Onion's founder, reported that finally, he found some takers with a little less fear: some cadets from West Point, who took a tour of the Lower East Side. 
 "The tragedy happened, but you have to go on, sir," said Janaia Nash, a 20-year-old sociology major from North Carolina. "Staying away is not going to make the situation go away, sir." 
It was a tour that Big Onion Walking Tours usually conducts dozens of times each fall, all over the city, for school groups from all over the country. But it was the first one that Big Onion had done since Sept. 11.
In the aftermath of the terrorist attack, the economic woes of New York businesses that feed, entertain, house and generally cater to visitors have been well documented. But the ripple effect has also spread to the tour guides who depend heavily on traffic from out-of-town schools.
In a normal autumn, Mr. Kamil's company usually organizes roughly 150 field trips for schools from all over the country. This fall, Mr. Kamil has two; yesterday's, and another on Nov. 13 for a Quaker school on Long Island.
Even school field trips scheduled for spring have been canceled, too. One school cited recent reports of anthrax in the city as the basis for canceling a trip to Central Park, he said.
"We've lost much of our business," Mr. Kamil said. "Part of the problem is the belief that the Lower East Side is downtown, so people are like, 'Oh my God, it's downtown, it's dangerous.' "
It does not matter where the schools are from, either. Joyce Gold, owner of Joyce Gold History Tours of New York, says that one company that organizes tours for more than a dozen schools from Florida each winter is poised to cancel. She has also had cancellations from two private schools, one in Manhattan, the other in Staten Island.
Yesterday morning, Mr. Kamil said he had received word that a group of perhaps 50 or 60 students from Forest Hills High School scheduled to tour Lower Manhattan on Election Day had canceled, too.
"They were concerned about trips outside of the borough of Queens," said Arlene Kahn, an administrator at La Guardia Community College who helps run a program at the school. "Their feeling was that they wanted to be cautious."
So it was with some relief on Mr. Kamil's part, and some pride on the part of the cadets, that there even was a field trip yesterday. Led by Bruce Keith, an associate professor of sociology, 9 male and 10 female students, mostly junior and senior sociology majors who are taking a class called Social Inequality, made the hour-plus bus ride from West Point to Lower Manhattan.
Not one cadet backed out at the last minute or had any worries about personal safety. If anything, the students said, they had an even stronger desire to visit the city and show that life could and should continue as planned.
"I refuse to live in fear," said Luke Haverstick, a strapping 21-year-old from Algona, Iowa.
The tour started, as usual, with a trip to the Tenement Museum on Orchard Street. Then, the tour veered in a generally southerly direction past what Mr. Kamil described as the new Chinatown, where recent immigrants from Fujian and other provinces live in cramped quarters. Next, the group went past the familiar places in historic Chinatown, like the Transfiguration School and Mott Street. Finally, the tour concluded with a stroll down Mulberry Street in Little Italy.
Mr. Kamil was, as usual, witty, informative and encyclopedic in evoking a historical portrait of a city so influenced by immigration and religion. He did not skirt the issue of Sept. 11, either, noting the anomalous quiet of the streets in tourist-dependent stretches of Chinatown and Little Italy.
The cadets did not disagree. But still, they said, they relished the chance to visit New York, even during a time of heightened security. After all, West Point has been under a tight seal, too.
"I'd be very upset if they canceled," said Sara Ewing, a 24-year-old senior from Vassar, Mich. "They don't let us out of school very often."

GRAPHIC: Photo: West Point cadets taking a tour of the Lower East Side yesterday led by Big Onion Walking Tours, which has seen its business drop off. (Frances Roberts for The New York Times)
