HEADLINE: Peres Says He May See Arafat, Drawing Criticism From Right 

BYLINE:  By JAMES BENNET 

DATELINE: JERUSALEM, Oct. 30 

 BODY:
Foreign Minister Shimon Peres said today that he would probably meet this week with Yasir Arafat, and that he had drafted a new peace plan for the Middle East.
 Mr. Peres said that the meeting with Mr. Arafat, the Palestinian leader, would not be substantive, and that he would present his plan to Prime Minister Ariel Sharon before making its details public. But both developments are likely to deepen the divide between left and right in Israel's coalition government. 
 During the present impasse between Israelis and Palestinians, a face-to-face encounter with Mr. Arafat would carry symbolic weight. It would be the highest-level contact in more than a month.
Mr. Peres said he was likely to see Mr. Arafat during a conference on Middle Eastern economies to be held on Friday and Saturday on the Spanish island of Majorca.
"We shall probably meet, but we are not going to negotiate," Mr. Peres said, "because I think that negotiations should be prepared very carefully, otherwise it will create a disappointment instead of a hope."
On television tonight, Mr. Peres said that he favored moving immediately to create a Palestinian state and that he had drafted a peace plan. Mr. Sharon has developed his own plan, Mr. Peres said, so he thought it was important for his Labor Party to present an alternative. He insisted that the unity government was "not speaking with two tongues."
The newspaper Maariv reported today that Mr. Peres's plan called for Israel to dismantle its settlements in the Gaza Strip and to permit the founding of a Palestinian state "for political and not military purposes."
Other Labor leaders have proposed "unilateral disengagement" by Israel from the West Bank and Gaza, leaving the areas to the supervision of an international coalition.
Right-wing cabinet members have repeatedly rejected any suggestion of a meeting with Mr. Arafat.
Advisers to Mr. Sharon seemed surprised by the news. Ranaan Gissin, a spokesman for Mr. Sharon, said any such decision must be coordinated between the offices of the foreign minister and the prime minister. 
Mr. Gissin said Mr. Sharon would consider permitting a meeting if it would help "in any way to bring about a cessation of hostilities." But, he added, "I think a photo opportunity, even Peres would not go for."
The exchange had a familiar ring. Through most of September, Mr. Peres and Mr. Arafat planned a meeting that was on and off like a strobe light. Dates were broken as Mr. Sharon insisted on an absolute cessation of violence beforehand.
Under American pressure, Mr. Sharon relented, and the two men met in the Gaza Strip on Sept. 26 to recommit their two sides to a declared cease-fire. Since then, violence has surged in Israel, the West Bank and Gaza.
After the killing on Oct. 17 of Tourism Minister Rehavam Zeevi, Mr. Sharon ordered Mr. Arafat to arrest and turn over those responsible or prepare to be treated as a sponsor of terrorism. Mr. Arafat refused to extradite any suspects, and the Israeli Army seized positions in seven cities in Palestinian-controlled areas of the West Bank. Israeli soldiers remain in four of those cities.
The possible meeting comes as some ministers within Mr. Sharon's government have been urging him to banish Mr. Arafat or to undermine his legitimacy as an international figure. Finance Minister Silvan Shalom told Israeli television today that a meeting would signal to world leaders that it was proper to sit down with Mr. Arafat.
"This man generates more terror attacks than the Hamas and Islamic Jihad," Mr. Shalom said. "There is no room for this meeting, nothing will come out of it and, frankly, I do not understand the crazy chase after him every single time. It only hurts us eventually."
Israeli leaders are most concerned about the possibility of a meeting between President Bush and Mr. Arafat. Other leaders have shown little reluctance to sit down with the Palestinian leader. Today he met separately in Rome with Pope John Paul II and the Italian prime minister, Silvio Berlusconi.
Relations between the United States and Israel have been strained by the Israeli incursions. Today the State Department criticized Israel for the demolition in East Jerusalem of four Palestinian houses. The State Department spokesman, Richard A. Boucher, called the demolitions "highly provocative."
Israeli officials said the owners lacked permits. Palestinians have long accused the Israelis of denying them such permits to push them out of East Jerusalem, which they envision as the capital of their state.

HEADLINE: Few Businesses Seek Disaster Aid; Fewer Are Approved 

BYLINE:  By EDWARD WYATT 

 BODY:
More than half of the small businesses in New York City that have applied for federal disaster loans since the World Trade Center attack have had their applications rejected, resulting in one of the lowest loan-approval rates in recent years among communities that have had to grapple with large-scale disasters.
 At the same time, despite a widespread downturn among small businesses below 14th Street and elsewhere, relatively few business owners have applied for disaster relief, with some of them expressing doubt about the economic climate. 
 As a result, the United States Small Business Administration is all but begging for applicants. While those businesses that are applying are getting their answers in a few days, compared to the normal three-week process, some downtown business owners say their experiences have left them confused and dejected about their prospects. 
Only seven weeks after the disaster, it is too early to say whether the approval rate will continue at its current low level or, as Small Business Administration officials predict, the rate will rise as generally healthier businesses are drawn to apply for help. But in either case, an ominous picture emerges of the state of small business in the city: a major downturn, plus the disaster at the trade center, affecting the a large number businesses and employers at what is, for many, their most important season of the year.
"There is a large number of businesses out there with major decisions to make," said William Leggiero, the S.B.A's director of operations for the region that stretches from Maine through Virginia. "Many of them are deciding whether to relocate or to where. These are difficult decisions, not like we see with most physical disasters, where businesses know they are going back into business and are going to stay right where they are."
Business owners give varied reasons for being wary of applying for the disaster loans, not the least of which are their own serious doubts about business prospects in the immediate neighborhood of the destroyed trade center. In addition, they say, applications require extensive documentation, and many businesses are waiting to see what costs will be covered by their own insurance before taking on extra debt.
Through yesterday, the Small Business Administration had approved 844 applications totaling $80 million in disaster assistance to businesses or homeowners in New York City, Mr. Leggiero said. 
But the agency had rejected 976 applications, for an approval rate of 46 percent. More than 500 additional applications were completed but withdrawn, in many cases after the applicants were advised that they did not meet some of the basic requirements for approval. Roughly 1,500 applications were still being processed.
S.B.A. officials said that nationwide approval rates run at about 50 percent. But following several large disasters over the last decade, approval rates were significantly higher. In 1992, in the aftermath of Hurricane Andrew, 60 percent of Florida's disaster loan applications were approved, as were 56 percent of the applications that followed the Northridge earthquake near Los Angeles in 1994.
The trade center attack differs from recent hurricanes and floods, which have affected more homeowners than business owners. Homeowners and renters, who can also apply for S.B.A. loans to repair property damage, sometimes account for four out of every five loan requests. 
But in downtown Manhattan, homeowners account for only about 10 percent of the loans approved so far in New York City.
In addition, the businesses that do apply quickly are often those that are in the most dire straits, and therefore might not be the most creditworthy applicants.
Because the application period extends through June, S.B.A. officials said they expected that the approval rate would rise as more business owners who are surviving but still feeling pinched seek assistance.
Some small business owners say they feel they were unfairly denied assistance. Nancy Chambers, the owner of Urban Bird, a parrot breeding and sales business on Greenwich Avenue in Greenwich Village, said that her loan application was denied because of a dispute with state tax authorities over how long she had been in business.
"Like a lot of small-business people, I don't have sterling credit," Ms. Chambers said. "But I had to keep the store open, the birds alive and my life going." After her federal application was denied, she found help from Accion New York, a microlending organization that arranged a $15,000 loan.
Those who make it through the S.B.A.'s application process do so quickly. Darryl McCray, the owner of House of Nubian, a specialty store on 8th Street in Greenwich Village, saw his daily business fall off by 90 percent, from $4,000 a day before Sept. 11 to about $400 a day currently. He received approval for a $100,000 loan in less than 24 hours.
For those uncertain about applying for a loan, a few options are available. Tom Kollar, the co-owner of Tortola Hair Salon on Franklin Street in TriBeCa, said that instead of immediately turning to the S.B.A., he and his partner were able to secure a $1,000 grant from the Manhattan borough president's office -- a more desirable option to help pay for cleanup costs.
"We needed a little bit of money to help us get through the losses that we are having each week," Mr. Kollar said. "We had to pay rent for this month and make our payroll, and so the grant was a very quick help in comparison to the other options."

HEADLINE: LESSONS; 
Linking Poor Performance To Working After School

BYLINE:  By Richard Rothstein; E-mail: rrothstein@nytimes.com

 BODY:
AMERICAN student proficiency seems to decline sharply in adolescence. On international tests, pupils here perform relatively well in elementary school, but then fall behind in middle school and even more starkly in high school.
 This trend has spurred many education reform proposals, but the biggest difference between teenagers here and elsewhere may have less to do with instruction than with employment after school. 
 The Third International Mathematics and Science Survey, where low exam scores of American students stir such great concern, found that 55 percent of American 12th graders work three hours or more on a normal school day. The proportion for other nations is 18 percent, on average. To improve achievement, we have either got to cut back on after-school employment or transform adolescent jobs so they contribute more to learning. 
The negative effects of employment -- lower grades, school absenteeism, drug and alcohol use, less challenging course loads -- seem to occur mostly when students work more than about 15 hours a week. Some evidence suggests that working less than that may actually improve learning. One study found that when students work after school to earn money for college, not for CD's or high tops, achievement improves. Another found that employed teenagers steal time from watching television, not homework. If so, some jobs could be helpful.
Certainly, many Americans believe that teenage work teaches responsibility, punctuality, independence and self-confidence. Prof. Stephen Heyneman of Vanderbilt University, a former World Bank education analyst, says student jobs may explain why, despite lower academic scores, Americans are so productive. Dr. Heyneman says that teenage experience in applying for positions and cooperating with co-workers and supervisors may pay off later in an ability to adapt more easily than workers in other nations to new jobs and changes in work requirements.
But even if it is harmful, the employment of youths is now so crucial to the economy that curbing it is nearly impossible. Most teenagers earn money not to supplement family income or save for college, but for entertainment and clothing, comprising a $150 billion market.
The retail and fast-food sectors depend on young low-wage workers. Efforts in Congress and state legislatures to restrict student work hours meet fierce resistance from industry lobbyists who proclaim the character-building virtues of after-school jobs. Other industrialized nations have relatively higher service industry salaries to attract adult employees, so students are less needed.
If teenage work is an untouchable feature of American life, perhaps schools can enhance its virtues and reduce the harm. This is the idea behind the best school-to-work programs. Hours of work are limited, and teachers meet with work supervisors to coordinate job-based learning with academic studies.
Career-theme high schools in New York City and elsewhere try this with unpaid internships. But Philadelphia has 1,500 high school students spending up to two days a week in paid placements at hospitals, technology companies and other firms where work is more challenging than ringing a cash register. Teachers visit these workplaces in the summer, learning to incorporate practical applications into regular lessons. Students are graded for the work based on joint evaluations by a job supervisor and teacher.
Yet most students should not miss a day or two of classes each week. So the Philadelphia district is now asking employers to create after-school placements as well. Rochester, along with other districts, has such a program; students must enroll in college preparatory courses to be eligible.
Nationwide, only 13 percent of all 12th graders work in paid jobs obtained through school-business partnerships. And even these placements may not require coordination of curriculum and student evaluation by work supervisors and teachers together. 
In 1994, Congress provided subsidies for such programs, but they came under fierce attack by conservatives like Phyllis Schlafly who asserted that the plan was a scheme to track students into dead-end employment, that it let "central planners" decide what jobs students should aim for and that it was wrongly designed to get girls to shun traditionally female occupations.
Last month the federal school-to-work office was closed, so schools now must develop such programs on their own. They should do so, along with the business leaders who have been fierce critics of graduate skills but whose teenage hiring practices contribute to poor achievement.
If we cannot stop students from working, we can certainly improve the kind of work they do. This could turn the excessive hours worked by American students from a national handicap to a strength.

 INTERNATIONAL A3-11

Peres May Meet Arafat, But Not to Negotiate
Foreign Minister Shimon Peres of Israel, left, said he would probably meet -- but not negotiate with -- the Palestinian leader, Yasir Arafat, at an economic conference on Majorca this week. Mr. Peres also said he had drafted a new peace plan, one that called for a Palestinian state, but would not make its details public until Prime Minister Ariel Sharon had reviewed it. A10

Milosevic Calls Trial a 'Farce'
Slobodan Milosevic, the former Yugoslav president facing an array of charges, including genocide, at the international tribunal at The Hague, told the judges he would fight until the tribunal was overthrown, and he called the trial a farce. The court said his trial could begin in February. A6

Polish Police Cleared in '81 Case
Twenty-two former officers were acquitted of killing nine miners in December 1981, three days after a coup toppled the Polish government and Gen. Wojciech Jaruzelski and other military leaders moved to crush the Solidarity trade union. Officials and policemen from General Jaruzelski on down have been put on trial in connection with some 150 deaths, but none have been convicted. A11

Refugees Snarl Chunnel Traffic
Dozens of asylum seekers broke into a freight yard near Calais, France, and tried to hide on trains bound for Britain, forcing the Channel Tunnel to be closed to freight for 11 hours. The authorities arrested about 150 migrants, mostly Afghans, Iranians or Iraqi and Turkish Kurds from a Red Cross refugee camp nearby. A6

Suicide Attack in Sri Lanka
Tamil separatists staged a suicide attack when they rammed a boat loaded with explosives into a tanker carrying more than 650 tons of oil off Sri Lanka's northern coast, setting the vessel ablaze, officials said. Five boats took part in the attack, which left three watchmen missing off the coast of Point Pedro, officials said. A8

'Virtual' Pornography Case
The Supreme Court heard the government's defense of a law that treats computer-generated pornographic images of children or pornographic productions using young-looking performers who are really adults as if they were the real thing. A13

Two black men in York, Pa., were charged in the death of a white police officer during the 10 days of rioting there in 1969, an episode that just five months ago also produced murder charges against the current mayor, Charlie Robertson, who is white and was a police officer at the time. A12

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING; 
Just how safe is that catalog in your mailbox? The direct mail industry is working on assurances.

BYLINE:  By Stuart Elliott 

 BODY:
AS the crucial holiday shopping season approaches, executives of companies that sell billions of dollars of merchandise a year through direct mail and catalogs are disclosing myriad steps being taken to increase security and safety for customers made anxious by anthrax.
 The disclosures, made yesterday at a news conference at the 84th annual conference of the Direct Marketing Association in Chicago, underscored the new marketing climate, in which unwanted mail can be not just annoying but also hazardous. Yet the industry, which is responsible for annual sales of goods and services estimated at $528 billion last year and $580 billion in 2001, is sending mixed signals about the dangers. 
 Though the executives agreed they had seen no indications of sales slowdowns related to consumer reluctance to open or use mail, their remarks clearly indicated concern about the potential effects of the anthrax scares on the operations of the Postal Service, upon which they remain largely dependent, as well as on consumer confidence -- particularly as recent cases involved for the first time people who do not work for the government, the news media or the post office. 
"We need a viable Postal Service and postal system," said Michael P. Sherman, president at the Fingerhut Companies in Minnetonka, Minn., a division of Federated Department Stores that he described as the largest catalog customer of the Postal Service and the ninth-largest customer over all.
"We're not going to let the threat of terrorism undermine the post office," said Mr. Sherman, who is the new chairman of the association. "There's going to be a Christmas this year."
At the same time, the association announced no new industrywide measures being taken to allay consumer concerns and repeatedly played down the possibility that those worries could spread to the point of doing considerable damage to holiday sales.
The problems have been centered on "a handful of handwritten letters in the mail," said H. Robert Wientzen, president and chief executive at the association, as he sought to minimize the scope of the anthrax incidents, which have killed three people.
"So far, no commercial mail has been involved," he added.
At one point, Mr. Wientzen, who is based in New York, said, "We have to keep this in perspective." He noted, for example, that driving has not been curbed even though traffic deaths continue to be recorded on the nation's highways.
Of course, many people are aware, at least occasionally, that highway driving might result in hospitalization or even death, a thought that until recently probably did not cross the minds of most Americans as they opened junk mail.
There are even "some fairly positive things on the horizon," Mr. Wientzen said, at least from the perspective of his membership, in that as consumers travel less they are "tending to spend more time at home," a situation that can historically cause "catalog sales to be fairly robust."
Apparently not completely willing to rely on historic trends to bolster sales, particularly at a time of year when a decline in demand can doom an entire year's results, the executives at the news conference described numerous moves they are making or plan to make to tamp down consumer fears.
For instance, George D. Ittner, president and chief executive at the Newport News division of Spiegel Inc. in Downers Grove, Ill., which sells women's apparel and home furnishings through catalogs, said his company was making its name and logo "more prominent on catalogs, packages" and more closely monitoring how its catalogs are printed, from the "rotogravure plant to the binding line."
Jeff Jones, chief operating officer at Lands' End in Dodgeville, Wis., which mails about 300 million catalogs a year to 11 million households and 200,000 companies, outlined steps including a decision to "enhance our security greatly" both internally and externally.
Mr. Jones said, for example, that Lands' End had "moved incoming mail to a separate facility down the road from Dodgeville."
"Whatever it takes," he pledged, the company will do.
Other changes, the executives said, include sending customers e-mail messages telling them to expect the arrival of new catalogs; adding identification like company logos, telephone numbers and Web site addresses to packages that may be sent as gifts and are therefore not expected by the recipients; posting reproductions of covers of coming catalogs on company Web sites, which concerned consumers may examine; posting the entire contents of catalogs on Web sites for those consumers who are refusing to open unsolicited mail; and paying additional attention to temporary and casual employees working at the companies during the holiday season.
Mr. Wientzen expressed his concern several times that the added costs of security being incurred by the Postal Service, along with the potential loss of revenue from lower mail volumes, would "provide an additional financial burden" that must be addressed by Congress, which "will have to enact legislation very quickly."
Even before the anthrax attacks, Postal Service mail volume had declined 6.6 percent from Sept. 8 to Oct. 5, compared with the period a year earlier. The Postal Service suspended its television and print advertising last week because of what a statement described as "current events"; only last month, Leo Burnett USA in Chicago, part of the Leo Burnett Worldwide division of the Bcom3 Group, introduced a campaign carrying the theme "The U.S. Postal Service is everywhere so you can be anywhere."
If mail volume continues to decline at current rates, Mr. Wientzen said, the Postal Service could lose $3.5 billion in annual revenue.
The Postal Service "operates under rules from 1970," Mr. Wientzen said, referring to its transformation from a Cabinet department to an autonomous business operation that is not supposed to be subsidized by Congress. And, he said, it "did not anticipate faxes, e-mail and the Internet," which have all siphoned revenue from the mail system.
"It sure as hell did not anticipate anthrax," he added.

HEADLINE: Ford Family Takes the Helm; 
Effort to Overcome Rift At Troubled Company

BYLINE:  By DANNY HAKIM 

DATELINE: DEARBORN, Mich., Oct. 30 

 BODY:
The Fords, one of the most prominent and enduring families in American business, reasserted control today of the Ford Motor Company for the first time in more than two decades. 
 But William Clay Ford Jr. -- the family member who took over as the company's sole chief executive, ending an awkward power sharing arrangement with the ousted Jacques A. Nasser -- assumes leadership of a company mired in deep trouble. 
 Mr. Ford said yesterday that the events of Sept. 11 had worsened an already bleak outlook for the auto industry and accelerated the need for change at the top of Ford, which has been struggling to devise a turnaround plan. 
"This isn't a time for grand visions and for a lot of barnstorming tours of the world talking about how we're going to revolutionize the world," he said at Ford's headquarters here. "What we need to do now is fix our core business, put a plan together that is credible and, underlying all of that, fix the quality and introduce cars that are exciting."
Mr. Ford, 44, has a lot going for him. He is regarded as affable and open -- qualities rarely associated with Mr. Nasser, who resigned after meeting with Mr. Ford on Monday afternoon. He has a winning sense of humor, too. As hundreds of workers cheered him today, Mr. Ford began a speech by cracking, "You'd think the Detroit Lions had won a game or something" -- a reference to the family's football team, the only squad without a victory in the National Football League.
He has friends in odd places for an auto executive. A Sierra Club official today called Mr. Ford -- who has pledged the company to improve the fuel economy of its sport utility vehicles by 25 percent, prompting General Motors and Chrysler to make similar commitments -- "an enlightened executive."
And he has the solid support of his family, which holds only 6 percent of Ford's stock and just 3 of 14 seats on the board but has voting rights that give it effective control of the company. Family members, who regularly meet three times a year, gathered for a particularly gloomy discussion of the company's prospects three weeks ago. The company had just cut its dividend, at great cost to the Fords, and the yearlong tug-of-war for power between Mr. Ford and Mr. Nasser was on everyone's mind, Mr. Ford said in an interview. 
"We talked about all of the issues facing the company," he said. "They wanted my input into how I saw things. It was probably clear then that if things didn't get better, this was going to happen." 
What is less clear is if Mr. Ford has the skills to succeed as a chief executive at an increasingly difficult time. Ford is locked in a price war with G.M. and Chrysler and is losing market share. Its two consecutive quarterly losses are the first such six-month period in a decade. Its relationships with dealers, suppliers and employees are frayed. Its credit rating has been lowered, its cash reserves are dwindling and the industry expects overall sales to fall sharply in 2002. It stock closed yesterday at $16.05, down 16 cents. 
Today, Mr. Ford -- who first came to work at Ford when he was 22 years old -- said he would surround himself with seasoned executives and make it his business to smooth ruffled feathers. Among other squabbles, he said he would consider ending the company's bitter feud with Bridgestone, the tire manufacturer whose American Firestone unit was founded by his maternal great-grandfather.
Wall Street analysts said today that Mr. Ford was unproved, but generally agreed that having a Ford at the company's helm would improve morale.
More important, they said, was that Mr. Ford has chosen credible top executives to serve under him. Nicholas V. Scheele, brought back from Ford of Europe this summer to run the company's automotive operations in North America, was named president and chief operating officer. Carl E. Reichardt, a longtime board member, was named vice chairman. Mr. Reichardt, who served for more than a decade as the chief executive of Wells Fargo & Company, will oversee Ford's financial operations at a time when they are struggling. 
"I'm not dictatorial at all," Mr. Ford said. "I look to Nick and Carl both as sort of an easygoing partnership where we just sit around and figure things out."
Mr. Ford never was able to achieve that kind of partnership with Mr. Nasser. They vowed to work as a team in 1999, when Mr. Ford was named nonexecutive chairman and Mr. Nasser was named chief executive. But Mr. Ford complained increasingly that he was outside the flow of important information, and he was clearly uncomfortable with Mr. Nasser's more autocratic management style. 
As the company's financial problems mounted -- along with the cost in dollars and reputation from the deaths and injuries in Ford Explorers equipped with Firestone tires -- the two men renegotiated their relationship. In July, Mr. Ford created an office of chairman and chief executive in a move to share power, and there was speculation that Mr. Nasser's days were numbered. 
Mr. Nasser's demise is the latest in a long history of Ford family dust-ups with management. The original Henry Ford, the company's brilliant but spiteful founder, clashed frequently with his managers, though perhaps none so bitterly as his own son, Edsel B. Ford, who served for years as the company's president but died before, and never escaped the shadow of, his father.
Edsel's son, Henry Ford II, became the second Ford to dominate the company, presiding over it for three decades. He was known for his refusal to delegate much authority and for shuffling top executives. When he brought a top G.M. executive, Semon Knudsen, to the company as president in 1968, Mr. Knudsen lasted 19 months. When he fired another president, Lee A. Iacocca, Mr. Ford said simply: "Well, sometimes you just don't like somebody."
The new chief executive, who prefers to be called Bill, came to the company in 1979, at 22, the same year that his, uncle Henry Ford II, stepped down, and now becomes the third Ford to preside over the company that bears his name. He is considered the only family member of his generation to possess the qualifications of a top executive. He has worked in manufacturing, sales, product development and finance. He left in 1983 to study finance at M.I.T., and has run the company's finance committee for five years. 
But Mr. Ford has not been a chief executive, and in an interview today said he had mixed feelings about running the company.
"I came close to leaving several times, as I got frustrated with the company and the pace of change and some of the positions we'd taken," he said. "In the end, I really love this company, and it's my children's future and my grandchildren's future. So here I am."
Most analysts said it was time that the company moved past Mr. Nasser's stormy tenure and faced up to the problems at hand. 
"Whether rightly or wrongly, Jacques Nasser had become a lighting rod for many of Ford's problems, and moving past his tenure will help this new team attack the new problems," said Jim Benson, an auto analyst and fund manager for the Oakmark Funds, which invest in Ford.
Stephen J. Girsky, an analyst at Morgan Stanley, said, "I think Nasser wanted to do too much too quickly, and the organization wasn't ready too accept it."
He added that Ford's organization -- Mr. Nasser never named a chief operating officer -- itself was not up to the task. "The job is bigger than one person," Mr. Girsky said. He noted that the structure Ford announced today was similar to that recently put in place at G.M.
Until now, arguably, Mr. Ford's biggest impact on the car business has been a push for the industry to accept responsibility for its impact on the environment.
"He recognizes that we have to solve the problem of global warming," said Daniel Becker, director of global warming issues for the Sierra Club, who praised Mr. Ford's elevation to chief executive.
"People always like to put you down as something, either a numbers guy, a car guy or a tree hugger," Mr. Ford said yesterday. 
"If you look at where society is going, those distinctions are becoming blurred between being a businessman, an environmentalist and an industrialist," he added.
He said his primary focus as chief executive would be turning the company around and using his name to create opportunities, rather than bearing it as a burden.
"For years, probably all the way back to Henry Ford the first, there has always been kind of a cult of personality that has swirled around the Ford Motor Company," he said. "This company is bigger than any individual, and it has proven that time and time again. We're a strong family with a strong ethic and a strong culture. It transcends any individual, including me."
Still, some things are personal. Mr. Ford said it might be time to end hostilities with Firestone. Ford Motor and Bridgestone/Firestone have feuded bitterly over responsibility for hundreds of deaths in Explorers equipped with Firestone tires. The fight has pitted the companies against each other in Congressional hearings and courtrooms, has led Ford to spend $3 billion replacing Firestone tires and resulted, earlier this year, in the severing of a business relationship that last nearly a century.
"My mother is a Firestone," Mr. Ford said, adding that when the controversy over the tires first became public in August 2000, he was scheduled to be the keynote speaker at a Firestone family reunion. 
"I called my mother up and said, 'Mom, I can't go.' " he said "And she said, 'Do you think I shouldn't go?,' And I said 'No, no, you should go. People aren't going to expect you to know about Explorer details.' So she went, and my wife and children went." 
Mr. Ford said that Mr. Nasser's handling of the Firestone dispute did not play a role in his departure. But he indicated he might take a different approach.
"We're caught up in so many legal issues and everything else that I don't know where we go from here," he said. "But I would like to have a constructive relationship with Firestone, with Bridgestone, the parent company. Whether that's possible, I'd certainly be open to it.
"In the broader sense, leaving Firestone aside, we need to reach out to all of our constituents -- suppliers, dealers, government and obviously the biggest of all, our employees, and really start to rebuild relationships that may have been frayed."

HEADLINE: A Nation Challenged 

 BODY:
 Strategy and Diplomacy

MILITARY ADVISERS -- A small group of American soldiers has been working closely with rebel forces in northern Afghanistan. A1

ECHOES OF VIETNAM -- The word quagmire has again begun to haunt conversations among government officials and students of foreign policy as complications become apparent in Afghanistan. B1

HOLDING THE COALITION -- Defense Secretary Donald H. Rumsfeld plans to leave this week on a mission to ensure support from Russia and the nations surrounding Afghanistan. B1

THE UZBEKISTAN ALLIANCE -- Tommy R. Franks, the American general in charge of the military campaign, met with the president of Uzbekistan to cement the countries' expanding relationship. B3

CONCERN OVER CASUALTIES -- Upset by civilian deaths, the head of the United Nations refugee agency called for the United States and Britain to show restraint in bombing. B3

ASSASSINATION CONSPIRACY -- An Egyptian man in London was charged with conspiracy in the assassination of Ahmed Shah Massoud, the Afghan rebel leader. B3

CHANGING GENERALS -- Lt. Gen. Charles F. Wald, the commander of the air war over Afghanistan, will leave to take a high-ranking position in the Pentagon. B3

Anthrax Investigation

A CHANGING INQUIRY -- Just a few days ago, it seemed possible that the spate of anthrax infections could be largely traced to a single, potent letter that traveled from New Jersey to Washington. Now that seems increasingly unlikely. A1

TRACKING A LETTER -- The envelope that created a cluster of inhalation anthrax cases in Washington. B9

NEW DISCOVERIES -- Anthrax traces were found in two more Washington post offices. B9

New Anthrax in New York

HOSPITAL WORKERS TREATED -- Officials ordered antibiotic treatment for all New York hospital employees and perhaps thousands of patients and visitors as investigators searched for the source of inhaled anthrax that left a 61-year-old medical worker critically ill. A1

THE VICTIM -- The Bronx neighbors of the hospital employee fighting for her life against inhalation anthrax talk about her kindness and her flight from Vietnam in 1977. A1

HUNTING FOR THE SOURCE -- Officials desperately try to trace the steps of the ill woman to find out how, and where, she encountered anthrax spores. B4

SHUT DOWN -- Staff members and patients of the Manhattan Eye, Ear and Throat Hospital arrived to find closed doors and health workers doling out doses of antibiotics. It was the first time an entire hospital was closed by the threat of anthrax contamination. B4

Tracking the Outbreaks

IN NEW YORK -- The latest anthrax diagnosis in New York is the city's seventh such case since a tainted letter was opened in the NBC news offices this month. B6

NEW JERSEY EXPOSURES -- A cluster of anthrax cases in New Jersey seemed to prove officials wrong after they went to pains to assure the public that merely handling tainted mail could not make anyone ill. B6

THE CAPITAL'S CASES -- In Washington, the Brentwood mail complex has been a common factor in the confirmed anthrax cases. B7

FLORIDA INVESTIGATION -- It has been a month since an editor at a tabloid was killed by inhalation anthrax, but officials still do not know specifically how spores got into his office. B7

Treatment and Safety

TESTING COMES SECOND -- Officials are treating first and waiting for test results later because of the delay before a blood test can confirm exposure to anthrax. B8

LEARNING TO CURE -- With every anthrax case, doctors are finding new ways to give the lie to a previous assumption that inhalation anthrax was inevitably fatal. B9

CLEANING THE MAIL -- The first batch of mail from Washington arrived in Lima, Ohio, for electron-beam sterilization. B10

THE DRUG INDUSTRY -- Drug companies are asking for government contracts to research drugs to protect against bioterrorism. The request is creating an uneasy debate over how close the industry should get to the government. B10

Security Concerns

TERRORISM WARNING -- The government said its latest warning of possible terrorist attacks stemmed from credible intelligence sources, including an electronic interception of a coded message from an associate of Osama bin Laden. A1

LIFE DURING WARTIME -- Many Americans say that despite some worry about terrorism and the war in Afghanistan, they are going on with their everyday lives. B1

NOT QUITE CAREFREE -- While the president attended the World Series, the vice president was being kept in a secure location. It continued a mixed message of urging the public to carry on with normal life, but warily. A1

SECURING THE SERIES -- Secret Service agents swept the stands, snipers were stationed on rooftops and even hot dog vendors were screened at Yankee Stadium. B5

VACCINE SUPPLY -- The administration said it wanted access to a stock of anthrax vaccine large enough to protect almost a million people. B10

GUARDING RESOURCES -- States in the Northeast deployed more National Guard troops at transportation centers, water supplies and nuclear power plants after federal warnings of possible terrorist attacks. B11

AIR SECURITY -- The government temporarily banned private airplanes from flying within 11 1/2 miles of nuclear sites. B11

Ripples From Sept. 11

ECONOMIC STIMULUS -- The Bush administration pressured Congress to reach agreement on the stalled economic recovery package. A12

BUSINESS IN THE MAIL -- With the holiday season approaching, the mail order industry is scrambling to assuage new public fears about unsolicited deliveries. A12

FUND-RAISING HALTED -- American Red Cross officials suspended fund-raising for victims of the terrorist attacks, saying that the $547 million pledged so far should be enough to cover the costs of relief efforts. B11

PAKISTANI INDUSTRY -- Pakistan's textile and apparel industry, which employs 60 percent of the country's industrial work force, has lost at least 18,000 jobs recently after some American clothing retailers decided not to renew orders. C1

LOAN REJECTIONS -- More than half of the small businesses in New York City that applied for federal disaster loans since the trade center attacks have had their applications rejected, resulting in one of the lowest loan-approval rates in recent years among communities that have had to grapple with large-scale disasters. The Arts

ESCAPIST TV -- Writers of successful television comedies set in New York are studiously ignoring the real-life events of Sept. 11. Perhaps as a result, the shows are doing better than ever. E1

Portraits of Grief B12

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

SECTION: Section D; Column 1; Metropolitan Desk; Pg. 4

LENGTH: 2361 words

HEADLINE: In Crisis Management, Green Aims to Be Calm and Compassionate 

 BODY:
 Following are excerpts from an interview with the mayoral candidate Mark Green conducted yesterday by reporters and editors of The New York Times.

Q. So it's 3 o'clock a couple of months from now and you're sitting in City Hall, Mayor Green, and the attorney general gets on television and says there's a threat, a credible threat, but we don't know what it is. What do you do?

MARK GREEN -- I call my police and fire commissioners and the head of the Office of Emergency Management and ask them to come to City Hall so we can review the latest information that we've been gathering on such threats locally. By then there'll be a deputy police commissioner for counterterrorism . . . whose mission will be to stay in touch with regional equivalents, the F.B.I. and Governor Ridge of Homeland Security. One of the problems with terrorism is that they can strike anywhere where you're least prepared. The World Trade Center comes to mind. But you just try to have the best plans in place. Jerry Hauer's told me that until Sept. 11, he thought that New York . . . was the most prepared municipality in the country to anticipate and respond to terrorism. But there's always more to do. He suggested that our hospitals could be better linked and ready to handle casualties and can be better used as points of departure, P.O.D.'s, for distribution of antibiotics, for example. . . . The attitude I bring to any such meeting would . . . reflect the temperament that Rudy Giuliani brought to the tragedy six Tuesdays ago. You've got to be calm. You've got to be resolute. . . . You've got to be compassionate. But you have to utterly focus on the threat and the challenge in front of you.

Q. And what do you tell New Yorkers?

MR. GREEN -- Depends on the information I get. . . . What I would tell New Yorkers is what I would tell my own family. There's no alternative to getting on with our lives. But at the same time just be alert to anything unusual happening. . . . If you see people in your neighborhood who are acting in a very unusual way, think whether it's worth reporting that to the local police. Normalcy is essential, balanced by prudence.

Q. Do you think, from a public policy point of view, the attorney general was right in sort of passing on an unspecific warning like that? Would you like that, or would you share information like that?

MR. GREEN -- Of course the answer to that turns on exactly what he knows -- which I don't know. My wife and I were discussing that . . . we thought that it seemed alarmist without a purpose. But again, he might know something that we don't.

Q. What about day-to-day safety and security? You've spoken about more random searches happening in public places. How often would those kind of things happen? And . . . say the police found, not weapons or something, but a six-pack of beer on a teenager, would that be used to prosecute the teenager for alcohol?

MR. GREEN -- It is, of course, constitutionally impermissible and divisive for the police to select entire categories of people by race, ethnicity, nationality, to search. My memory is that it is constitutionally permissible to have a checkpoint where you stop randomly every 10th car and ask for ID. And it is constitutionally permissible that if you see something in open view that is potentially illegal, let's say an unlicensed weapon or contraband, the police then are allowed to seize it and prosecute it. I'm a lawyer, but my memory is you're not allowed to, at random checkpoints, go into the trunk of a car without a search warrant. I want to give more professional discretion to the cop on the beat to decide, given all the circumstances, whether to prosecute a 19-year-old at a checkpoint who happens to have an exposed beer can in a car at a time when the city should be focusing on anticipating, preventing or avoiding a terrorist attack. I don't know that's something that a mayor or someone from 1 Police Plaza ought to be deciding.

Q. Giving discretion to the cop on the beat was what presented a lot of the complaints in past years over police abuse. And as a corollary, when you talk about stopping one out of every 10, we have about a thousand people, even more according to this morning's paper, who are in detention, most of them secret. But I think it's safe to say that most of them have either the names Mohammed, Abdul or Ahmed in their names. How do you go against -- how do you stop the terror threat that you know comes from a specific group without stopping only or primarily people from that group? . . .

MR. GREEN -- Of course, you have to have guidelines from City Hall and 1 Police Plaza . . . that can guide police in exercising their discretion on the street. You don't just leave it up to a police officer willy-nilly in each ZIP code to enforce the law. Remember, I've had several private meetings with police officers off hours, no brass, no chiefs. And one, a white officer in Queens, told me, "Mr. Green, what good does it do to have 20 more so-called quality-of-life arrests -- and then have the community hate you?" And he argued that he should have the discretion whether to arrest a truly menacing young man who was blocking an entrance to a convenience store drinking a beer. But should he have the discretion not to arrest a 60-year-old man sitting on . . . the stoop with his family drinking a beer. As for the profiling issue, this is a very agonizing issue. I watched how Ray Kelly, who's the head of customs, avoided racial profiling, . . . using other indicia and more intercepts at the borders to reduce the amount of contraband and dangerous weapons from coming in at our borders. And I just hope that by better technology, smarter triggers, that we can avoid . . . apprehending anybody who has Mohammed as a first name -- other generations had other first and last names.

Q. Can we talk about the budget -- next year's? . . . Do you think the $4 billion deficit figure is about right?

MR. GREEN -- According to budget monitors at the Independent Budget Office and the Citizens Budget Committee, there was already an at least $2.5 billion shortfall in 2003 and 2004. This is not unusual because it is common to underestimate revenues at a time of growth. Computers are static and they don't always pick up a rising tide. Hence surpluses have covered out-year deficits in the past half-decade because of a strong economy. On the flip side, projections often don't pick up a decline -- as steep a decline as may be happening. I do have right now a budget advisory panel meeting to advise me as a candidate, but largely to advise me as a mayor-elect should I win on Nov. 6. It's chaired by Comptroller Hevesi and it has 10 experts at the state and city levels who are looking closely at the numbers and giving me advice on options. As of now, I believe there will be a minimum of $4 billion to a ceiling of $6 billion deficit in fiscal '03 which starts in eight months. As a percentage of . . . discretionary city spending, that is more than double the projected deficit through the 1990's. Where there was, you know, 4 to 6 percent was common, but $4 billion to $6 billion would be nearly 18 percent of discretionary city spending excluding federal and state contributions because the city doesn't -- would never cut that. We accept, we hope that's as large as feasible. So I do think the looming fiscal problem is as great a crisis as any since the . . . mid- to late-1970's. . . . Felix Rohatyn says that he thinks the budget gap and fiscal crisis is greater than the 1970's.

Q. But that's coming, isn't it?

MR. GREEN -- It's among the options. I think . . . since Sept. 11, I was the first public official to urge a temporary hiring freeze to shrink the work force through attrition, exempting cops and teachers. Now I would add firefighters because we lost 342 of them. But, look, if the fiscal situation worsens and we have the worst fiscal crisis in a half century, of course terrible things would be on the triage table. And if you'd like to ask me, one by one, what they are and what I would do, I'd say everything's on the table because the goal is to make the city economically and fiscally whole. . . .

Q. Would you push for increased raises for cops and firemen, and secondarily, teachers, above, beyond what others -- city workers -- would get? And how would you do that in the context of what you just said?

MR. GREEN -- The mayor's own budget anticipates the possibility of 4 percent salary increases for all civilian employees and 5 percent salary increase for all the uniformed personnel. This doesn't mean it should be that number. But it's awkward but essential that the city budget for possible increases. So already there is a anticipated if modest disparity between civilian and uniformed. Again, at a time of crisis, I'd be open to new ideas. . . . There is a presumption in favor of pattern bargaining because of history and convenience. It's what lawyers call a rebuttable presumption that there's a compelling case to the contrary. What's compelling? I'd have to be open to it if we couldn't hire cops and teachers because in the regional economy Nassau and Suffolk were hiring them away because they're paying starting salaries a third higher than we. So while there's a good argument based on precedent for pattern bargaining, there's also an economic argument for revisiting it. Or else we won't be able to fill the entering police class and the entering teacher group, both of which are essential for a safe, smart city.

Q. And which of those arguments do you prefer?

MR. GREEN -- I'll know better when I see the data. And I'll certainly be able to talk about it when I negotiate at the proper forum, which is a collective bargaining table. . . . But I have resisted throughout the whole campaign saying what I would give to get in municipal labor negotiations. And I think it's important to stick to that. Otherwise . . . politicians can start playing a pandering game where they throw out big numbers without obligations. At the risk of credentialing the attacks on my self-confidence and arrogance, since I thought I would be elected at the start of this campaign, I resisted making excessive promises in the belief that I'd have to keep them. By the way, I've run sometimes in a campaign where it was real hard to get elected. And there it's real easy to make big promises to get big headlines . . . because they're unlikely to be redeemed. I thought I'd have to redeem my promises. And at the risk of again offending Ed Koch, I've tried to not enter office with a lot of I.O.U.'s that might hurt the city.

Q. Back to the deficit and what the city faces over the next couple of years. The governor has endorsed your opponent and he's seeking $54 billion in aid from the federal government to help both the state and the city. I want to know how much, or how realistic you think that aid request is, which pieces of it you think the city really needs and how you'll work with the governor to get what the city needs given the relationship between the two of you.

MR. GREEN -- First, the governor and I have an excellent relationship. And I know you know that it was 100 percent inevitable that the Republican governor and the Republican mayor would endorse the Republican nominee for mayor. That has absolutely no role or impact on my potential relationship should I be the mayor-elect. He and I have always gotten along very well. And I predict we'll get along better than the current governor and mayor. I think the bulk of his request was urgent and necessary. And at the margins, there were some requests that Washington legislators thought were overreaching. I'm not going to undermine or second-guess the only relief package we're currently seeking in Washington. But on . . . the night of Nov. 6 or the day of Nov. 7, I look forward to speaking with Governor Pataki and beginning the first day of the rest of our lives -- the first day of a new relationship. . . . For example, today I'll be being endorsed on the steps of City Hall by Speaker Silver and a lot of assembly members. And I'll be discussing my proposal a week after the attack for a state-city redevelopment authority. It was the first proposal of its kind. And I want to just remind people that I think it wrong to argue we need a city authority, as my rival has said. Because it's jurisdictionally unsophisticated. It has to be a state-city authority for no other reason than the state is contributing funds and ideas. And the state creates the city under law. But any authority has to have parity between the governor and the mayor. And there has to not be an imbalance in say and sway over a emergency redevelopment authority between the governor and the mayor. . . . I spoke to the governor about my ideas within 10 days of the attack. And I had spoken to Speaker Silver about it two weeks ago. And I can't have a serious role in this as an aspirant, but I will have a serious role, I hope, in the creation of an emergency redevelopment authority as a mayor-elect.

Q. How's that redevelopment authority going to be financed if there are things, as you say, . . . on the margins like request for a high-speed rail that goes upstate to create jobs around the state?

MR. GREEN -- But that was a minor part of the package.

Q. But can you prioritize what things you would ask from Congress that should be financed immediately?

MR. GREEN -- Yeah, well, what should be financed immediately, of course, is to make us whole down there. Like a southern state after Hurricane Andrew. So that's to -- it's a FEMA relief for the cleanup over time. You can apply for it, but small businesses can apply for loans if they've been shattered, dislocated, lost revenues down there. Beyond that, I do want to take a more comprehensive approach to rebuilding downtown Manhattan. It's not simply those 17 acres in and around what was the World Trade Center, but a river-to-river Canal Street South, so we have a comprehensive commercial, retail, residential and cultural integrated plan for the downtown of the future. . . . We'll be paying the costs of Sept. 11 probably for a century. But it has provided the opportunity to think anew downtown.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Mark Green (James Estrin/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Liberties; 
These Spooky Times

BYLINE:  By MAUREEN DOWD 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON 

 BODY:
Last week, Georgetown University held a job fair. At the booths of the big Wall Street companies and banks, students had little wait. But to talk to a C.I.A. recruiter, the line stretched for over half an hour.
 Ed Friedman, 21, a senior studying at the School for Foreign Affairs, was in that line. "Two years ago," he said, "Goldman Sachs was the place to be -- even people in the School for Foreign Affairs lined up there. People were interested in six-figure salaries and they viewed the C.I.A. as not having much of a purpose." 
 But that was before Sept. 11. "Everyone wants to be part of this, to help," he said.
Despite questions of another quagmire, America is at the moment a weird inside-out image of the Vietnam era. The C.I.A. and R.O.T.C. are chic on elite campuses, where flags hang from dorm rooms. Police have gone from "pigs" to heroes. And many of those who complained about America's escalation in Southeast Asia are now complaining about America's hesitation in Central Asia.
In an odd role reversal, the country is more gung-ho than the administration. And the Pentagon seems more skittish about the mounting number of civilian casualties and more consumed with winning Afghan hearts than the rest of America is. 
Back then Walter Cronkite famously wondered what we were doing in Vietnam. Now Tom Brokaw presses Donald Rumsfeld about whether we can ever succeed in Afghanistan unless we put in "a division-size force, seize an airport, make that the base of operations." 
Just as some A.C.L.U. lawyers now secretly mutter that they want to seal our borders, and some liberals are easily dismissing their concerns about civil liberties abuses and capital punishment, so many old peaceniks are now hawky, less concerned about which group of beards runs post-Taliban Afghanistan than about aggressively going after the villains.
This is the first war since World War II that is personal. Vietnam was inspired by the domino theory, and dragged on because Lyndon Johnson and Robert McNamara did not want to lose face. Desert Storm was about keeping our gas prices low. 
But this time they brought the war to us, and now it is about protecting ourselves and the way we live.
The terrorists have already turned Halloween into an adult holiday and stolen Christmas. Who wants to open Christmas cards or touch catalogs or go to malls? They have debilitated our confidence, both in our economy and our freedom, and made us jittery about the very breaths we take and food we eat.
President Bush went to the Yankees game with 1,200 police officers as Dick Cheney headed back underground and John Ashcroft warned us to watch out for every single thing. 
But the truth is we can't be alert because we don't know what we're looking for, and most of what we're looking for is invisible, and we don't want to fall into ugly generalizations.
The F.B.I. has gotten over 300,000 tips from Americans trying to be alert, but the agency is still stumped.
Secretary Rumsfeld says agitation for a quicker military pace is due to the American desire for "instant gratification." But that is too glib. Americans are impatient about the war because -- unless we live near a crop duster or nuclear plant and can keep watch, or find ourselves on a plane being hijacked and can throw a punch -- it is our only avenue for striking back. 
Just as Robert McNamara once said we lost Vietnam because the government had never understood the Vietnamese culture, so Rear Adm. John Stufflebeem said yesterday that he had underestimated the tenacity of the Afghan troops because he had not understood that the Taliban "don't see the world the same way we do," or automatically accept American "inevitability."
Admiral Stufflebeem may be the last to know that Afghanistan is a stubborn and durable place, whose history is a long tale of war.
In a speech yesterday, Tony Blair said we must never forget the twin towers and the heroes "and the menace of bin Laden." It makes me worried that he is worried that the horror is already dimming. And it makes me nostalgic for that old "Wanted: dead or alive" certainty.
If ever the United States needed historical memory, it is right now. This is a just war, and not just war. 

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: A NATION CHALLENGED: THE NEW CASE; 
HOSPITAL WORKER'S ILLNESS SUGGESTS WIDENING THREAT; SECURITY TIGHTENS OVER U.S.

BYLINE:  By JENNIFER STEINHAUER 

 BODY:
City and public health officials yesterday began giving antibiotics to hundreds of hospital employees and started tracking down thousands of patients and visitors as investigators searched for the mysterious source of the infection that has left a Manhattan hospital worker critically ill with inhalation anthrax. 
 The employee, identified as Kathy T. Nguyen, 61, of the Bronx, was described by health officials as being in grave condition on a respirator and struggling to survive after contracting the first case in New York of pulmonary anthrax, the most serious form of the disease. She worked in a medical supply room at Manhattan Eye, Ear and Throat Hospital, in a spot that until recently had also housed a mailroom. 
 But health officials said they found no direct connection to any tainted mail and knew of no evidence of a suspicious letter, fueling the growing belief that the nascent path of anthrax infection had moved beyond media companies, government offices and mail routes, to ordinary citizens. 
That possibility also propelled officials to alert city hospitals, emergency rooms in particular, to watch for odd cases involving infections. And in a rare move, the authorities shut down Ms. Nguyen's employer, a small hospital on the Upper East Side known mostly for cosmetic surgery, and prepared to treat its staff and recent patients with antibiotics. Investigators began to retrace Ms. Nguyen's steps, and people in protective suits forced their way into her Bronx apartment to test for spores.
Word of the case spread on a day when the city was at its highest stage of alert anyway, as President Bush threw out the first ball at Game 3 of the World Series at Yankee Stadium and the city braced against continued warnings of credible threats of more terrorist attacks. Experts in hazardous materials, bomb-sniffing dogs and more than 1,000 police officers cased the stadium, and fans arrived hours before the game and went through X-ray machines, leaving bags and umbrellas home. Some hospitals kept extra staff members on hand in anticipation of a vast medical emergency.
Security was tightened around the nation. While Mr. Bush traveled, Vice President Cheney moved to an undisclosed location. "We're getting some intelligence that suggests that we may again be the focus of an attack or attacks," Tom Ridge, the director of the Office of Homeland Security, said, echoing remarks by Attorney General John Ashcroft on Monday.
"As a result of the alert yesterday, we have security guards at shopping malls paying more attention, personnel at airports paying more attention, the policemen on the street from the 11-to-7 beat who are driving that patrol car around that neighborhood," Mr. Ridge said. "If everybody has a heightened sense of alert, we send a signal not only to America, but those who would terrorize us, those who are trying to disrupt our way of life, that we are on guard as a country." 
Also yesterday, new federal office buildings were added to the growing list of those that have tested positive for traces of anthrax. Mr. Ridge said trace amounts of anthrax had been found on Monday in the mailroom of the Economic Research Service of the United States Department of Agriculture, and the head of the State Department's medical unit said anthrax spores were "probably all over" the two-block-long building. 
Further, Postal Service officials said traces of anthrax spores had been found at a postal station in northwest Washington and in Dulles Station, Va., and Postmaster General John E. Potter said at a Senate hearing that the service would need several billion dollars for cleanup and new security measures. 
Also yesterday, health officials in New Jersey confirmed the second case of inhalation anthrax in a postal worker, a case they had already suspected. And an accountant who worked in Hamilton Township and contracted the first case of skin anthrax outside the post office there was released from a hospital, officials said. 
So far, three people have died of anthrax infections after inhaling spores, a photo editor in Florida and two postal workers in the Washington area. The similar, life-threatening anthrax infection apparently suffered by Ms. Nguyen is alarming, officials said, both because she did not work in the areas that have been identified by the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention as most risky and because the source of her infection is a mystery. 
Prior to this week, in the other anthrax cases, the infected people worked for media companies or in government office buildings, which are suspected targets, or in a post office through which the anthrax-laced letters were carried.
But on Monday, two new cases -- that of Ms. Nguyen and the New Jersey accountant -- came to light that had no obvious connection to the tainted letters.
The accountant did not open mail as a regular part of her job and had not visited the Hamilton post office. 
Ms. Nguyen worked in a supply room at the hospital that was once adjacent to a mailroom, and postal officials said mail sent to the hospital would first be sorted at the Morgan Station distribution center in Manhattan, where anthrax spores have already been discovered in four high-speed sorting machines. 
However, that connection was tenuous, an extrapolation from previous cases, officials said. Health care workers and city officials readily acknowledged that Ms. Nguyen could have become infected outside the hospital.
"There's no question there's the possibility she got it somewhere else," Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani said yesterday. "The hospital is being focused on because that's where she worked. And she was proximate to a mailroom. And so you have to give that a lot of consideration. But so far the 10 samples that have come back have come back negative. And if all the rest come back negative then we're going to have to focus on the possibility that she got it somewhere else." The first 10 samples were from the mailroom.
Dr. Neal L. Cohen, the city's health commissioner, said Ms. Nguyen was in critical condition last night at Lenox Hill Hospital, the owner of Manhattan Eye and Ear. She arrived at Lenox Hill on Sunday after flulike symptoms that she began feeling on Thursday had rapidly escalated. According to Dr. Cohen, she developed chills and muscle aches on Thursday, but worked through them that day and Friday.
Late Sunday afternoon, she started to get very serious symptoms, and by late evening she had checked herself into Lenox Hill, where she remained yesterday on a respirator, Dr. Cohen said. Initial tests were consistent with anthrax, and while more results are pending, the case is being treated as such, he added. The case was announced late Monday.
Ms. Nguyen's illness underscored the complex and often confounding effort to diagnose and contain anthrax infections. Anthrax, which American health care workers have little exposure to, defies the diagnostic pathways laid out for other outbreaks of infectious diseases. For example, while the city's hospitals and doctors are trained to be on the alert for patterns of diseases, so far, anthrax cases have come in isolated cases. Since Sept. 11, the number of hospitals making detailed electronic logs of illness patterns among patients has almost doubled.
Further, anthrax symptoms at first mirror the flu, and so its victims have tended not to seek medical help until they are very ill, which makes treatment less effective. And while physicians have been schooled in what to look for in patients who come in with flu symptoms, health care officials concede that as recently as last week, they were discouraging people from running to the emergency room with every little ache. 
Now, as hospitals continue to gear up for possible chemical and biological attacks, they have also had to step up surveillance of their own employees. For example, workers at Memorial Sloan-Kettering Cancer Center who come down with symptoms that resemble the flu are now extensively interviewed, and doctors are prepared to X-ray, take blood samples from and give antibiotics to anyone they suspect has been exposed to anthrax. The hospital had already taken nasal swabs of 250 employees who worked in the Midtown office building shared with Gov. George E. Pataki, where slight traces of anthrax were found two weeks ago.
"There is a move now to provide a much more thorough work-up" on people who come to hospitals with flu symptoms, said Susan C. Waltman, general counsel for the Greater New York Hospital Association, "and that could extend to hospital workers." The trade group and the mayor are encouraging people to get flu shots.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Employees of Manhattan Eye, Ear and Throat Hospital waited for testing and antibiotics at Lenox Hill Hospital yesterday after inhalation anthrax was diagnosed in a woman who worked with them. (Marilynn K. Yee/The New York Times)(pg. A1); F.B.I. agents trying to find the source of the anthrax that sickened a Bronx woman interviewed her neighbors yesterday, seeking details about her. (Edward Keating/The New York Times)(pg. B4)

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS; 
Vivendi of France Reports A Strong Financial Quarter

BYLINE:  By SETH SCHIESEL 

 BODY:
Bucking the trend of gloom that has settled over much of the media industry, Vivendi Universal S.A. of France, Europe's biggest media company, announced strong quarterly financial results yesterday.
 The company reported a 24 percent increase in revenue from its media operations, which include Universal Studios and Universal Music and the French pay television service Canal+. The results for the company, which does not yet fully use American-style accounting methods, did not include its water-processing and liquor businesses. 
 Compared with the other media giants, Vivendi has been buffered from the poor advertising environment and the general slowing of the United States economy. Those problems have already hurt the results of AOL Time Warner, the News Corporation and Viacom, which are all significantly reliant on advertising revenue and derive a large portion of their revenue from the United States. 
By contrast, Vivendi generates less than 5 percent of its sales from advertising and generates only about 35 percent of its revenue in the United States.
Vivendi said yesterday that its third-quarter revenue was 7.3 billion euros, or about $6.6 billion, based on current exchange rates. The results included the effect of acquisitions and spinoffs, like the company's $2.2 billion acquisition of Houghton Mifflin, the educational publisher, last summer.
The company's earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization, often referred to as cash flow and the most commonly watched financial measure for media companies, increased by 90 percent, to 1.5 billion euros, or about $1.35 billion.
As a French company, Vivendi does not report its results in full accordance with United States accounting principles. Nor does the company report per-share figures. As a result, financial analysts in the United States had not estimated what Vivendi's results would be, according to Thomson Financial/First Call, a firm that polls analysts. Vivendi intends to begin reporting its results in line with United States accounting standards next year.
Vivendi Universal was formed last year when Vivendi, traditionally a French conglomerate, acquired Seagram Ltd. and its Universal media units, and separately acquired Canal+. 
Vivendi has been trying to unload Seagram's liquor business. But a deal to sell that unit to the European spirits companies Diageo and Pernod Ricard for $8.15 billion was blocked last week by the Federal Trade Commission on the ground that the arrangement would give Diageo too much power in the rum market.
Yesterday, however, Vivendi executives said that the companies were in discussions with regulators over how to win their approval and that the companies were confident that the sale would be completed by the end of the year.
Excluding the impact of acquisitions and spin-offs -- what might be called results from continuing operations in American financial parlance -- Vivendi's revenue increased by about 8 percent in the third quarter, compared with the period a year earlier. Excluding the effect of deals, the company's Ebitda increased by 36 percent over the year-earlier quarter.
Especially in an economic environment as unsettled as the current one, those were robust results. One of Vivendi's main engines appeared to be its television and film unit, where hits including "Jurassic Park III" and "American Pie 2" helped send the division's revenue up by 10 percent and Ebitda up by 73 percent.
"Having the highest resiliency and lowest sensitivity to a recessionary environment explains our ability to outperform most of our peers," Jean-Marie Messier, Vivendi's chairman, said in a statement. 
The company said that in the first three quarters, it had already exceeded its full-year target for financial synergies between Vivendi and Seagram. Mr. Messier also said the company was poised to meet its 2001 financial goals of 10 percent revenue growth and 35 percent Ebitda growth.

HEADLINE: Conseco Posts Loss And Lowers Outlook For 4th Quarter 

BYLINE:  Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: CARMEL, Ind., Oct. 30 

 BODY:
Conseco, the finance and insurance firm, posted a loss for a second quarter, as it wrote down $471 million of junk bonds, mortgage debt and other assets. The company also said today that it had cut its fourth-quarter forecast. 
 Conseco's net loss narrowed to $410.6 million, or $1.21 a share, from $489.5 million, or $1.50, a year earlier. The loss marked the latest setback for the chairman, Gary Wendt, who has struggled to increase profits by cutting 5,000 jobs and selling assets. 
 Shares of Conseco, which have fallen more than 80 percent from a high on May 3 of $20.20, slid 32 cents, to $3.72.. 
Excluding the write-offs and realized gains and losses, Conseco's operating results missed estimates, hurt by a slowing economy that caused a rise in defaults on loans to people who pay their debts late or have spotty credit. The slowdown and equity market declines also pared Conseco's assets under management and fee income and dampened demand for variable annuities. Conseco, which is based in Indianapolis, had operating earnings in the quarter of $60.6 million, or 18 cents a share, compared with $40.8 million, or 12 cents, a year earlier. The results were below the estimate of 20 cents a share, according to a survey by Thomson Financial/First Call. 
Mr. Wendt said in a statement that Conseco's fourth-quarter operating earnings would be in the range of 17 cents to 20 cents a share. That will mean operating earnings of 72 cents to 75 cents for the year, down from a previous estimate of 80 to 95 cents. 
"I am disappointed that our original earnings expectations turned out to be too optimistic," Mr. Wendt said. Conseco is about 12 months behind schedule on its turnaround because of the slowing economy, he said.

HEADLINE: Law Firm May Lay Off 10% of Associates 

 BODY:
Shearman & Sterling, a law firm based in New York with about 1,000 lawyers in 18 offices worldwide, has quietly informed employees that as many as 10 percent of its associates might be laid off because of the slowing economy, a person close to the firm said yesterday. A majority of the lawyers who will be affected work on corporate transactions, this person said. 
 The announcement makes Shearman one of the first prominent New York firms to acknowledge that it has been significantly affected by spending cutbacks at its corporate clients over the last year, although partners at several other law firms have said privately that they are seeing steep revenue declines as well. 
 The affected associates will receive three months' notice and assistance in finding other employment, a spokesman for the firm, Edward J. Burke, said. Mr. Burke said that the firm would still finish the year larger than it began it because of this fall's influx of law school graduates.
