HEADLINE: U.S. Accuses The Japanese Of Inflexibility On Trade Talks 

BYLINE:  By JOSEPH KAHN 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30 

 BODY:
Using unusually blunt language to pressure a leading ally, the United States trade representative, Robert B. Zoellick, said today that Japan's pursuit of its own "narrow-minded interests" threatened to obstruct efforts to forge agreement at a long-planned world trade summit meeting.
 While the United States and the European Union have worked together to draft an agenda for global trade talks scheduled to take place in Doha, Qatar, next week, Japan, the world's second-largest economy, has sat on the sidelines, hobbled by its own domestic "paralysis," Mr. Zoellick said. 
 "Japan has just said no to everything in this process," Mr. Zoellick said in a question-and-answer session after an address on trade issues here. "I have been, frankly, extremely disappointed by the lack of Japanese -- I wouldn't expect leadership -- but even respectable followership." 
Mr. Zoellick's comments were clearly part of an American strategy to soften the Japanese stance as trade ministers scramble to make progress on divisive issues, including trade in agricultural products, before the World Trade Organization talks formally commence. But Mr. Zoellick was also expressing concern that negotiations could collapse just as they did two years ago in Seattle, where fights between rich and poor nations and violent street protests sunk hopes of reducing trade barriers.
"If other countries refuse to cooperate and compromise, we cannot compel a result," Mr. Zoellick said.
Pointed criticism of a major ally is rare in diplomatic circles, and the effort to single out Japan as an obstacle comes at an especially sensitive time. The United States has been urging Japan to help combat terrorists by sending money to Pakistan, a front-line ally, and rescinding a ban on deploying Japanese soldiers abroad.
Japan recently agreed to lift sanctions it imposed on Pakistan after that country became a nuclear power, clearing a path for more aid and debt relief. Its Parliament voted this week to allow Japanese troops to serve in noncombat roles abroad.
The White House on Monday praised Japan for taking that rare step. But Japanese officials complain privately that the concession received too little attention in Washington.
Claire Buchan, a White House spokeswoman, said today that the administration's policy toward Japan had not changed and that it had always maintained a "constructive and candid" relationship with that country.
Mr. Zoellick was not specific about why he felt Japan had stalled negotiations. But Bush administration officials say that Japan has repeatedly declined to endorse the idea of negotiating freer trade for farm goods, the highest American trade priority. Japan, struggling to re-ignite an economy that has not had sustained growth in a decade, has balked at challenging its politically powerful and elaborately protected rice farmers.
Japanese officials acknowledge that they have so far refused to compromise on farm goods. But one official today called Mr. Zoellick's comments "surprisingly harsh," adding that both the United States and the European Union had core trade positions that they were unwilling to compromise ahead of the trade summit meeting.
"They have their strong domestic constituencies and we have ours," a Japanese trade official said. "They do not want to offend these constituencies before the negotiations are complete. That is also our position."
Yet the fear is that ministers will travel to Qatar with too many such disputes left wide open. Trade negotiators fear unscripted debates much as trial lawyers hesitate to ask questions with uncertain answers; reaching agreement is easier when the differences are narrow and clearly defined.
Ministers are also worried because they will have to confront that task in an uncertain environment. Trade negotiators and lobbyists are complaining about the decision to hold the trade talks in Qatar despite the war in Afghanistan and threats against Westerners in the Middle East. The location was chosen last year, and Qatar resisted entreaties to move the talks someplace else after Sept. 11.
The United States and other major nations have announced that they will arrive with a relatively small contingent of negotiators. Many delegates say they want to wrap up the talks quickly.
The most recent draft texts indicate that leading countries are still far apart on perhaps half a dozen issues going into the summit meeting, despite efforts by both the United States and the European Union to settle disputes and shorten the agenda.
Among the most contentious issues: The United States, Australia and many developing countries that have comparative advantages in farm goods are pushing hard on agricultural subsidies against Japanese, and to a lesser extent, South Korean and European resistance.
Europe has fought to win more rights to restrict imports for environmental reasons and to make it easier for companies to invest around the world. Japan has pressed for negotiations on what it argues is the abuse of antidumping rules by the United States and other nations that restrict imports they claim are sold below their production cost. Many poor countries are eager to soften trade rules that protect intellectual property, and they want more trading rights for low-end manufactured goods, like textiles.
Bush administration officials have in the past said that they preferred to use white gloves when dealing with Japan, contrasting their approach with the Clinton administration's expressions of frustration. During the Clinton years, top officials pressured Japanese leaders to fix their banks and do more to stimulate their slumping economy.
But President Bush has also made starting a new round of global trade talks a top priority, speaking frequently on the subject in recent days. He and other top officials have said that forging ahead with trade negotiations will solidify the alliance against terrorism and help revive the world economy.
And American officials have come to view Japan as the most intransigent sparring partner, unwilling even to informally signal flexibility to help map areas of potential compromise.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST 

 BODY:
Trump Says He May Miss Bond Interest Payments
With the outlook gloomy for his Atlantic City casinos, Donald Trump said that he might not make nearly $90 million in interest payments due tomorrow on his bonds. He has enough cash, he said, but may try to negotiate with bondholders to reduce the amount owed because his casinos are loaded with debt and confronting increased competition.
 At least some bondholders said they did not take Mr. Trump's threats seriously. They are wondering whether Mr. Trump is trying to take advantage of a decline in bond prices to cut a deal. Market Place. Page C13. 

Vivendi Posts Strong Results
Vivendi Universal, Europe's biggest media company, announced strong quarterly financial results, including a 24 percent increase in revenue from its media operations. C8.

Consumer Confidence Falls Sharply
The Conference Board's consumer confidence index plummeted in October to its lowest level in more than seven years. C4.

Stocks Decrease Broadly Again
Stocks plunged as investors responded to the consumer confidence report. The Dow Jones industrial average fell 147.52 points, or 1.6 percent, to 9,121.98. The Nasdaq composite index dropped 32.11 points, or 1.9 percent, to 1,667.41, and the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index lost 18.51 points, or 1.7 percent, to 1,059.79. C12.

Drug Research Money Is Sought
Drug companies are asking for government contracts to undertake research on bioterror drugs, creating a debate among executives, politicians and activists over how close the industry should get to the government. B10.

Breaking With Corporate America
David L. Steward, chief of World Wide Technology in St. Louis, recalled his break with corporate America. "I had a mortgage, two kids and a wife," he said. "We were living paycheck to paycheck with all the trappings that hold you close to a job." He was able to buy a consulting firm in Kansas City, Mo., with no money down and after about a year, it started making money. "During the struggles," he said, "the one thing I remember was the continued support from my wife." The Boss. C6.

Gillette Introduces a Line of Razors
Gillette introduced a line of blades and razors called Mach3 Turbo that will cost about $9 for a razor and two blades, a 17 percent premium over the current Mach3 line. C4.

Nominee for Safety Commission
President Bush announced that he intended to nominate Harold D. Stratton Jr., a former New Mexico attorney general, to lead the Consumer Product Safety Commission. A13.

Swissair Faces Grounding Again
Another cash shortage is threatening to ground Swissair, this time because subsidiaries that perform aircraft maintenance and handle ticketing and reservations are nearly out of money. World Business, Section W.

AOL Told to Remove Music Player
A federal court has ruled that America Online probably violated a copyright of a software company and ordered AOL to remove the music player feature from the AOL 6.0 software used by more than 10 million customers. C2.

Database Used to Profile Fliers
Airlines are making much more use of a database that has 26 categories for profiling potential terrorists. It also is programmed to choose passengers at random to avoid allegations of bias. Business Travel: Joe Sharkey. C13.

HEADLINE: In Spanish Or English, Debate Has Rowdy Tone 

BYLINE:  By ADAM NAGOURNEY 

 BODY:
In an often rowdy encounter before a hooting, cheering audience, Michael R. Bloomberg and Mark Green offered differing views last night on rebuilding New York, on improving public education and on what the city needed in its next mayor. They poked and jabbed away at each other's credentials and style, and the audience egged them on.
 It was the first of two debates between the candidates -- Mr. Green insisted there be only two -- and it was the first Spanish-language mayoral debate in the history of New York, the organizers said. 
 Mr. Green and Mr. Bloomberg wore earpieces, which served up simultaneous (and often rough) English translations of questions that were posed in Spanish. Their answers were simultaneously translated into Spanish for broadcast on a Spanish radio station, WADO-AM, and Univision 41, the Spanish-language television station. 
Given the broadcast stations that sponsored the debate, the result of a draw from a hat, aides to Mr. Green and Mr. Bloomberg said it was unlikely that many New Yorkers would see or hear more about the debate. It took place on the afternoon of the third game of the World Series, which was played in the Bronx with President Bush in attendance, and in city distressed by the terrorist attack of seven weeks ago, and worried about the potential for another one.
But there was much to see.
Mr. Bloomberg, a Republican, presented himself as an independent-minded business leader who could push the city through difficult years. He painted Mr. Green as a career politician, indebted to special interests, be they builders that contributed to his campaign or unions that endorsed him.
Mr. Bloomberg said that the city needed to make changes in the way it regulates construction and turned to Mr. Green. "How can you do that when you also take $700,000 from the the real estate industry? At least I don't have any conflicts at all."
Mr. Green waited until the cheering from Mr. Bloomberg's supporters in the auditorium at New School University settled down. "It is true that a person who tries to buy City Hall has fewer conflicts of interest," he said. "I concede the point. I'm not trying to win an auction. I'm trying to earn an election."
For his part, Mr. Green, a Democrat, portrayed Mr. Bloomberg as a wealthy, politically ungrounded novice. He noted that his opponent had no experience in government, and he mentioned frequently that Mr. Bloomberg was spending huge sums of money to win the mayoralty.
He suggested that Mr. Bloomberg had displayed a lack of conviction by quitting the Democratic Party to run as a Republican, and mocked his explanation that he had done that because the Republican Party had "invited" him to run, while the Democrats had not.
"I have been, I am, and I will be a proud Democrat, and I didn't have to change my parties or my principles to run for mayor," Mr. Green said. 
He added: 'You didn't have to wait for an invitation. You switched party in order to run for mayor. And I think that is treating principles rather cavalierly."
This time it was Mr. Bloomberg's turn to wait for the cheering of Mr. Green's coterie of supporters to fade away. "It has nothing to do with principles," he said. "All it has to do with is how you get a chance to serve."
All this led to a series of exchanges that grew in intensity as the debate rolled on.
Mr. Bloomberg, noting that Mr. Green had won the support of most of the city's major unions in the general election, said: "You've got to remember the unions are there to represent their members and get the best financial deal for their members. If I were a union leader, I would like to have Mark Green across the table rather than Michael Bloomberg."
Mr. Green looked toward Mr. Bloomberg and noted that he had visited the headquarters of the city's powerful health care workers and met privately for more than an hour with its leader, Dennis Rivera.
"Is it sour grapes that you sat down with Dennis Rivera and that he didn't endorse you?" he said. "Why did you sit down with him if you didn't want his support? I am real proud that in the first time in 20 years, the firefighters, the police officers and 14 uniformed service unions are all endorsing the Democrat for mayor. It's not something I'm going to apologize for."
At that, Mr. Green's contingent responded with rowdy shouts of "Tell him, Mark!"
The debate brought out some fairly clear differences between these two men on issues.
Mr. Bloomberg said that the financial services industry had already begun to flee downtown New York before the attack of Sept. 11, and that the city needed to figure out a new way to promote economic development in the area. "What we have to find for the World Trade Center site is a whole new industry that wants to come there, that wants to be there," he said.
Mr. Green disputed that assessment, and asserted that it would undermine New York's economy not to replace it. "The heart of financial services was in downtown Manhattan," he said. 
Both candidates repeatedly said that aside from the rebuilding, the public school system remained the critical problem facing New York's next government. "Education, education, education, education," Mr. Bloomberg said repeatedly. 
Mr. Bloomberg said the city should increase salaries for the existing teachers to avoid an exodus of experienced school personnel. "If you don't have enough money to do everything, the first and foremost thing is to pay our teachers that have the experience," he said. "We keep losing teachers, and our focus ought to be on retaining teachers."
Repeatedly, Mr. Green said the priority should instead be hiring more teachers to reduce class size in the lower grades, noting that Mr. Bloomberg had said earlier in the campaign that it was unrealistic to expect to be able to reduce class size. "I think it's really defeatist to say that we can't shrink class size and we can't hire more teachers," he said.
They also argued over Mr. Bloomberg's contention that most neighborhoods did not want new high schools. "What we have to do is find ways to have high schools without destroying the neighborhoods," he said. "That's why I think building a bunch of high schools on Governors Island would be a great idea."
As he talked, Mr. Green spoke over him, suggesting neighborhoods -- the Upper East Side and Kingsbridge in The Bronx -- that would welcome a school.
Mr. Bloomberg challenged Mr. Green to account for his toughly worded attacks on Fernando Ferrer, his opponent in the Democratic primary, who was sitting in the audience for the debate. Mr. Bloomberg listed some criticisms Mr. Green had made of Mr. Ferrer, and asked: "How can you go then and ask for his support?"
To which Mr. Green responded: "This from a man who a week ago said he wouldn't be divisive." He said the exchanges with Mr. Ferrer were part of the back-and-forth that takes place in a contested campaign.
At another point, an audience member asked Mr. Green whether he would name the Rev. Al Sharpton, who was one of Mr. Ferrer's most prominent supporters, to a post in City Hall should he win. Mr. Green grinned widely at the question.
"I've never said who I would ask to leave the Giuliani administration or whom I might appoint," he said. "Let me say from the point of political obviousness, I am certainly not going to start with the Rev. Sharpton."
At several points, Mr. Bloomberg sounded more like a Democrat than Mr. Green did. He said he would support paying city employees more, within the constraints of the city budget. He said that, in an effort to bring more minorities into City Hall, he would tell his commissioners, "You get us diversity or you're not going to work for me."
In a discussion of homelessness, he said: "We can not let people sleep on the streets. We have to get them into accommodations that are clean and safe, and then find the way to pay for it, no matter what it takes."

HEADLINE: Top Democrats Are Confident Of a Victory by McGreevey 

BYLINE:  By DAVID M. HERSZENHORN 

DATELINE: TRENTON, Oct. 30 

 BODY:
In a signal that Democrats are increasingly confident about winning the New Jersey governor's race, the national party chairman, Terry McAuliffe, campaigned at the State House here today, not for James E. McGreevey, but for three legislative candidates from Mercer County. 
 Mr. McAuliffe said his party would "take no chances and make sure that we have a massive get-out-the-vote operation" in the governor's race. But Mr. McAuliffe and other Democrats gleefully pointed to a new poll released today by Quinnipiac University showing Mr. McGreevey leading Bret D. Schundler, his Republican opponent, by 17 percentage points among likely voters. The telephone poll, conducted Oct. 10 to 15, surveyed 1,140 registered voters and identified 742 of them as likely to go to the polls. The margin of sampling error for likely voters was 4 percent. 
 With just one week until Election Day, the results of the poll were clearly discouraging for Mr. Schundler. In recent weeks, his campaign has fired off news releases after every new poll to proclaim that the gap between the candidates was closing. It did not issue any response to the Quinnipiac poll today. 
Mr. Schundler himself tried to laugh off the results. After an anti-tax rally this morning on the steps of the State House, he said, "I think all those people who want lower taxes, if they want them you better get out there and vote for me, because we need your support." 
Pressed about the 17-point margin, Mr. Schundler said, "I think, everybody, you better vote twice then, maybe voting once won't be enough, so you better vote early and often." Then, sounding optimistic, he added, "It just shows that we have got to have more people vote for me on Election Day, and then we'll still be able to win." 
Mr. Schundler's aides said today that he expected to get a lift from a letter by President Bush that is to be mailed to 600,000 New Jersey voters. Mr. Schundler had hoped that Mr. Bush would visit New Jersey to campaign with him, but the White House has avoided political events to maintain bipartisan support for the war on terrorism. 
"Bret has laid out a positive agenda to cut property taxes, improve New Jersey schools and revive the economy in the New York-New Jersey metropolitan area," Mr. Bush's letter said. "I know Bret Schundler can do all of these things because of his outstanding work as mayor of Jersey City." 
The Schundler campaign also released a new 30-second television advertisement featuring Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani of New York. "In this crisis, New Jersey just can't afford to raise taxes," Mr. Giuliani says in the ad. "Now more than ever, you need a proven leader who will protect jobs and keep families safe. You need my friend Bret Schundler." 
The ad also seeks to combat one of Mr. McGreevey's most damaging attacks on Mr. Schundler throughout the campaign -- the assertion that he supports a law that would legalize concealed weapons. "And I'll tell you this," Mr. Guiliani says in the ad, "Bret will not change current gun laws -- he never wanted to." 
The Republican National Committee today began a nearly $1 million television advertising campaign for the final week of the race. The ad supports all Republican candidates in New Jersey and does not mention Mr. Schundler by name.
Mr. McAuliffe, the Democratic national chairman, said the effort by national Republicans was coming too late. "They know they can't win the governorship," he said. "They are worried about the Senate and the Assembly." 
This afternoon, Mr. McGreevey picked up the endorsement of the Black Ministers Council of New Jersey, an influential group that represents some 600 churches. In 1997, the council endorsed Gov. Christie Whitman over Mr. McGreevey, and it had hesitated to endorse Mr. McGreevey this year because of differences over racial profiling. 
Mr. McGreevey has voiced support for a law banning profiling, but he has opposed a ban on consent searches by law enforcement officers. Such a ban is a main priority of the ministers council. Nevertheless, the Rev. Reginald T. Jackson, the group's executive director, said the ministers had decided to endorse Mr. McGreevey. "Jim McGreevey today is stronger, more prepared and more self-assured than he was four years ago," Mr. Jackson said. "Jim McGreevey is up to the task."

HEADLINE: Reckonings; 
The One-Eyed Man

BYLINE:  By PAUL KRUGMAN 

 BODY:
Somewhere I read that to really understand legislation you have to look for the clause giving special consideration to one-eyed bearded men with a limp -- that is, you have to look for the provision that turns a bill ostensibly serving a public purpose into a giveaway for some special interest. 
 Most of the commentary about the "stimulus" bill passed by the House last week focuses on the huge benefits it lavishes on giant corporations. But that doesn't tell us much about the specific interests being served. What's good for corporate America is good for General Motors; it would be hard to devise a bill that consists mainly of corporate giveaways without giving a lot of money to the biggest companies. To understand what the bill is really about, you have to look at the big payoffs to not-so-big companies. 
 One piece of the bill is custom-designed to benefit a small group of multinational financial firms. Another is clearly there for the sake of certain health insurors. But the most remarkable thing is how much of the benefit from repeal of the alternative minimum tax -- a measure that is also included in the Bush administration's supposed stimulus plan, and which seems to be one of the administration's key priorities -- goes to companies that are not all that big.
For example, it's not too surprising that calculations by Citizens for Tax Justice show General Motors, with its 380,000 workers, getting a check for $800 million. But it's quite amazing that TXU (formerly Dallas Power and Light), a company with only 16,000 employees, would get a check for $600 million. And there are a number of medium-sized companies that, like TXU, are in line for surprisingly big benefits. These companies include ChevronTexaco, Enron, Phillips Petroleum, IMC Global and CMS Energy. What do they have in common?
Well, they tend to be in the energy or mining businesses; and they tend to be based in or near Texas. In other words, the one-eyed bearded man with a limp looks a lot like Dick Cheney.
There is almost certainly a lot of overlap between the companies that would derive large benefits from alternative minimum tax repeal and those that would have received large subsidies under the energy plan devised by Mr. Cheney's task force. You may remember that the administration, in apparent defiance of the law, refused to make the records of that task force's meetings available to Congress; that's one of those issues that seems to have been dropped after Sept. 11. 
And I guess it's superfluous to point out that the big winners in all this seem to be companies that gave large, one-sided donations to the Republican Party in the last election. (This is not to suggest that Democrats are any less susceptible to the influence of money.) 
To me, the story of the Bush administration is starting to look like the plot of "Victor/Victoria." First we had a candidate who was supposed to be a moderate. Then we learned, or thought we learned, that this was a mask; he was really a hard-line conservative who pretended to be a moderate in order to gain office. 
But the latest economic proposals from the administration, like the Cheney energy plan, don't look as if they came from serious free-marketeers. They don't make sense in terms of either demand-side or supply-side economics, but they do give a lot of money to certain companies. So maybe ideology was just another mask for someone who was really the candidate of corporations -- not corporations in general, but a small group of companies with a quite specific set of business interests -- and who is only pretending to be a hard-line conservative who pretended to be a moderate in order to gain office.
It's an interesting and all too plausible picture. But it's a picture that most people will never see on their TV, and that many people would refuse to accept no matter how strong the evidence. That, of course, is what makes the whole thing possible. In the land of the blind, the one-eyed bearded man with a limp is king.
In last Wednesday's column I said the "original" war bonds were issued during World War II. In fact, war bonds were also issued during the Civil War and World War I. I don't think this affects the argument.

HEADLINE: Green Says City Faces Fiscal Crisis As Bad as in 70's 

BYLINE:  By MICHAEL COOPER 

 BODY:
Mark Green warned yesterday that the $4-to-6-billion deficit that the city is expected to face next year is its greatest fiscal crisis since it nearly went bankrupt in the 1970's, and he said that he has "the experience in government to make hard choices, tell the public hard truths and take the heat."
 For the most part, Mr. Green avoided talking about specific cuts that he would make if he was elected mayor, except to reiterate his support for a temporary hiring freeze on all city workers other than teachers, police officers and firefighters. Although he still spoke about paying teachers more and hiring more of them to shrink class sizes, he made it clear that such goals would now take longer to achieve. 
 "Look, if the fiscal situation worsens and we have the worst fiscal crisis in a half-century, of course terrible things would be on the triage table," Mr. Green said during a 45-minute breakfast interview yesterday with reporters and editors of The New York Times. "And if you'd like to ask me, one by one, what they are and what I would do, I'd say everything's on the table because the goal is to make the city economically and fiscally whole." 
The interview with Mr. Green, 56, the Democratic nominee, was a stark reminder of the extent to which the attack on the World Trade Center has transformed the city and the race to govern it. The last time Mr. Green came for a breakfast interview, on Aug. 1, he spent a great deal of time enumerating his differences with Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani and discussing political issues that then seemed important.
Yesterday, by contrast, he found himself praising Mayor Giuliani for his handling of the crisis and describing his plans for more random searches by the police to keep the city safe from terrorist attacks and for building up a health network that could swiftly distribute antibiotics in the event of a large-scale biological attack. Instead of laying out plans for how to spend the increased revenues generated by the leasing of the World Trade Center, as he had in August, Mr. Green spoke yesterday about the rebuilding of the site.
And he cautioned that the nation should not overreact by detaining hundreds of people of Arab descent without cause, the way it incarcerated Japanese-Americans during World War II.
Still, Mr. Green, who sometimes describes himself as a "pathological optimist," held out hope that the city would be able to weather the looming economic crisis and rebuild itself. 
"Every mayor has lean years and fat years, and I don't doubt that the 108th mayor may be facing a couple of very lean years early in the hope and expectation that a nearly $400 billion private gross domestic economy called New York City will shake off even this economic assault and attack on our buildings and people and gain its footing in Years 3 to 8," he said.
During a wide-ranging discussion, Mr. Green promised to run an administration that would make information about the government readily available to the public; spoke of giving police officers on the beat greater discretion about when to make arrests in some cases; said he was amenable to privatizing some parks services, like tree-pruning; and left open the possibility of ending the city's long practice of pattern bargaining on labor contracts.
Mr. Green, who has sometimes battled a reputation for glibness, weighed his words carefully yesterday, clearly trying to sound more like a future mayor of New York than a former panelist on CNN's "Crossfire." He spoke like a man who expects to be governing soon, even using the term "mayor-elect."
At one point he explained why he had refused during the campaign to discuss what raises or contract provisions he planned to offer the city's unions by saying, "At the risk of credentialing the attacks on my self-confidence and arrogance, since I thought I would be elected at the start of this campaign, I resisted making excessive promises in the belief that I'd have to keep them."
The only criticism he made of Mr. Giuliani yesterday was that his administration was not forthcoming enough with information -- something Mr. Green pledged to change, even if it might damage him politically. "Trust me, I will be so transparent as compared to the Giuliani administration when it comes to disclosing numbers and flaws, my mayor's management report will be used by all of you to write articles about how I'm screwing up as mayor because I think it's important to run an open democracy," he said.
Mr. Green said he would appoint a deputy police commissioner for counterterrorism. The new powers given to the police and to federal authorities to try to prevent terrorism, like the increase in random stops, should be balanced with new discretion, he said. "I want to give more professional discretion to the cop on the beat to decide, given all the circumstances, whether to prosecute a 19-year-old at a checkpoint who happens to have an exposed beer can in a car, at a time when the city should be focusing on anticipating, preventing or avoiding a terrorist attack," he said.
And he said that, faced with a compelling enough reason, he would be open to ending pattern bargaining, the city's practice of using a contract with one municipal union to set the pattern for all others. "I'd have to be open to it if we couldn't hire cops and teachers because in the regional economy Nassau and Suffolk were hiring them away because they're paying starting salaries a third higher than we," he said.
Mr. Green did take a couple of swipes at his opponent, Michael R. Bloomberg, the Republican nominee, but he never mentioned him by name. At one point, describing himself as a known quantity in New York politics, he added, "I am no novice who has suddenly been born full-blown into public life, defining myself in speeches in the election year."
Later, he called attention to his disagreement with Mr. Bloomberg over what kind of governmental entity should be responsible for rebuilding Lower Manhattan. Mr. Green said that the job should be done by a newly created authority that is run by the state and the city together. Mr. Bloomberg has said he believes that the existing city agencies and governmental authorities should be able to do it, a position that Mr. Green derided yesterday as "jurisdictionally unsophisticated." 
But Mr. Green resisted introspection when he was asked how the experience of running for mayor had changed him. "You know, most biologists and psychologists will tell you that you're basically formed in utero, either in utero or in the first six months," he said. "Well, in the last 55 and a half years since then, yes, I've learned a lot. But never more than in the last year."
Asked specifically what he had learned, though, he spoke of the desperation that parents in poor communities feel because their children are falling behind in school and lack health insurance, issues he has worked on for much of his career; of the problems small businesses face in navigating the city's bureaucracy; of the fact that there is a genre of music called Texas swing; and of just how diverse the city of New York has become.
When pressed again, Mr. Green explained that he had always known that the city was diverse, but that the experience of visiting so many people in so many communities had brought the point home as never before. "You know that the water goes down at Niagara Falls," he said, "but when you stand at the falls you just pick up a lot more."

HEADLINE: A NATION CHALLENGED: NEWS ANALYSIS; 
Walking a Wary Line

BYLINE:  By TODD S. PURDUM 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30 

 BODY:
President Bush was in the Bronx, home of one of the latest victims of inhalation anthrax, watching the World Series. Vice President Dick Cheney was in a secure location to preserve the chain of succession in the face of the second nationwide terrorism alert in three weeks.
 And where should Americans be this Halloween, balanced between frightful warnings of a new reality and injunctions to resume an old normalcy that seems ever more remote? The government's own message was, again, mixed: Be wary, but go on with workaday routines. 
 "It's a difficult and fine line that we walk," Tom Ridge, the White House's director of homeland security, said of the F.B.I.'s latest, unspecified warning -- based on what it calls credible but vague intelligence about more terrorist attacks. "But I think America understands and, hopefully, appreciates that when there's that kind of information available to us, we just share it with America, as incomplete as it might be." 
The White House press secretary, Ari Fleischer, acknowledging the tension inherent in keeping one's guard and one's chin up at once, told a reporter in his briefing this morning: "You say it's not normal, and you're correct. Neither is war."
In fact, as Raymond W. Kelly, the former New York police commissioner, said in an interview, it seems clearer than ever that "we're learning as we go along."
"It's hard to say what the happy medium is," he said. "Obviously, there are legitimate concerns. The government has something, we don't know what it is. It's partly a matter of getting information out that they think is important. But it's also that they don't want to be caught holding the bag if something bad happens."
Members of Congress, alert to the worries of their constituents, said the trick was how to keep the populace concerned about future emergencies without alarming people, a challenge that one Republican official called "a dicey deal."
After Mr. Ridge addressed separate party caucuses this afternoon, Senator Christopher J. Dodd, Democrat of Connecticut, said that a vague public warning "provokes its own level of panic," and that he hoped Mr. Ridge took away "a stronger sense that there are people who are disappointed about how this is being managed."
Across the government, optimistic exhortations blended with pessimistic analyses, as they have for days. 
Mr. Ridge said the mail was safe, "by and large," but the postmaster general, John E. Potter, told Congress that it would take billions of dollars to clean up contamination already found and devise safer ways to screen and sort the mail.
Mr. Ridge found comfort in the fact that about 25 billion letters had passed through the mails since the first anthrax case was identified in Florida almost a month ago and that only one person sick with inhalation anthrax, a 61-year-old hospital worker from the Bronx, had no apparent connection to a post office or bulk mailroom.
But health officials called her case troubling precisely because it did not fit the pattern and raised the possibility that the bacteria was being distributed in some new way.
President Bush has relentlessly urged Americans to fly and buy to help the economy, but his secretary of transportation, Norman Y. Mineta, told state and local transportation officials today that, despite improvements in airport security since Sept. 11, many deficiencies remained, adding, "This is intolerable."
Bush officials have made it clear that they do not take the frightening effect of the F.B.I.'s broad warning lightly, and they have debated whether to issue such warnings without specific information as to times and targets. 
Mr. Ridge acknowledged the troubling moral of the "boy who cried wolf" but added, "I think you can fairly assume that the experts view this tied in, this information somehow related to Al Qaeda or bin Laden, or else we wouldn't have ramped it up." 
No attacks occurred after the last such general warning, on Oct. 11, but Attorney General John Ashcroft said it was possible that the warning helped to avert any such attacks. And, as Mr. Fleischer noted, it would be impossible to keep a warning to 18,000 state and local law enforcement agencies from the public.
"When it comes to the balance of going on with normal lives and adjusting to a heightened state of security alert, the American people get it," Mr. Fleischer said. "They do understand that it's possible to do both."
"Nobody likes it," he added, "but I've seen nothing that suggests the country doesn't understand it."
The latest New York Times/CBS News poll suggests that public opinion is mixed, with slightly less than half the respondents saying the government was telling people all they need to know about anthrax attacks, and 50 percent saying it was not. 
Public anxiety about possible further terrorist attacks has risen steadily in recent weeks, with 56 percent now saying they regard such attacks as likely, compared with 36 percent in late September. But 71 percent of respondents said they were not very concerned about an attack occurring where they lived; 91 percent said they thought it unnecessary to be tested for anthrax.
And at Yankee Stadium tonight, the normal routines continued as the president threw out the first pitch. But there was also a massive police retinue and a sober new symbol of heightened vigilance: As fans streamed into the House That Ruth Built, they had to pass through metal detectors for the first time.

HEADLINE: Hard-Edged Insurance Chief Is Taking On a New and Public Face 

BYLINE:  By JOSEPH B. TREASTER 

 BODY:
Before Sept. 11, insurers thought the risk of a passenger jet causing major damage in a crash into buildings and people on the ground was so remote that they did not even bother to charge extra for the coverage.
 But shortly after the terrorist attacks, they withdrew all but a tiny bit of the insurance. As a result, banks and aircraft leasing companies threatened to ground the airlines unless they were covered. 
 The insurers had lost billions in the attacks, and most were in no mood to take more chances. But Maurice R. Greenberg, the chief executive of the American International Group, saw an opportunity. By charging enough -- he figured $1.85 a passenger would do the trick for about $1 billion in coverage -- not only would his insurance company make a profit, but this would also help keep the airlines flying. 
"We're in the risk business," he said. "I felt a responsibility to come up with a solution. Maybe I'm a genius; maybe I'm just too dumb to see it's too risky to do."
No one has ever accused Mr. Greenberg of being dumb. And while there is certainly risk involved, Mr. Greenberg -- widely known in the industry as Hank -- did not turn A.I.G. into one of the biggest and most aggressive insurers without instinctively calculating his chances of success.
Even as Mr. Greenberg is again jumping into the kind of situation that often intimidates his competitors, he is also leading the industry's charge in Washington for the government to serve as insurer of last resort in any future terror assaults.
"He's always been an insurance figure," said John Oxendine, the chief insurance regulator in Georgia, "but now he's becoming a public figure."
Since the attacks, which are expected to cost A.I.G. about $820 million in payments on claims, Mr. Greenberg has met twice with President Bush and has been commuting to Washington in A.I.G.'s corporate jet to lobby members of Congress with the industry's message. Insurers may have $300 billion in capital, plenty to cover the claims from Sept. 11, he argues, but that huge pot of money could soon be eaten up in a few more terrorist disasters.
His strategy, which has become the industry's as well, has been to emphasize the role insurance plays in underpinning banking, real estate, manufacturing and other vital industries, and arguing that government help for insurers is really help for the economy as a whole.
"Hank has been like the Paul Revere on this," said Senator Charles E. Schumer, Democrat of New York, who has known Mr. Greenberg for years. "He came to Washington and said this is a huge problem, a national problem, not just something in the insurance industry."
Events since the attacks have only added to Mr. Greenberg's sense of urgency. Even before A.I.G. could offer its new insurance product for airlines, Congress and many European governments were forced to come up with temporary financial guarantees to keep the airlines flying.
But more than 100 airlines around the world did not receive such assistance, and they became the first customers for Mr. Greenberg's new coverage. By doing so, he gained a leap on his rivals for what promises to be a permanent feature of future airline insurance. Many of the carriers now covered by guarantees are likely to come to him and his coalition of a dozen other insurers when the government measures expire.
At the moment, he is as edgy about the airline coverage as a poker player with a good hand who knows that others still have several cards to show. "You have to look at how this is going to play out over time," he said. "You don't judge the success of a thing like this in the first few weeks."
Some industry executives say Mr. Greenberg had another incentive for developing the airline coverage: A.I.G. owns a big company that leases planes to the airlines. But Mr. Greenberg said his leasing company did not need new coverage. "All but one aircraft we lease was covered by the government programs," he said.
At 76, Mr. Greenberg is almost as trim and feisty as the day in 1944 he charged ashore as a Signal Corps sergeant in the Normandy invasion with a unit of Army Rangers. And in many ways he runs his company like a military operation, with no doubt about who is in charge.
"He's a C.E.O. who knows the big concepts and strategies as well as the details," said Ron Ferguson, the chairman of General Re, one of the world's largest companies that insures other insurance companies. "He's everywhere. He's an absolute workaholic. He has facts and opinions on everything, and they're well founded."
As a boss, he can be brutal. "He can border on being abusive with his language and the way he treats you," said Bob O'Connell, who spent nine years at A.I.G. before becoming chief executive of the Massachusetts Mutual Financial Group. Yet he "can make it eminently clear to you," Mr. O'Connell said, "that he cares about you as a person, about your development, about your family."
"And both sides are genuine," Mr. O'Connell continued. "It's not like he's a tough guy pretending to be nice, or a nice guy pretending to be tough. He's genuinely both."
But when it comes to defending his company against competitors, he is all tough guy. In the long and contentious negotiations leading up to China's acceptance into the World Trade Organization, Mr. Greenberg was tenacious in prodding Washington to defend A.I.G.'s special status in China against efforts by the European Union to open the door wider to European insurers.
He strongly advocated China's W.T.O. membership -- something that could only have further endeared him to the Chinese leaders he has cultivated for decades and who have granted A.I.G., founded in China in 1919, licenses to operate eight insurance units in four cities, far more than any competitor.
Over the years, Mr. Greenberg has been a big contributor to both the Republican and Democratic parties as well as a big fund-raiser for the Republicans. But the key to his access in Washington and in other world capitals is A.I.G.'s financial power. The company commands more than $300 billion in assets, making it the largest American insurer and the ninth-largest American corporation in terms of assets.
While there has never been any doubt about A.I.G.'s financial strength, it is monitored, like all insurers, by regulators and rating agencies to be sure it has sufficient reserves to stand behind any claims.
Mr. Greenberg's business strategy differs from that of most other insurers. Instead of pricing insurance low to reap premiums and then to invest them for profit, Mr. Greenberg strives to profit on both his insurance and his investments.
He has pioneered coverage that protects corporations overseas from expropriation and political violence and that pays ransoms for kidnapped executives, and he has led the way in insurance in cyberspace. Just last week he started a company, with Goldman, Sachs and Chubb as his partners, that permits him to capitalize on sharply rising prices for insurance without loading more risk onto A.I.G.
Along the way, Mr. Greenberg has left a few bruises. "There's no question that some people in the industry resent him a little bit," a competitor said. "There's a little jealousy because he's been more successful. They just find him to be a little bit of a bully."
Mr. Greenberg had hoped to pass on control of A.I.G. to one of two sons. But both have left the company, raising questions about who will run A.I.G. after Mr. Greenberg is gone. (He says he has no plans to retire.)
Mr. Greenberg says he expects his successor to come from within A.I.G. But analysts and others say they do not see a likely candidate. "Hank has made the company so vast and complex that it requires somebody of enormous intellectual diversity with the skill to run a wide multiplicity of businesses," Mr. O'Connell said. "You also want somebody of potentially great recognition in the business and shareholder community. And there is no one yet like that, front and center, in A.I.G."
One reason Mr. Greenberg can sell insurance that others dare not, competitors and analysts say, is that he can calculate risk better than most and makes judgments on the basis of a strict quantitative analysis.
"Because he understands risk so well and doesn't let emotions get in the way," an insurance industry executive said, "he can do things that scare most people."

HEADLINE: Sports of The Times; 
Jordan, a Bit Older, Comes Up Short

BYLINE:  By HARVEY ARATON 

 BODY:
Michael Jordan never made it all the way to the podium in the Madison Square Garden rotunda through the tunnel from the arena late yesterday morning after the Washington Wizards' shoot-around. He pulled up short of the area set up for a news conference, the way he would settle for jump shots last night against the Knicks and, in the final count, come up short. 
 "I go in with no preconceived notions, other than to play hard," he had said, hours before he would sluggishly end his three-year absence from the National Basketball Association in a 93-91 loss to the Knicks. "Whatever happens, happens." 
 Jordan was keeping with the serendipitous script that he followed all summer, working out in private with a handful of scrimmage partners and providing occasional progress reports, like Donald Rumsfeld on C-Span. Right to the final detail -- which, not surprisingly, turned out to be some haggle over video game rights -- Jordan and the N.B.A. contrived the drama, as if the decision hadn't been reached for months. As if the opening night schedule made months in advance was some stroke of fate.
When fate really gets involved, it tends to be devilishly fickle. Late in the fourth quarter, when Jordan has routinely tortured the Knicks, he made a key turnover. On unsteady legs, he missed what would have been a game-tying 3-pointer, hitting the front of the rim on an open look from the right wing.
"I guess the difference is that I'm a little bit older than the last time I shot the basketball," he admitted, referring to his 1998 title-winner in Utah in 1998. "I could never find my rhythm."
He is bigger in the upper body, and technically superior to all those fundamentally challenged players around him. At 38, with a cast of hyperactive wannabes, Jordan is part point guard, part floor coach, while trying to rediscover the role of leading man and legend.
"I'm going to get stronger and stronger as the season goes along," he said, recognizing that 19 points on 7 for 21 from the field was not exactly Kobe-esque, much less what New York City was accustomed to seeing of him on the Broadway stage. Playing in the celebrity-laden Garden again evoked pleasant memories of the old battles with the departed Patrick Ewing, Charles Oakley and John Starks. Then again, as Jordan quickly recognized, "I'm not back here with the same guys I was normally with either."
No Scottie. No Rodman. No Phil Jackson, the former sitting Bull, to whom Jordan once pledged his everlasting loyalty, claiming he would never play for any other coach. That was before Jordan got bored with wearing an executive suit and remembered life was always more fun when he modeled the latest sportswear from Nike.
Eventually, the reports last spring quickly derided as farcical began to make more sense. Jordan needed to prove to himself that he could still soar, as he said yesterday, "at the highest level." His Q score and endorsement interests, suffering the effects of no recently recorded game-winning jumpers, needed a boost. 
Having won more championships (six) than Magic Johnson (five), he has returned as a one-man promotion, to the point where they could just list his team as "Michael" in the Atlantic Division standing. His handsome sweat suit yesterday bore no mention of the Wizards, as was the case when he held his first precamp news conference earlier this month. 
Actually, the young Wizards weren't all that bad last night, making an early fourth-quarter run on their own. Don't kid yourself, though. For Jordan, this comeback is about action and accounting, the two things that have always defined him.
The Wizards, in fact, played their final preseason game last week at a casino in Connecticut. Nobody at a league office that once threatened to withhold expansion into Toronto unless the province of Ontario removed basketball betting from its Pro-Line lottery said a word.
The Associated Press story read, in part, "Combining two of his biggest passions -- basketball and gambling -- Jordan helped christen the 10,000-seat arena at the Mohegan Sun Casino, where he has not one but two restaurants that bear his name." The marketing director for the restaurants, Peter Klein, said, "The fact that he's playing his last preseason game was a great, great coincidence."
Sure it was, as much as last night's date with the Knicks was. The N.B.A. is full of emerging new stars and rising young teams, but what sports enterprise during these troubled economic times can't use a shot of nostalgic, feel-good juice? 
Maybe when the rust is removed, Jordan can again be as improvisational on the court as he is predictable off it and that will be enough to carry the Wizards into contention for a low playoff seeding. Wouldn't it be a twist of that aforementioned fate if he deprived the Knicks of their 15th straight playoff appearance? If Marcus Camby doesn't get healthy soon, it's not out of the question, though even Jordan probably wouldn't bet on it, despite claiming yesterday that he expects the Wizards to finish with a plus .500 record.
Whatever the coming months bring, he certainly will be back on the sideline by late May and June, when it matters most. Even in the N.B.A., even with the Jordan marketing machine, there is only so much you can script.

HEADLINE: TRENDS; 
Nerves Are Steady So Far, and Employers Can Take Some Credit

BYLINE:  By DYLAN LOEB McCLAIN

 BODY:
American workers are showing that they are not easily intimidated.
 Terrorist attacks have closed down parts of Washington and New York and caused full-scale alerts at many of the leading news media companies, but around the nation's water coolers, people seem to be taking events in stride. 
 According to a survey by Integra Realty Resources, done after the Sept. 11 attacks, the percentage of employees who were fairly worried about workplace safety was about the same as it was a year ago. 
 In another survey, conducted by the Gallup Organization Oct. 20-21, two-thirds of the respondents expressed little concern about being exposed to anthrax. 
 Part of the credit for such equanimity should go to employers, according to an Oct. 7 survey by the Council of Public Relations Firms. The survey found that about 60 percent of employees said their companies were doing an excellent or good job of communicating with them since Sept. 11. 
 Those numbers are in contrast to a survey conducted by the council in August. Then, with job cuts rising and economic concerns paramount in most people's minds, 54 percent of employees said that companies that had downsized had failed to explain the need for the job cuts or what each company's prospects for survival were. 
 DYLAN LOEB McCLAIN

HEADLINE: To Close the Saudi Tap 

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Re "Drilling for Tolerance," by Thomas L. Friedman (column, Oct. 30): 
 Mr. Friedman argues persuasively for energy conservation and the need to reduce our dependence on Saudi Arabia for energy supplies. 
 There is a simple and effective way to conserve: begin immediately to tax gasoline at rates comparable to the high rates levied by European governments. This will undoubtedly cause much initial economic pain, but that pain will be nothing like what we will endure if we continue to ignore the role Saudi Arabia has played in fostering the growth of Osama bin Laden and his movement, Al Qaeda.

RICHARD L. RUBENSTEIN
Fairfield, Conn., Oct. 30, 2001

HEADLINE: Bush Advisers Say Congress Must Act Now on Economy 

BYLINE:  By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30 

 BODY:
The Bush administration warned today that failure by Congress to pass an economic recovery package would endanger the prospects for a rebound next year, but it offered no compromises to break a partisan deadlock on Capitol Hill.
 In an interview, President Bush's top economic policy advisers said they remained optimistic about reaching a deal based on the administration's proposal for a new round of tax cuts. 
 The advisers said, however, that the consequences for the economy would be serious if the Senate could not bridge the gap between Democratic proposals that focus on helping the unemployed and lower-income workers and Republican plans, like one that passed the House last week calling for extensive tax breaks to businesses.
After an initial burst of bipartisanship following the Sept. 11 terrorist attacks, the two sides have been drifting farther apart for several weeks, even as the economy has continued to deteriorate. 
Members of both parties said there was now a chance that Congress would be unable to agree on an economic package before adjourning, probably around Christmas. Most economists are forecasting that the economy will bounce back by the middle of next year, assuming that Congress and the administration will pass some combination of tax cuts and spending increases to augment the Federal Reserve's continuing campaign of interest rate cuts.
"We are playing with fire if we don't pass a reasonable stimulus," said R. Glenn Hubbard, the chairman of the White House's Council of Economic Advisers.
Lawrence B. Lindsey, Mr. Bush's chief economic adviser, noted that the stock market had risen by more than 400 points since the president proposed a stimulus package of up to $75 billion for the current year. If the expectation built into stock prices and economic forecasts for passage of the stimulus plan is removed, Mr. Lindsey said, "I think there is a significant risk to the economy."
Democrats said that if Mr. Bush was serious about getting a bill passed this year, he would have to be more active in brokering a compromise.
The president "has to step up as well and spend the appropriate time and send the appropriate signals," said Senator Max Baucus, Democrat of Montana, the chairman of the Senate Finance Committee. "Otherwise I fear that those who shout the loudest, who tend to be the most partisan, are going to make this into a partisan exchange."
Mr. Hubbard and Mr. Lindsey, along with Treasury Secretary Paul H. O'Neill and Mitchell E. Daniels Jr., the White House budget director, set out the administration's views on a variety of economic matters in an interview with a small group of reporters. 
They said they were skeptical about the approach emerging in Congress for dealing with the reluctance of insurers to provide coverage against terrorist attacks. 
Under a bipartisan proposal being discussed in the Senate, the government would not step in to help until the insurance industry has paid as much as $10 billion in claims for any terrorist attacks next year. The administration has proposed paying at least 80 percent of any claims from the first dollar next year.
Mr. O'Neill said the $10 billion figure, should it become law, might well lead insurers to set premiums so high that companies would be unable or unwilling to pay, forcing Congress and the administration to revisit the legislation to keep the economy operating.
"If it doesn't work, we're going to have to do something else," Mr. O'Neill said. "Our economy cannot be committed to go into the tank because we failed to provide a risk insurance process that works."
Asked about the budget outlook for next year, Mr. Daniels suggested that the administration was likely to seek savings in many areas to pay for the war against terrorism. 
"The starting point is to err on the side of action with regard to the true emergencies we face," he said. "Everything else will be scrutinized even more carefully than before."
Administration officials played down the partisan eruptions over the economic stimulus package, but on Capitol Hill tensions only intensified.
Senator Charles B. Grassley, Republican of Iowa, unveiled a proposal that would accelerate all the income tax rate reductions in the tax-cut bill Mr. Bush signed earlier this year, give companies a tax break for investing in new equipment, repeal the corporate alternative minimum tax and provide one-time cash payments to people who did not earn enough money to qualify for this summer's tax rebates.
Mr. Grassley said the tax cuts in his package would total $89 billion this year, well above the $60 billion to $75 billion Mr. Bush has proposed.
Democrats immediately criticized Mr. Grassley's proposal as no better than a Republican plan the House narrowly passed last week. Democrats said any bill they bring up in the Senate would have to address the needs of unemployed workers by expanding the unemployment insurance program and offering laid-off workers substantial help in paying health-insurance premiums -- provisions Republicans have resisted.
"We will not even consider a bill unless it has those components," said Senator Tom Daschle of South Dakota, the Democratic leader.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: Market Place; 
Where Donald Trump sees trouble in Atlantic City, bondholders see an effort to cut a deal.

BYLINE:  By Riva D. Atlas 

 BODY:
WITH the outlook gloomy for his Atlantic City casinos, Donald Trump said yesterday that he might not make about $90 million in interest payments due next month on his bonds.
 He has enough cash, he said, but may try to negotiate with bondholders to reduce the amount owed because his casinos are loaded with debt and confronting increased competition in a weak economy. 
 At least some bondholders contacted yesterday said they did not take Mr. Trump's threats seriously. In fact, they are wondering whether Mr. Trump could be trying to take advantage of a decline in bond prices to cut a favorable deal for himself. 
The prospects for Mr. Trump's properties, and all Atlantic City casinos, grew dimmer last week, after New York passed legislation to allow gambling in the state, a decision partly intended to increase revenue as the economy softens.
Mr. Trump warned that New York casinos could divert business from Atlantic City and have a negative effect on his three casinos: the Trump Marina, the Trump Plaza and the Trump Taj Mahal.
"The world has changed greatly since Sept. 11," Mr. Trump said in an interview yesterday. "At some point in the near future I am going to go talk with bondholders." 
Mr. Trump has more than $1.6 billion in publicly traded junk bonds, most of which are secured by his Atlantic City casinos. He controls these properties through a public entity called Trump Hotels and Casino Resorts, whose shares trade at $1.25. Mr. Trump owns roughly 42 percent of this company.
Mr. Trump was already facing additional competition in Atlantic City from a casino called the Borgata, scheduled to open in 2003. The property is a joint venture of MGM Mirage and Boyd Gaming. 
Mr. Trump owes about $73 million in interest payments tomorrow on $1.3 billion in bonds issued by an entity called Trump Atlantic City, secured by the Taj Mahal and Plaza casinos, and about $15 million more in payments due on $242 million in bonds issued by Trump Castle Associates, which are backed by the Trump Marina casino. These bonds mature in November 2003.
Mr. Trump said he had not yet decided whether to pay bondholders, even though he emphasized his ability to do so. "The question is do I want to wait until 2003," he said, "or deal with this now?" 
The price of the Atlantic City and Castle bonds have fallen sharply since early September, according to John Kempf, a high-yield bond analyst at Goldman, Sachs.
The Trump Atlantic City bonds have fallen to about 60 percent of their face value from about 70 percent, while the Castle bonds trade at 74 cents on the dollar from the mid-80's.
Mr. Trump's New York City real estate interests are not publicly held and are not affiliated with the casino entities. The property portfolio is in relatively good shape, Mr. Trump said. "I've sold out or rented out everything," he said. He is proceeding with The Chicago Sun-Times on a project announced in July to build an office tower in Chicago. 
According to reports at the time, Mr. Trump intended for the Chicago tower to be the world's tallest building. Mr. Trump denied making that claim. Still, he said, it would "be a somewhat different project had Sept. 11 not taken place." 
Some investors think Mr. Trump would be wise not to pay the money he owes bondholders next month, and instead spend the money fixing up his properties before new casinos open in New York and Atlantic City. "He'd be stupid to pay this next coupon," said one investor who recently bought some of Trump's bonds. "It would be in his best interest not to."
Still, by failing to pay interest on his debt, Mr. Trump runs the risk of losing control of his properties. Often, in a bankruptcy process, stockholders receive nothing unless the debt is paid off in full.
But when his three casinos went bankrupt in the early 1990's, Mr. Trump managed to maintain a controlling interest in the properties because he convinced bondholders that the properties would be worth less without his involvement. Also, if owners of Trump's bonds tried to take control of his casinos, they might have difficulty obtaining a license to operate the properties.
Still, one investor who also owned Trump bonds a decade ago, said he would try for a tougher bargain with Mr. Trump this time around. "I don't think we'll let him pull his pockets inside out like he did in the early 1990's," the investor said. "Once bitten, twice shy." 
When told of the comment, Mr. Trump threatened to drag out the negotiations with bondholders, if need be. "The bondholders don't want to go through a five-year restructuring and then end up with nothing," he said.
Some investors say Mr. Trump may not want to take the chance of losing control. "I would be shocked if he can make that payment but doesn't," said one investment banker who has worked with Mr. Trump. "He is a brinksman; 2003 for him is a lifetime away."
Some investors and analysts suggest Mr. Trump could merely be trying to drive down the price of his bonds. Mr. Trump quietly spent $46 million last spring buying bonds issued by Trump Hotels and Casino Resorts, said Mr. Kempf of Goldman, Sachs.
"I like my properties, and I have a lot of cash," Mr. Trump said in response. "But do I want to invest in Atlantic City right now?"

GRAPHIC: Photo: Donald Trump said his three Atlantic City casinos were loaded with debt and confronting increased competition in a weak economy. (Ozier Muhammad/The New York Times)
